CLEVELAND STREET SYDNEY.
LITERARY NOTES.
Louis Becke's New Book,
“ The Ebbing of the Tide,” Louis Becke’s
new book, collects those of his stories in re-
gard to which The Bulletin has already ex-
pressed a chastened surprise at the honorable
nonduct of the British publisher. Many of
these stories were originally paid contribu-
tions to The Bulletin ; others first appeared
m the North Queensland Register (Char-
ters Towers) and the Sunday Times (Sydney).
Literary and artistic contributions to The
Bulletin are covered by general copyright;
but this journal does not claim copyright
against its contributors, and, in ceding to
Mr. Becke the right of republication of his
Bulletin stories, the only stipulation made
was a verbal stipulation that in any reprint
tiie fact of first publication in The Bulletin
should be acknowledged—in accordance with
ordinary courtesy and ordinary usage. In
Becke’s first book, “By Reef and Palm,”
tiiis stipulation was duly complied with ; in
the case of his second book, several of the
stories were re-sold to Mr. Clement Shorter,
of the Illustrated London News, and ap-
peared as new and original in the publica-
tions of the I.L. News proprietary—the
Album, the Sketch, the English Illustrated
Magazine —and elsewhere. To secure a
sale, the fact of previous publication was
apparently suppressed : Mr. Shorter tells
The Bulletin that had he known the facts
he would not have published the stories. And
now, in defiance of courtesy, usage, and
right, the tales are reprinted in volume form
without a word to tell of previous publica-
tion, either in Australia or in England.
* * * *
For these proceedings Mr. Becke disowns
responsibility ; and The Bulletin cheer-
fully exonerates him. It would be unjust
to saddle Mr. Becke with the little eccen-
tricities of Fisher Unwin, his antipodal pub-
lisher—of whom, by the way, he had the
other day to complain that the name of Walter
Jeffery, his collaborator in another new
story, was suppressed on publication in the
Illustrated London News, apparently be-
cause Becke was known and Jeffery un-
known, and it was thought that Jeffery’s
name might interfere with the sale of their
■joint work. All that this paper cares to do
under the circumstances is to recite the facts
as another tribute to the versatile genius of
John Bull, whose sense of honor, wdiere
cash is concerned, is as keen as a sixpenny
razor.
* * « *
Criticism of Becke’s new book begins with
Lts title, which, one gathers, is owed also to
his publisher. Considering that Stevenson
attempted to exploit the field of South Sea
romance ; that “ The Ebb Tide ” is the title
of a well-known book to which his name is
attached ; and that Becke is first and fore-
most a South Sea romancer; “The Ebb-
ing of the Tide, by Louis Becke,”
seems obviously intended to suggest, and
does suggest, that Becke is a sort
of “colonial Stevenson,” a parasite of
the Samoan “ Tusitala ” whom the British
mind has been educated to admire, a timid
disciple who asks for British appreciation
from the safe shelter of “ the Master’s ”
cloak. The suggestion is false in idea and
fact. Stevenson was a wonderful craftsman,
but with all his art he could not present a
man or woman in five pages as Becke does
in five sentences. His Adams are marvellous
combinations of dust, his rarer Eves would
make any rib proud to mother them, but he
could not breathe the breath of life into
either. Becke is, of all things, alive, vivid,
vital. His characters leap at you from the
printed page. No one dreams of comparing
his total achievement with Stevenson’s, but
tn Becke’s special sphere Stevenson is a
weakling by comparison : all his art fails to
reach the eloquence of Becke’s simple touch
®f nature.
* * * •
In Becke’s new book his graphic talent has
again full scope. It says much for him that
“ The Ebbing of the Tide ” is, on the whole,
quite as good as “By Reef and Palm.”
There are more weak spots ; the note of
monotony begins to be heard ; but the best
things are memorable. “ Luliban of the
Pool,” for example, not alone from its in-
trinsic interest, but even more from its wide
human suggestion, ranks as high as—per-
haps higher than—anything Becke has
attempted. The king, the woman, the men
who fight for her, the priests who help to
dupe a savage populace—these are im-
memorial figures in the human drama ; and
the scene they enact has been repeated a
thousand times in human history. “At a
Kava-Drinking ” and “ Kennedy the Boat-
Steerer ” are as good—histories of real men
and women, loving warmly, hating keenly,
and placed before us still palpitating with
the emotions that strained their natures to
the highest pitch of intensity. The vigor
and truth of the narrative can hardly be
praised too highly. The South Sea epic of
Drown women and white men will never find
a better interpreter than Louis Becke.
* * * *
A careful re-perusal of “Byßeef and Palm”
and “The Ebbing of the Tide ” strengthens
the conviction that Becke has very many of
the qualities which make a really great
writer. He needs only a little more art, a
fittle more restraint, a little more sense of
proportion; and all these things come with
practice and striving. r At'present, Becke has
more dramatic instinct'than dramatic talent.
His mind is so full that he is embarrassed
with crowding details which he cannot re-
duce to their proper subordination. In story
.after story his main conception is obscured
by the mass of surrounding incidents placed
in the same light, on the same plane. Take
Deschard of Oneaka, ” in the new volume.
Here is the germ of a most powerful tale, its
motive the best in Becke’s repertory. On a
South Sea atoll lives Harry Deschard, an
escaped convict and leader of the natives.
One morning a schooner anchors near the
islet. He plots to cut her out, murder the
crew, and take possession of the vessel. The
plot succeeds admirably. The captain and
three passengers (of whose presence the
assailants are ignorant) take refuge in the
cabin. Deschard turns one of the ship’s six-
pounders against the door, and blows them
out of existence. Then, going through the
ship s papers, he comes across an agreement
by the captain to convey Henry Deschard,
Anna Deschard, and their children to a safe
port. The mangled bodies in the cabin are
those of his own wife and daughters, who
have sought him out with infinite hopes and
fears, to give him a chance of regeneration.
And Deschard shoots himself.
* * * *
Here is a theme almost HUschylean in
scope and grandeur : by odds the noblest
Becke has ever dealt with. He has plainly
a vision of its potency, yet he handles it
lamely, almost tediously, with not a tithe of
his own capacity. Page after page is filled
with weak or irrelevant details ; the story is
told half as fact and half as fiction ; the
reader s attention is perpetually diverted
from the main issues; and the final im-
pression left on his mind is compara-
tively vague and confused. With a little
remodelling and a good deal of pruning the
value and force of the tale could have been
doubled.
* * . * *
The point involved is the point raised by a
recent Bulletin contributor who suggested
many things which he did not satisfactorily
settle : the need of scenery in a short story.
Now, a little consideration will make it
evident that the only justification of scenery
is as an aid to the story. The Story is the
thing to consider. Whatever helps it, pre-
pares the way for it, deepens the impression
which it makes upon the reader, is an advan-
tage : anything more is a disadvantage. Thus
the introduction—if there is an intro-
duction must be always germane to
the story, and must never overweight
it. The shorter the story, the shorter
should be the introduction, as a matter
of course. A composer precedes an opera
with an overture, but he never dreams
of overturing each concerted number in the
opera : at most, he gives a preparatory
bar or two. So, in The Bulletin, where
considerations of space limit most story-
tellers to about two columns, it would be
obviously inadvisable to spend a column, or
even half-a-column, in an introduction, how-
ever intrinsically brilliant, which did not
help the story. or the climax. To take
another illustration from another art : the
effect of a statue is conceivably increased,
and its beauty is better seen, when it is
placed on a pedestal. But the pedestal must
always be in proportion to the statue, and
suited to the situation. A statue in the open
air, in a public place, might be advantage-
ously raised twenty or thirty feet from
the ground : in a gallery, a height of
five or ten feet would be sufficient.
But Becke occasionally, as in “At a Kava-
Drinking,” prefaces an eleven-page yarn
with a five-page introduction which does not
aid, but rather weakens the force of his
story, while he could have crystallised into
twenty lines a more vivid, definite picture of
the circumstances which brought about its
telling.
Louis Becke’s merits are above depreciation:
his demerits are a small thing by comparison,
and he has plenty of time to remedy them—
if he will take it. He has safely skirted the
familiar second-book gulf. His fertility
shows no signs of exhaustion, and his success
has stimulated many other local writers. To
adapt a big phrase to our little sphere, he
might be called in prose, as “The Banjo”
in verse, the leader of an Australian literary
Renascence.
Reprints.
A BEVY OF BALLADES.
Ballade of the Southern Cross.
Fair islands of the silver fleece,
Hoards of unsunned, uncounted gold,
Whose havens are the haunts of Peace,
Whose boys are in our quarrel bold ;
Our bolt is shot, our tale is told,
Our ship of state in storms may toss,
But ye are young if we are old,
Ye Islands of the Southern Cross !
Aye, we must dwindle and decrease,
Such fates the ruthless years unfold;
And yet we shall not wholly cease,
We shall not parish unconsoled ;
Nay, still shall Freedom keep her hold
Within the sea’s inviolate fosse,
And boast her sons of English mould,
Ye Islands of the Southern Cross !
All empires tumble—Rome and Greece—
Their swords are rust, their altars cold !
For us, the Children of the Seas,
Who ruled where’er the waves have rolled,
For us, in Fortune’s books enserolled,
I read no runes of hopeless loss ;
Nor—while ye last—our knell is tolled,
Ye Islands of the Southern Cross 1
Envoy.
Britannia, when thy hearth’s a-cold,
When o’er thy grave has grown the moss,
Still Rule Australia shall be trolled
In Islands of the Southern Cross !
ANDREW-IAN.
The Ballad' of Imitation.
“ C'egt imiter quelqu'un que de planter deg choux."
—Alfred de Musset.
If they hint, O Musician, the piece that you
played
Is nought but a copy of Chopin or Spohr ;
That the ballad you sing is but merely “conveyed ”
From the stock of the Arnes and the Purcells of
yore;
That there’s nothing, in short, in the words or
the score,
That is not as worn-cut as the “ Wandering
Jew ”;
Make answer—Beethoven could scarcely do
more—
That the man who plants cabbages imitates, too !
If they tell you, Sir Artist, your light and your
shade
Are simply “ adapted ” from other men’s lore ;
That—plainly to speak of a “ spade ” as a
“ spade”—
You’ve “stolen” your grouping from three or
from four ;
That (however the writer the truth may deplore),
’Twas Gainsborough painted your “Little Boy
Blue ”;
Smile only serenely—though cut to the core —
For the man who plants cabbages imitates, too !
And you, too, my Poet, be never dismayed
If they whisper your Epic—“ Sir Eperond’Or ”
Is nothing but Tennyson thinly arrayed
In a tissue that’s taken from Morris’s store;
That no one, in fact, but a child could ignore
That you “lift” or “ accommodate ” all that you
do;
Take heart—though your Pegasus’ withers be
sore—
For the man who plants cabbages imitates, too !
Postscriptum.— And you, whom we all so adore,
Dear Critics, whose verdicts are always so new!—
One word in your ear. There were Critics before . .
And the man who plants cabbages imitates, too 1
AUSTIN DOBSON.
Ballade of Primitive Man.
He lived in a cave by the seas,
He lived upon oysters and foes,
But his list of forbidden degrees
An extensive morality shows ;
Geological evidence goes
To prove he had never a pan,
But he shaved with a shell whpn he chose,
'Twas the manner of Primitive Man !
He worshipped the rain and the breeze,
He worshipped the river that flows,
And the Dawn, and the Moon, and the tree*
And bogies, and serpents, and crows;
He buried his dead with their toes
Tucked up—an original plan—
Till their knees came right under their nose
’Twas the manner of Primitive Man 1
His communal wives, at his ease,
He would curb with occasional blows;
Or his State had a queen, like the bees
(As another philosopher trows):
When he spoke it was never in prose,
But he sang in a strain that would scan,
For (to doubt it, perchance, were morose)
'Twas the manner of Primitive Man !
Envoy.
Max, proudly your Aryans pose,
But their rigs they undoubtedly ran,
For, as every Darwinian knows,
’Twas the manner of Primitive Man !
ANDREW LANG.
A Ballade of Old Sweethearts.
Who is it that weeps for the last year’s flowers
When the wood is aflame with the fires of spring
And we hear tier voice in the iilao bowers *
As she croons the runes of the blossoming?
For the same old blooms do the new years” bring;
But not to our lives do the years come so,
New lips must kiss and new bosoms cling.—
Ah ! lost are the loves of the long ago.
Ah me ! for a breath of those morning hours
When Alice and I went awandering
Through the shining fields, and it still was ours
To kiss and to feel we were shuddermg—
Ah me ! when a kiss was a holy thing.—
How sweet were a smile from Maud, and oh !
With Phyllis once more to be whispering.
Ah ! lost are the loves of the long ago.
But it cannot be that old Time devours
Such loves as was Annie’s and mine we sing,
And surely beneficent heavenly powers
Save Muriel’s beauty from perishing;
And if in some golden evening
To a quaint old garden I chance to go,
Shall Marion no more by the wicket sing?—
Ah ! lost are the loves of the long ago.
In these lives of ours do the new years bring
Old loves as old flowers again to blow ?
Or do new lips kiss and new bosoms cling?—
Ah ! lost are the loves of the long ago.
R. LE GALLIENNE.

BEFORE YOU
T. E A Ft
When you go out shopping, consider, think
■of the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
(reorge-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“GET OUT—I’M' WRITING HOME.”
A voice in the tent to the voice outside
Growled, “ Go to the devil, do !
m °®. y° ur tents ! for I’m not at home
To-night to the likes of you.”
We are coming in ! ” “ You’d better not,
Or out you’ll shift again ;
Spirits of mischief, get off to the deuce !
To-night I’m a knight of the pen.
“ There’s a boot for one and a boot for two,
You know I’m a splendid shot;
There’s a towel three, and a pillow four,
Keep out or I’ll pelt the lot.”
’Twas away out West where the gold is found,
Or where it’s supposed to be ;
Where you live on hope when you can’t get bread,
And you drink—well—dream-made tea.
Defiant of threats, a couple of heads
Slid in at the canvas door ;
They dodged the pillow, the towel and boots,
And their owners took the floor.
They took the floor (good reason why,
Out West we don’t keep chairs),
And four long limbs, Australian-bred,
Sprawled out in royal pairs.
And their owners smoked their pipes, content,
And said they’d come to stay;
They’d been mates, you see, for many a day,
These three at work or play.
“ We have just dropped in for a smoke, old boy,
Only a smoke and a chat,
With a game of euchre or whist or nap
What are you grumbling at ? ”
“ Get off the grass ! Clear out, now ! Shift ! ”
Came the answer unpolite ;
“ I’m writing—move off ! —l’m writing to home,
I’ll play you to-morrow night.
“ I must write to-night or I’ll miss the mail,
So shift your lumbering frames !
They’ll expect this letter at home from me,
Get out ! don’t jump their claims !
“They know to the day when the post will come
And eager they’ll look for it;
It wouldn’t be fair if I missed it now,
So you two fellows may quit.
“ A small young brother will stand at the gate
To watch for the postman’s coat,
And a tiny sister will wait at his side
For the letter that Charlie wrote.
“ ‘ I tan see his toat, I tan see his toat,
I hear the youngsters cry,
And off down the street go four little feet
As fast as wee legs can fly.
“ Then back in triumph they’ll carry their prize,
Their bright eyes flashing glad,
And their eager shout—l can hear it now :
‘ A letter from Tarlie, Dad.’
“ And the old man tries to look unconcerned,
But, deep down under his vest,
He’s as anxious as they to know what says
The lad in the wild far West.
“ Then there’s my mother —well, here, you fellows,
1 reckon you’d better roam,
Shift your long lumbering limbs out of here !
Get out ! I’m writing' to home.”
CRAGS.
BUCKED OFF ITS BRAND.
Take my word ! he could buck, could Brown Baron;
And to ride ! Who cotdd ride like long Jac k ?
There was never a thing born with hair on
Could throw him when once on its back.
Tn the crush went on saddle and bridle,
And he set Jack a go pretty hard ;
But his previous efforts seemed idle
When we flung down the rails of the yard.
A few bucks, and the gear was all lying—
Busted girths, broken bit —on the sand ;
And away through the trees he went flying—
Nothing on him but Jack and the brand.
Through the paddock the Baron went sailing ;
Jack was keeping him straight with his hat
When we saw him jump over the railing
At the creek on the Kurrajong Flat.
And then—where on earth were they hidden ?
Though the boss swore he’d soon have ’em back,
And rode as he never had ridden,
The traps had to start on their track.
But Jack was not beaten by trifles,
And, .when he and the Baron were found,
It took four police, ditto rifles,
Ere the long-’un set foot on the ground.
When we came to examine the Baron,
All the brand-mark had disappeared clean :
’Twas the horse, we could swear--a great scar on
The place where the Z 9 had been.
Jack explained, in the dock at his trial,
That the horse slung his brand on the track ;
To the charge gave indignant denial -
Said, when shot he was just coming back. _
Jack sits now on stones for a saddle,
And for reins has a hammer in hand
’Cause an ignorant Judge chose to twaddle
That a horse couldn’t buck off its brand.

MINISTERIAL TRir TO THE
NORTH.
Mr. Reid and Mr. Young began their
northern tour yesterday. On arriving
at Tenterfleld, they were welcomed by the
Mayor and the citizens. The Mayor, Mr. J.
H. Reid, entertained the party at luncheon
at his fine residence overlooking the town.
As he is a total abstainer, no liquor was
given banquet, * ' *»»».*..
On Thursday they will proceed by water tq
Woodburn, the Broadwater sugar mill, and
the mouth of the river, ending up with ban-
quet No. 4 at Ballina, where the harbor
works are to be inspected. On Friday there
will be a visit to the Rous sugar mill, with
banquet No. 6at Lismore f-n Saturday the
party will go to Byron Bay, then take the
tarin to Brunswick and Tho Tweed, where
No. 6 banquet will take place, .'.ft.v tnls
the party are to travel down the river to
catch the train for Brisbane. There are to
be various deputations on the way,-the most
important being a conference with sugar-
growers and others interested in the indus-
try, to be held at Lismore. This deputation
will submit proposals for assistance to tbc
sugargrowers in lieu of the duties ofi
imported sugar.

SATURDAY, APRIL 11, 1890.
Tombs and Other Matters.
The periodical outbreak of typhoid, which
arrives in Sydney and Melbourne once or
twice a year, turned up recently, as usual,
and excited a certain languid interest amongst
those not attacked. Typhoid in one or two
Australian cities —principally in loathsome
and undrained Perth —-is always more or less
in evidence, and the other cities are visited
by it at longer or shorter intervals. When
typhoid is not present diphtheria or some
other dirt-disease generally takes its place,
and when none of these epidemics is specially
noticeable, the community is generally
harassed more or less about the steady,
continuous increase of cancer, tuberculosis,
and other unnatural agencies which cut off
mankind before its time. Science has now
practically decided that man, if not killed by
accident or illegal violence or judicial execu-
tion, should only die of old age, and that
every cause of death save old age is merely
the outcome of ignorance or neglect. All
the innumerable diseases which used to be
piously summed up as the “act of God” are
now recognised as the results of dirt, over-
crowding, want of light, bad drainage,
diseased meat, decayed and leprous vege-
tables, impure water, and unnatural modes
of living generally. As sanitation pro-
gresses disease diminishes and the average
duration of life increases—in England, it
is now something over 40 years, whereas five
or six centuries ago it seems to have been
only about 14 years ; and it appears quite
probable that a time will yet arrive when
every man may live, if he pleases, to the age
of 100 years, and become a long and dreadful
weariness to himself and all his acquaint-
ances by so doing.
* * * *
Despite the overturn of all the old ideas
of sanitation, however, one of the unclean
habits of onr-unelean. forefathers is still pre-
served as a holy rite by the mass of the
population. The disposal of the dead goes
on in practically the same primitive fashion
as it did fifteen centuries ago ; and how much
sudden death and slow murder by maudlin
sentiment it has caused, and is still causing,
no one will ever know. In specially infec-
tious cases, the room which the corpse has
occupied is carefully disinfected to remove
the germs of disease, and the clothing he has
worn is burned, because fire is the only agent
which will destroy the bacteria with absolute
certainty, and thus cut off all possibility of
further infection. Then the mortal remains
are carefully deposited in the rich, damp
soil, that the disease germs may grow and
multiply exceedingly among the corruption
and the worms and the poisonous ex-
halations ; and the official ghoul comes
along periodically and digs up the tainted
earth to make room for more coffins,
and thereby scatters the infection upon
the four winds. Also the rain-waters
percolate among the dead, and go away laden
with the elements of typhoid, cancer, tuber-
culosis, diphtheria, leprosy, and other com-
plaints, to enrich the pastures and thereby
cultivate the diseased cow 7 that supplies the
community with tainted meat and milk. Or
they travel by long and devious ways into
wells and reservoirs, and leave a faint,
sickly-flavor of the dead in the water which
is consumed by the living, and infinitesimal
particles of the deceased ancestor are found
there and are briefly described by the analyst
as “ decomposed organic matter. ” Then, in
the course of years, the cemetery is filled up
with death and decomposition and is aban-
doned, and in the course of more years it
loses its distinctive character and is used
possibly for building ground or for pasture,
and a new generation lives on top of the
unwholesome mass, with possible conse-
quences which need not be further enlarged
upon.
* * * *
When the casual ,/ or bullock dies of
some especially infectious disease the law, in
civilised countries, orders the destruction of
its carcase by fire, to prevent the spread of
ufecbion. When a human being dies of the
..line complaint or one equally dangerous,
he law allows the remains to be buried
under conditions which guarantee that their
m pacify for spreading infection w r ill be pre-
served as long as possible, and the general
public sentiment, for some unexplained
reason, seems to regard compulsory crema-
tion as an outrage, and clings lovingly to
“the grave” as part of its creed and its
civilisation. Public sentiment is advancing,
certainly, but in this particular direction it
advances very slowly. The ordinary process
of burial is a method by which the deceased
is gradually transposed into a shapeless,
putrid object —a gassy thing without form or
outline, for the midnight worm to associate
with and for every other living creature to
avoid ; yet somehow 7 this awful relic has
been surrounded by a halo of poetry, and
the great mass of the community is reluctant
to hart w T ith it. Cremation, on the other
hand, is a clean and wholesome process, and
is as little shocking to the sense of any
logical being as any process for the disposal
of the dead well can be. Moreover, it does
away with one of the worst horrors of the
old process one concerning which the
lugubrious romancist has spread himself for
many years —the possibility of premature
burial. Whether burial before death is
common or not, no -one really knows—except
the people w r ho are prematurely buried, and
they offer no explanation on the point. But
soqie authorities certainly profess to believe
that 'it is a common thing to mistake mere
insensibility for death. Others have ad-
vanced the opinion that at least one form of
alleged death, that by frost, is not extinction
as is commonly supposed, but merely sus-
pension of life for an indefinite period, or
rather indefinite preservation of life ; and
that the individual who was frozen in a land
of perpetual ice tqn centuries ago, and who
has remained froze ever since, is still alive,
and will remain so unless killed by injudicious
thawing, and that consequently his survival
underground may be next to eternal. And still
others have advanced the idea—which no one
is or seems likely to be in a position either
to prove or to controvert—that so-called
death is merely a species of permanent
paralysis of the body accompanied by reten-
tion of consciousness for an undefined period,
and that consequently everybody , so far as
consciousness goes, is buried alive. Assum-
ing that there is anything in any of these
theories—and no one can absolutely prove
that there is not—cremation, which means
almost instantaneous destruction by intense
heat, certainly looks far more humane than
the long horror and blackness and utter
silence underground, with the dead weight
of the globe overhead, and nothing beneath
and all around but blackness and more globe
and perpetual silence.
* * * *
Apart from all hygienic and other con-
siderations, the abolition of the present
method would have a good effect upon the
mental condition of those who are still alive.
It would be a long time before the lugubrious
bard could learn to fit his melody to the new
conditions ; many years of rhyming “ tomb ”
with “gloom,” and maundering about the
grass that grows above the grave, and the
cypress tree, and the sepulchre, and the clods
that fall on the coffin and such-like properties,
have forced him into a groove, and, until a
new generation of bards arose, this depres-
sing variety of verse would be at a
serious discount. Cremation would also
do away—at least, The Bulletin hopes
so—with the weepful cemetery, and drive
that morbid class - of people who go on
Sundays and holidays to spell out the in-
scriptions on the tombstones, and to show
their children the broken urns and the
epitaphs, and to sit beneath the dark-green
trees whose roots are down among the multi-
tudinous deceased, and to maunder over
“ dust ” and similar subjects, into some more
healthful form of recreation. It. wouM do
away this paper also trusts, with the dismal
hearse and the plumes, and the long row o,
empty carriages behind, and the hansom cab
with a fat man asleep in it at the rear of the
procession; and the card-parties
mourning coaches ; and it might e\ en a -
the undertaker as we know hnn—that woe-
ful-looking red-nosed person with the paid-
for depression on his countenance who chives
slowly and sadly to the cemetery, and drives
back from it at a joyful trotmmgled with beer.
It ought to put an end, also, to the dismal,
soft-footed person who comes to measure
the body,” and-to the old female with the
gin-breath who “lays it out.” In all pio-
bability it would badly discourage the m
memoriam ” verses in the Age, which have
long been in want of active discouragement ,
and it should put a summary closure upon
the aspiring person who writes verses con-
cerning the hackneyed “bush burial. In
short, it would revolutionise the most dreadful
department of modern life and the most
wailsome branch of modern literature, and
anything that seriously damages the tomb
variety of literature deserves the support of
every healthy-minded person.
* * * *
The one serious reason advanced against
cremation is that it at once and for ever
destroys all possibility of gaining information
as to the cause of death. In the case of
murder by poison it happens, about once in
a million instances, that some information,
often of no particular value, is gained by dis-
interring somebody deceased; in most in-
stances, owing to the person in question
being altogether too much deceased, no
information worth speaking about is pro-
curable. Under a system of compulsory
cremation, however, it is quite possible that
the law would require a [jjs!-mortem exami-
nation in every case of death, simply because
there would be no possibility of investi-
gation later on. In that case, there
would be a decided advantage attached to
the new system. As things are now,
the post-mortem is an extraordinary ex-
pedient, and even to ask for such a
thing implies grave suspicion and creates a
serious scandal. Many experts connected
with life-assurance business are of opinion
that secret murder by poison is much more
common than is supposed, but it is only pos-
sible to demand an investigation where rea-
sonably strong evidence can be adduced.
Therefore, a large number of suspicious cases
pass without investigation, covered by the
certificate of some doctor who is either in-
experienced, or incapable, or venal, or who
gives the document to cover up his own
mistakes ; and the matter is allowed to
drop, simply because an insurance office
which habitually creates trouble thereby
falls into disfavor. The amount of know-
ledge conserved by the present method
is too minute to be worth considering as an
argument in its favor. And when all the
things in its disfavor are considered—the
contamination of water-supplies, the careful
preservation of the germs of deadly disease,
the lamentable poetry about the “silent
grave ” and the alleged flower which grows
thereon, the lying epitaphs and the mentally
diseased people who go to read them, the
ancient grave-digger about whom so many
maunderings have been perpetrated, the
gruesome hearse and the black, bony steeds
which draw it along, the fatal colds acquired
by so many mourners while standing by
while the cold blast whistles round the coffin
and the officiating clergyman, the alleged
ghost which gibbers on the headstone, the
worms and the corpse-lights and the poison-
ous exhalations, the badly-drained cemetery,
where the late-lamented is found in two feet
of greenish water when his resting-place is
opened for the accommodation of another
deceased, and all the rest of the dank and
bony melancholy—when all these things are
taken into account any system which is clean
and sudden and wholesome and effective is
a change for the better.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A converted Foreign-trader.
A leading New South Wales Foreign-trader
named Goodlet has announced his conversion
to Protection under the influence of the new
tariff. Mr. Goodlet is connected with cement
and the new system struck his industry all of a
heap. Cement can be made more cheaply at
present in Europe than it can in New South
Wales, partly because wages are lower and
partly because the works, having a larger popu-
lation to support them than exists in this
country, are mostly on a much larger scale, and
working on a large scale means the more ex-
tended use of machinery and many other
economies. The difference in distance doesn’t
compensate for the disadvantages under which
the local makers labor ; it costs 6d. a cask to
send cement by rail from Granville to
Sydney, while cement is brought from Eng-
land to Sydney as ballast at Is. a cask, and tlTe
odd 6d. doesn’t nearly make up for the other
outlays. This is the same trouble which
the .wav-back farmer encounters iu competing
with sea-borne imports. There are no rails to
lay on the ocean, no stations, sidings, embank
meuts, bridges and tunnels to make, no land to
be resumed for the permanent way ; the ocean
doesn’t require constant repairs ; there are no
signalmen, lengthsmen, fettlers and gatekeepers
to pay out in the middle of the deep ; the large
ocean tramp, in proportion to its cost, carries
far more than the locomotive and requires much
fewer men to work it for each 1000 tons
of capacity. The wages of the seaman are
lower than those of the railway employe. j n
the case of the huge, fast sailing-vessels which
now dot the ocean there is no skilled labor iu
the shape of engineers to demand high wages,
no coals, no oil, no expensive engines to repair,
and the outlay is.kept down to an almost irre-
ducible minimum. Moreover, there is a large
trade in second-hand ships, and in many cases
the present owner of the vessel has bought it
from some bankrupt person in a glutted market
for half or a-third of its value, while the rail-
way department can’t buy second-hand tunnels
and permanent way and stations below cost;
the railway has to make interest on the
full value of its properties, while the owner
of a cargo-ship often has to make interest on
half or one-third the value only. The cargo-
ship, too, needn’t be kept in very careful
repair ; it can be put under some cheap and
disreputable foreign flag and sent out at last to
sink for the insurance money, while the railway
can’t. Also the ship can’t move without bal-
last, and when cargo is scarce it is glad to take
heavy goods, such as cement, for stiffening pur-
poses for next to nothing, to save the final
necessity of buying ballast and carrying it
absolutely gratis. Therefore, there is nothing
extraordinary in the fact that certain goods
can be carried, under certain circumstances,
6000 miles by sea about as cheaply as they can
50 or 100 miles by land, and the fact doesn’t
prove that the railway rates are excessive, or
should be reduced —it only proves that the con-
ditions are wholly unequal. Consequently,
there is nothing surprising in the fact that the
N.S.W. cement industry is going to wreck.
The one thing which is surprising is that
Mr. Goodlet, as a brainy man of business
didn’t see all this in time to prevent him howl-
ing for the great Foreign-trade cause, as he
apparently did up to a very few months ago.
The Maoriland Election.
If the Seddon Government gets another three
years’ lease of office as the result of the 1896
elections, it will be about the most remarkable
—if not the most remarkable—Ministry which
ever existed in Australasia. It has already
lasted through two Parliaments, and has had
nearly six continuous years of power, and its
majority remains practically unimpaired. It
was created in 1890, when John Ballanck was
still alive, and Richard of Kumara was his
first lieutenant, and it started with a large ma-
jority. It went to the country in 1893, and
came back, contrary to all precedent, with a
still larger majority. The ordinary course of
things is that a Government begins with a large
following, which steadily diminishes through
its three years of office until the next general
election, when it is decisively rejected. Then
the other party comes in with a big majority,
which fades away in like manner, till another
election comes along and it is also cast out, and
the previous men come back to go through the
same process all over again. Under the party-
system the people and the Parliament can’t
amend the Ministry’s bills if they think them
unsatisfactory, or undo any of its acts, or reject
part of its policy and endorse the remainder, or
reject one member of the Ministry ami keep the
remainder; all it can do is to accept all the
Ministry and all its policy, or else throw it out
bodily and entrust the same irresponsible power
to someone else. And, as hardly any Ministry
is fit to be trusted with such power, the country
generally throws out every Ministry at the first
opportunity, with the vague idea that the other
lot can’t be worse than the present lot—quite
regardless of the fact that the other lot had
previously been thrown out on exactly the same
grounds. There have been hardly more than
three occasions in Australasian history when a
Ministry, after serving the full term of one
Parliament, has returned with a majority, and
it is questionable if any Ministry ever came
back a second time with such a majority as the
Seddon Government gained in ’93. All through
its career it has had to encounter the bitter
hostility of five-sixths of the city press and the
great majority of the press of the entire coun-
try. It has declared war against abuses which '
no previous Ministry dared to attack, and has
thereby incurred the bitter hatred of almost
every “ vested interest ” in the country. It
has taken risks which almost all the alleged
financiers of Australasia declared to be ruinous,
and has come out right in almost every case.
It has been a Labor and semi-Socialistic Go-
vernment, and has proved that a Labor Govern-
ment can be as peaceful and much more intelli-
gent than the ordinary variety ; and it has sent
printers and boiler-makers into the Upper
House, where they have revealed much more
capacity than the ordinary Fat Mttn nominee.
It has been much more honest than the average.
It hasn’t bought support with any lavish dis-
tribution of borrowed money—the means by
which Vogel and Gillies obtained their second
terms of office. And, despite the fact that it has
most of the press, most of the capitalistic in-
terest, the whole grog influence, and the Na-
tional Ass to contend with, the present signs
are that it will get another big majority and go
on to complete nine consecutive years of office.
If it does, it will show that the policy of trust
ing the people pays best in the long run.
A Bogus Surprise.
A grand display of newspaper-surprise was
submitted to the public of Smellbourne and Vic-
toria when the Lormer Enquiry Board found
magistrates Rapiport, Baxter, and Bird guilty
ot scandalously corrupt dealings. The astonish-
ment, although it made no claim to freshness,
was not supposed to date back further than the
lev elations ” made before the Board. Nothing
unfavorable to the characters of Rapiport and
axter had reached the offices of the
Kespectable —or “Great”—journals during
ie years previous to the exposure of these
calamitous law-givers, which came as a great
s ioc - to the community, said the various
right-minded news-sheets. In the face of all

I he Raeiport-Baxtf.r combination was a

and, seeing that all daily newspapers are in-

whisperings, it goes without saying that many
of the revelations elicited by the Lormer
Enquiry Board might have been picked up in

True, there was a probability of the missiles

newspapers when Lormer proved his case
was altogether contemptible and charac-

who, in the cant acceptation of the words,
“ knew anything at all ” was ignorant of
the Rapiport -citra- Baxter business. Also,
nobody can help being quite well aware
that Smellbourne has supported at least one
professional jury-nobbier since it lost its con-
science in the hurly-burly of the boom. Un-
fortunately, a board of enquiry about him is
not likely to sit; but should, perchance, the
name of this notorious individual ever get
proclaimed aloud, he will occasion another
bogus ebullition of “surprise.”
Melbourne University—Yet Again.
Dear Bulletin, — Isn’t it about time that ill-
informed scribes gave up slinging ink at Melb.
University? “Demos” (March 21) asserts
several erratic things about the University, the
Affiliated Colleges, and the Masonic Lodge.
(1) “This impartial, State-supported institu-
tion (vide J.S. R.) shelters a whole brood of
theological colleges and a Masonic Lodge.”
(a) The University is not State-.s upported. It
receives State-oi(Z annually, but so do munici-
palities, shires, water-trusts, hospitals, lib-
raries, museums, art-societies, schools of mines
—yea, even cemeteries. But none of these
admit the world free—except, perhaps, the
cemeteries. ( b ) The Affiliated Colleges—
Trinity, Ormond, Queen’s —are not Univer-
sity-owned colleges. They are not Univer-
sity-governed colleges. They do net con-
tribute a cent to the University chest. The
University does not contribute a cent to their
coffers, (c). Nor are they theolorjiccd colleges.
Connected with each religious sect (or church)
is a theological hall to train its budding clerics.
But these halls are wholly self-governed, and
unrecognised by the University in any way.
These halls give theological instruction in the
college buildings—Anglican, Presbyterian, and
Wesleyan halls, that is—but they are no more
connected with the University than are, say,
the Congregational, Baptist, and R.C. halls
which give instruction miles away. The halls
do not, and never did, own or govern
Trinity, Ormond, or Queen’s, any more than
the University does or did. Again, the
three Affiliated Colleges are open to all-
comers without religious tests, and in them
there are no compulsory religious observances,
lu my time at Ormond (Presbyterian hall col-
lege) there were resident adherents of half-a-
dozen Protestant churches ; also, Unitarians,
Jews, Roman Catholics, and Agnostics, and a
present census would doubtless reveal the same.
These colleges are no more theolorjiccd colleges
than the British and Gennafi Universities are
theological Universities theological
instruction is therein given. They are simply
teaching institutions, with “ Divinity ” on their
programme for those who care for the subject.
(d) “ The colleges are built on the University
Reserve.” They are not. Away back in the
“ fifties ” a large block of land north of Mel-
bourne city was reserved by the then
Government for educational purposes. By
later Governments portions of this land
have from time to time been granted to
various bodies, viz., University, Public Library
Trustees, Anglican, Presbyterian, Wesleyan,
and R.C. Churches, Education and Defence
Departments, and some is still ordinary Crown
lands' reserves. Of the whole parcel of laud
which “ Demos ” seeks to style the “ University
Reserve,” only about one-third belongs to the
University, and it never held more than it does
now. (2) “ The Masonic Lodge spreads itself
in the official calendar.” It does not. I he
Masonic Lodge is simply looked on as an asso-
ciation of University men for a specific purpose,
just as the cricket-club is, or the Philosophical
Society. In the one solitary year (1893) in
which the lodge was mentioned in the calendar,
it received the same mention as the 11 other
societies then existent in the University —social,
educative, and athletic —so that it no mote
“ spread itself ” than, say, did the Princess Ida
(women’s) Club. If “ Demos ” knew more about
the University, he would probably talk less.
Yours truly, J.S.R. [This discussion ends
right here. If it were left to itself it would
evidently last out the century, and The Bulle-
tin is tired. —Ed. B.]
Sydney’s Meat Supply
Sydney is the second largest of Australian cities,
with a population of about 400,000 ; and practi-
cally the whole of the meat it consumes is killed
at abattoirs in the centre of one of its most
thickly-populated suburbs. A system more
dangerous to public health and life could hard y
be devised. Bullocks, wild and tame are
driven along the public roads by hundreds at
night-time and occasionally in the dav-time,
making residence or passage along the line of
route unsafe for- thousands of people. Acci-
dents occur frequently, and sometimes an in-
furiated animal has to be pursued and shot with
rifles in a main thoroughfare. The blood and
refuse from the abattoirs partly fester into the
harbor, and the water of Johnstone s Bay is so
polluted that no fish but sharks will enter it. In
addition, the killing of so many animals and the
exposure of so much offal creates a stink which
the wind carries for miles; and, lately, scores
of residents in the neighborhood have been
struck down by typhoid fever attributed
directly to infection from the abattoirs. Such
a combination of nuisances in any modern city
is a monstrous thing ; but there are many men
employed in connection with the abattoirs,
and houses and hotels in the neighborhood
are let in spite of its accumulation of
horrors. Hence a strong vested landlords’
interest absolutely opposes any change.
“ Let air and water be polluted,” they
say; “fill the streets with frantic cattle;
let the public health go hang ; but for Heaven’s
sake don’t drive away our tenants.” And so
well have they managed that Sydney in 1896
is as far behind in the matter of meat-supply
as it was 20 years ago. The Bulletin is glad
to see that Premier Reid shows a wobbling in-
clination to end the nuisance. Abattoirs in the
midst of a large population are intolerable.
There should be killing-centres adjacent to the
railway lines a substantial distance from Syd-
ney, whither stock could be easily conveyed ;
and the whole of the meat for the city’s con-
sumption should be slaughtered at these centres
under the close supervision of Govt, experts,
and chilled and forwarded by special trains to
the retailers. No other course can so well meet
the public convenience, or secure the public
health.

Sydn ky Telegraph in a recent issue thus calmly
consigns N.S. Wales’s sugar-industry—which
supports about 1800 workers and their families
—to oblivion :
We have never attempted 'to disguise the fact that the
future of the cane-growers was seriously jeopardised by
the removal of the duties. It might possibly stand a
reduction of the duties by one-half, but their entire
removal will in the end result in the colony drawing its
supplies of cane sugar from more tropical localities. The
Premier, when the removal of the duties was decided
upon, dealt with the sugar duties exceptionally, because
the position was exceptional, and although a substantial
rise in the world’s sugar markets would alter the outlook
considerably, it would not, in the long run, prevent the
inevitable decadence.
The smash won’t be immediate because sugar-
cane lias to be planted two years before a crop
is taken off, and the cane that was put in the
ground in ’94 and ’95, when the duty was sup-
posed to be fixed, will yield a return in ’96 and
’97 and ’9B, despite fiscal changes. But if the
farmers see no prospect of a reversion to the old
tariff conditions, then there is no inducement
to plant new crops, and the industry will start
violently downhill about ’99 and become practi-
cally extinct a year or two thereafter.
Re the release of Staples, the awful Melbourne
bank swindler, whom the Judge sentenced to
the longest term the law allowed of, stating, as
he did so, that he was sorry he couldn’t make
it more, and whom the Solicitor-General re-
leased so unobtrusively that even his colleagues
in the Ministry didn’t hear of it till some
months afterwards, Melbourne Punch alleges
that dozens of influential prisoners are similarly
sneaked out of gaol every year. In Staples’s
case the matter would never have become
public if the lamented banker had le f t Mel-
bourne quietly and hadn’t gone obtrusively filing
his schedule ; and supposing the other prisoners
leave quietly and do not make a row
with their schedules no one is likely to hear
anything about them. Therefore no one really
knows who is in prison and who isn’t, or
whether the influential person ever serves his
sentence at all. If Parliament demanded a
complete list of the persons whose sentences
were commuted, say within the last ten years,
the result would probably make it hand over
the whole business to a Court of Appeal, which
would sit in public, and openly give its reasons
for the release of any prisoner before his time,
and to which the influential friends of the man
who has gone wrong would have to make their
application in the garish light of day.
A cornstalk person, signing himself “A. A.
Baker,” writes in the usual cornstalk frame of
mind to S.M. Herald about how to cure the
“ depression ” :
Now, it is my conviction that the solution of this-pro-
blem is to be" found, not in the fluctuating, reversible
policy of either Freetrade or Protection, such as we have
had hitherto, but in a definite, settled, fixed policy (to be
determined by the electors themselves by a plebiscite
vote) for a period of not less than 10 years. With this
question settled for that period the attention of capitalists
and investors and others (not only in this, but in other
lands') would be attracted to New South Wales as a place
affording encouragement and security for the investment
of their capital (which they have not hitherto had), and
which would undoubtedly result in the establishment of
those permanent industries and manufactories in our
midst which our natural resources demand should be
located here.
The truly awful thing about the cornstalk is
that his * first and last and only idea' about
removing the depression is to “attract foreign
capital into the country ” and to “ promote the
investment of English capital,” or some other
such wheeze. And his only idea of N.>S. \V.
manufactures is to have big factories put up
with English money and bossed by English-
men, and have the profits go to England, while
the common Australian does the hard grind at
30s. or 365. a week. If Australia is ever going
to be a nation it wants to begin by making it an
offence, punishable by death, to write or talk
or drivel any longer the old. platitudes about
“ attracting foreign capital ” and “ promoting
the investment of foreign capital in opening up
the resources of the country,” or “ encouraging
a steady influx of foreign capital,” or any other
of the stale catchwords that this country has
lived upon so long. There is already about six
times more foreign capital invested in Australia
in proportion to population than in any other
country on earth, and the result is so awful
that any man who wants more foreign capital,
and consequently more debt and more interest
to pay and more grind to pay it, needs prompt
execution.
If the Lormer Board’s strictures on Lormer
for adjudicating on licensing cases as a teeto-
taller are correct, then the Victorian Chief
Justice stands condemned for adjudicating in
divorce-cases while confessedly entertaining a
strong prejudice against the Shiels Divorce
Act. On similar grounds, the agnostic Judges
are ineligible for blasphemous-libel prosecutions.
Judge Hodges, as an anti-gambler, acts impro-
perly when he sits on a lottery, totalisator, stock-
exchange, or even insurance case ; and a Judge
who is over-friendly with a girl or two should
refrain from hearing maintenance and affiliation
cases. Judge Holroyd, as a believer in Imperial
Federation, should retire from any consti-
tutional case, or possibly from any case in
which Separationist Purves appears as counsel;
while any Judge who has a strong prejudice
against law-breaking and who is determined to
put it down as far as possible, should certainly
refrain from sitting in judgment on any law-
breaker, civil or criminal. For the honest
belief that one can act justly in spite of one’s
“list” does not relieve one from the charge of
“acting improperly.”
The Lormer Board report—terse, logical, and
conclusive as it is—threatens to ensure the con-
tinuance of the “ honorary ” magistracy in
country districts on the present basis. It went
out of its way to suggest doubts as to the Ben-
digo and Ballarat beaks, but rather eulogised
than otherwise the rest. This leaves many
sores untouched—the squatter-employer J.P.,
the dictator-storekeeper J.P., the Little Bethel
J.P., the mine-owner J.P., the boodling irriga-
tion-trust J.P., the Orange lodge J.P., and the
boss-cookie nuisance. As to the substitution of
a board for appointing justices for the present
system, it is almost certain that the Chief Jus-
tice would be a member, and it is very ques-
tionable whether the principle of social
flunkeyism would prove a more desirable
method of testing fitness than the present prin-
ciple of political flunkeyism. An extension of
the stipendiary system and the election plan
are the only really arguable proposals.
Fat Man Lewis, National Ass member for
Christchurch (M.L.), a few weeks ago :
One of the worst of the many had results of the Govern-
ment policy has been to attract to our shores the poor and
destitute of Australia.
Australian Star, National Ass organ in
Sydney, the other day :
In 1893 New Zealand’s excess of immigration over emi-
gration was 10,412. In 1894 Mr. Seddon’s policy was be-
ginning to tell its tale ; the immigration figures dropped
to 2253. The year 1895 saw the complete bursting of the
bubble, the immigration figures fell to 895 —0 r to 9517 less
than in 1893 ! Such figures would have opened the eyes
of any less blinded by passion, class-hatred, stubbornness,
and lust for office than Mr. Seddon and his colleagues. . .
Well-wishers of that colony will sincerely hope for the
downfall of one of the very worst Governments ever
known in an Australasian colony.
The Ass thus argues equally well either way.
When Lewis was orating, the immigration
figures for ’95 were not available; but it was
generally supposed that people were still stream-
ing in, and their coming showed that the
Seddon Government was very bad indeed.
When it transpired that immigrants hadn’t
been coming in so very largely, after all, that
fact showed that the Seddon Govt, was even
more wicked for not attracting them than it
had been for attracting them. And it was the
same doleful Ass each time.

Sydney Telegraph, in its leading article of
March 20, sits heavily on the advance by the
State of seed-wheat to distressed farmers and
on the minimum-wage upon Government con-
tracts, on the ground that they are equally
opposed to the great “ terewths ” of Freetrade.
“ The especial desire of the people,” it says,
“ as communicated to the Government at the
ballot-boxes, was that class-legislation should
cease and class favors be abolished.” Yet our
contemporary hasn’t yet said one word about
the “class favor” by which the Telegraph is
carried free through the post, at the expense of
the taxpayer, all over N.S. Wales. “If the
farmer is not as capable of getting along by
himself as the butcher, baker, or blacksmith,
the consequences should be upon his own head,”
says the D.T. The same remark applies to the
newspaper. If it can’t compete with the
foreign paper unless a 50 per cent, duty is
placed on the latter in the shape of postage, it
ought to go bung. The distribution of seed-
wheat doesn’t cost the State half as much as
free newspaper-postage does ; moreover, it is
only a loan, and the money mostly comes back
again, while the free postage is a gift ; and the
distressed farmer only asks assistance once a
year at most, while the rich and prosperous
Telegraph is a protected and subsidised pauper
every day of its life.
The amount paid by Sydney clothing-factories
for “ slop-made ” trouseis ranges from lOd, to
as low as sd. per pair, “soaped, felled and
pressed, complete,” the work to be done at
home. Few establishments pay the maximum
price now, the general average being with
a tendency to get still lower down. Out of this
munificent sum the work-girl has to provide
her own needles, thread, &c., and the necessary
firing for the heating of the pressing-irons, be-
sides paying for machine-hire, repairs, oil and
incidentals. This, at a fair calculation, reduces
the 6£d.—-the average price—to about 6d., for
the making and delivery of each pair of
pants. And, even at this figure, the
worker can’t got anything like constant em-
ployment ! Further, when the wretched slave
has succeeded in securing a big order, she can’t
“ slum ” the work to make up for the enforced
idleness of yesterday, as the employer takes
each article in his fat-creased paws and, before
passing it for payment, gives it a great tug,
and, should a stitch start or crack, the white
slave has to trudge back garret-wards and there
unpick and do the work over again, at her own
cost, with loss of more thread, firing and time—
and loss, indeed, of everything save, alas ! an
unfailing belief in the alleged beneficial results
of Freetrade. And it is to compete with these,
and, consequently, still more reduce their
wages, that an English religious paper, the
British Weekly, urges the better-paid, or at
least equally-poorly-paid, sewing girl to come
along in boatloads to the Promised Land of
Botany Bay.

If a vestige of the N.S. \V. Custom House is to
remain, Protectionists and Freetraders should
make an opportunity in next Parliament for
amending the current Customs Act. That
portion of the Act which, instead of compel-
ling the criminal prosecution of a man guilty of
defrauding the Customs, expressly provides
that he may smother the charge by paying an
increased duty, should be hissed off the Statute
Book by a bulky majority. The fact that such
a provision exists in the Act and was unknown
until lately by prominent members of the
Executive, proves with what cunning that piece
of legislation was engineered through Parlia-
ment in the good old days which preceded pay-
ment of members.
The Australasian (the weekly echo of the
Argus) still harps on the story that the Vic-
torian Companies Bill was thrown out, not
because the 48 Rich Landlords wanted to pre-
serve the old, profitable abuses, but for want of
time. “ With regard to the Companies Bill,”
it wails, “ does Mr. Deakin think the last day
of the session the proper time to send up a
measure making important alterations in the
law?” Of course not. But, then, the Companies
Bill was sent to the Council more than a week
before that date. The Tory organ, of course,
doesn’t actually allege that the Bill wasn’t sent
up till the last day of the session ; it. merely
asks w'ildly if a certain date, upon which a
thing didn’t happen, was the sort of date when
a thing like that should have happened. And
The Bulletin, with a view to being quite as
much to the point, asks if the last day of the
session was a proper time for the editor of the
Australasian to go out with a black eye and
fish for the sea serpent in the Yarra with a
crooked pin and a worm? Which doesn’t imply
that he did anything of the kind ; it merely
raises the question whether, supposing a thing
that didn’t occur had occurred on a certain
occasion, that would have been a good occasion
for it to occur upon ? And that is just what
the Australasian is asking.
——-
Last Saturday’s Q. elections are a horse of
another color—the color the Govt, has been
cunningly trying to keep dark. Of 22 seats the
Labor-party took 12—gaining three on its pre-
vious holding. The strength of parties is now
estimated—Govt., 38; Labor, 19; Opposition,
6 ; Independent, 3. There are still six consti-
tuencies to poll. At Toowoomba, Oppositionists
Groom and Fogarty got in by a large majority,
beating both Govt, and Labor men. At
Cairns, ' the Labor candidate was beaten
by the alleged nominee of Burns, Philf
and Co. Charters Towers re - elected its
old Labor members, and Ministerialist Sayers
was again decisively beaten. Kidston, a
moderate Democrat, siding with Labor, headed
the poll at Rockhampton ; and Drake gave
Mayor Thurlow a drubbing at Enoggera.
Attorney-General Byrnes took his toga right
off, and .. after suggest ing retirement from
politics, and. regretting that he had been
nominated without his consent at Warwick,
and trumpeting his stern refusal to aceept a
seat at the expense of another Ministerialist,
permitted a Ministerialist to retire in his favor
and went in easily.'
A person calling himself “ Tenax ’" whoops in
the columns of the Age. Said whoop is worth
notice as an example of the common state of
the Victorian intelligence :
I wish, through your columns, to ask the democrats of
this country how much longer they propose to suffer the
action of the Legislative Council. That body has filled
up the cup of its iniquities by eliminating from the Fac-
tories Bill almost every proposition which would make
the measure a real protection to the workers. Yet there
is no method of overcoming such obstruction save by the
Government taking the sense of the country. No better
issue could be chosen. An enormous majority of voters
will decide against the Upper House and in favor of im-
prisonment for sweating on the third conviction, of regu-
lation of the hours of labor, and of strict limitation and
close inspection of out-door work. Then there is, &c., &c.
The Vic. elector returns Sargood and Zeal and
the other snags to the Upper House, yq£ir after
year, generally without even a contest ; a
hundred Assembly elections can’t influence that
body so long as the country won’t interfere
with it at Council elections ; and yet the great
aggregate elector who has re-elected Sargood
persistently for 22 years wants to know why
the Ministry doesn’t “do something” to shift
him. What is the good of asking the Govern-
ment to “ take the sense of the country ” when
the sense of the country—or of the great
bulk of it —is that Sargood is to be
in power for 22 years ? Sargood is the
sense of the country, so far as it has been ex-
press, ed.

of relinquishing her St. Kilda practice.

Bland Holt opened his season at Sydney
Royal with a very promising military drama
named, apropos of nothing at all, “ One of the
Best. ’ I’he military element concerns an alleged
Highland regiment, which gives somebody an ex-
cuse to come on and blow a fearsome blast on the
haggis or the usquebaugh or the philabeg or some
other instrument that looks like a goat’s-hair
football with several nozzles to it. There is no
war in the drama ; not a single gun goes off; and
the enemy doesn’t get routed even once. Also,
there is hardly a single allusion to the glorious
Flag. The trouble turns entirely on the fact that
Baker is convicted of taking important military
secrets out of the safe at night and selling them to
the enemy, when it is really Cosgrove who has
entered the gate secretly with a baleful sneer
and done it. Therefore, Baker is sentenced to
penal servitude for life and is stripped of his
decorations in the presence of 400 troops, the
process being carried out so effectually that he re-
tires to his dungeon cell with very little on save
his petticoat. Then things begin to move sud-
denly, Cosgrove being unmasked and rushed off
the stage for good inside 3u minutes, and the show
closes with Baker reinstated and waving a flag.
The drama goes so smoothly and is adorned with
such a quantity of gorgeous Highlander that the
extreme suddenness and want of finish in the last
act are less noticeable than they might be.
* * * *
Bland and Mrs. Holt are the comic element
of the show, said comedy consisting largely of
ladies’ stockings, and complaints about Bland’s
petticoat and allusions to infants. Some kind
remarks are called for about Mrs. Holt’s appear-
ance in bicycle costume. That lady makes an
exceedingly nice boy, and her knickerbockers are
a poem. Also, she is a good deal of a poem all
round, as she always is. Baker, as the hero, is
just about perfect, and even the awful kilts can’t
wreck his fine impersonation of a very fine char-
acter. Concerning the new star, Miss Elizabeth
Watson, sister of Henrietta with the swan neck,
The Bulletin suspends its judgment and regret-
fully hangs the lady up for further consideration.
She is strong in spots and weak in spots, and
vaguely unsatisfactory in spots ; but, possibly,
many of these spots will disappear later on.
As for Miss Frances Ross, this paper implores
her to put the awful skirt she wears in
act 111. into the ash-barrel, and the remainder of
the dress in the coal-hole, and to shove the hat up
the chimney as far as it will go, and stick to her
other two graceful costumes. No beauty could
stand the awfulness of the frock in act 111. As
regards the contents of the frock, Miss Ross is g,
graceful actress, who is good in her tender
moments and weak in her tragic ones, but the
writer has an impression so far that she is capable
of better things—provided she keeps the aforesaid
costume well up the chimney. Also, he desires to
say something really good about Charles Brown,
Corlesse, and Harford, and to record his conviction
that Miss Flora Anstead is one of the best old
women—old in a stagey sense—going.
** * *
The Brough-Boucicault co. started its fare-
well season at Sydney Cri. amid much enthusiasm
and a very large quantity of vegetables. “ The
Pickpocket ” was put up for two nights only, and
a large audience noted with interest certain
developments in that pleasant comedy since its
last appearance in Sydney. Boucicault has made
his waiter older and more tottersome in the legs
than he used to be, and he scoots across the stage
with less than his old velocity. What'he has lost
in this way, however, he has gained in the comic
rheumatism of his left leg, and the hilarious
flunkey is as hilarious as ever. Ward lurks on
the right side of the stage now while watching his
suspected wife, whereas he used to lurk on
the left. Brough takes Anson’s old part
as the grumpy invalid, and The Bulle-
tin hasn’t quite made up its mind which
grumpy person it likes best. Titheradge is the
same to a hair as he was before, while somehow
Miss Hilda Spong falls comparatively flat as the
g sh ul bride. Taking it in all its aspects, the
“Pi ntpQcket” is almost, though not quite, as
brignt as it used to be. By the time these remarks
appear in print, however, Hawtrey’s comedy will
be gone, and “Rebellious Susan” and “Dandy
Dick ” will also have made a. transient appearance
and passed away again, and “Mrs. Tanqueray”
Will be just due. “The Importance of Being
Earnest,” by Oscar Wilde, who is just now busy
showing the importance of being in gaol, will be
staged on Saturday, and this paper hopes to dis-
course extensively thereon next week.
#* * *
The Full Court, or the partially-empty
court, or some other authority, having injuncted
the production of “ Called to Arms ” at Her
Majesty’s, and used very considerable language
about the management, that production had to be
abandoned at the last moment, and the three big
guns moulder uselessly in the vestibule. “ Hands
Across the Sea” was shunted on the stage in great
haste to fill the gap. “ Hands ” hasn’t improved
since The Bulletin had the pleasure of making
many nasty remarks about it in bygone times, nor
has it got any worse. It still embodies the usual
tired feeling about the “ Old Flag,” and the lead-
ing character is in the same feverish state of whoop
about how the banner of his country will protect
him from the beastly foreign aggressor. When
the foreign aggressor gets too beastly the banner
is hoisted and the aggressor sees it and recognises
that the game is up and departs with his tail in
the dust. “Hands,” taking it all round, is
a very ordinary melodrama, and the author’s
attempt to hang on at the end an irrelevant
moral to the effect that this country should
be deeply attached to the hempire, and that Hing-
land is older than other places, and that the Union
Jack isn't so new as history says it is, and that
the Hinglishman’s blood is more ancient than the
average, is fortunately drowned by the rush of the
audience for its tram. There is no public enthu-
siasm about “Hands” just at present. The
scenery is remarkably good, considering the red-
hot haste with which the piece was put up. Miss
Lilia Wilde’s heroine is a very nice and creditable
girl in a pretty frock, and the rest of the co. is as
successful as could be expected under the circum-
stances.
** * *
“Djin-Djin” has consistently filled the
Lyceum to overflowing all the week. The stranger
who was keeping holiday in Sydney has gaped
with an exceeding great gape at the illuminated
city of Nagasaki, and the frozen forest, and the
earthquake, and the eruption, and the golden city,
and all the rest of the blaze and blare and frescoed
howl, and pronounced it good. All the week the
Lyceum has rung with the whack of his horny
palms, and the old man who has gone on the land
went away at last with a stunned expression to
the gum-trees and the salt-bush, and said “ My
oath ’’ frequently. For a holiday spectacle “ Djin
Djiu ” fills the bill to perfection. The person from
the plains wants something that is as little like
the sluggish cow and the taciturn calf as possible,
and the three hours of irresponsible riot and glory
supplied at the Lyceum probably satisfy the empty
places in his soul better than anything else could.
He doesn’t really crave for dramatic art; what he
is after is surprise and paint and yell and illumi-
nation. and they are all there.
Miss EDITH CRANE, as Trilly, at Melb. Princess's Theatre.
Photo, by Chickering, Boston.
Harry Rickards and Alice Leamar reap-
peared at Sydney Tivoli, and that house has been
running a constant series of matinees to satisfy
the public demand for more Rickards and more
Alice than can be crowded into an ordinary
evening performance. The Opera House remains
distinctly alive, and a new minstrel co. broke out
at the Music Hall. Also, small two-night shows,
too numerous to mention, raged in all parts of
the city to absorb the overflow from the big
theatres, and The Bulletin gave up all hope of
trying to overtake the rush.

The touch of grovel perceptible in Bouci-
cault’s benefit-night speech was much more marked
on the closing night of the Melb. season, when he
had to return thanks for a cheque. Clever Dion B.
is Irish, and centuries of oppression have produced
grovel in the Irish blood. Paddy is as ready to
fawn as to fight, and Lord ! how he delights to
butter one of his shamrock brethren. Witness
Chief Justice Madden’s eulogium of the unique
stage-manager,” and vice versa.
Westmacott’s Co. began to appear at Melb.
Royal on Saturday, and made a favorable im-
pression upon a crowded house. 1 The Enemy s
Camp,” late of Sydney, has already been referred
to by this journal as a deserving melodramatic
howl in many acts. It contains a larger amount
of unsophisticated plot to the square foot than any
two ordinary screams,- and is so full of stirring
incidents, and expensive scenery, and black-
hearted crime that no ordinary intellect can take
it all in at one sitting. Also, “The Enemy’s
Camp ” is sometimes a military spectacle, trimmed
with comic songs and a saucy ballet, in addition

** * *
Gabbett, and the raw flesh of the Moocher,
were withdrawn from Melb. Alexandra last Week,
owing to clever Dora Mostyn’s departure oh a
provincial Easter tour. The Haleck Company,
minus Dora aforesaid, is just now strugglirtgnian-
fully with H. J. Byron’s old play, “ The Lan-
cashire Lass,” which, in its time, was a good play,
and is still a respectable sample of stage literature!
If they could remove a couple of acts, and fill up
the gap with convicts and cannibalism, it would
be more appreciated by Dan. Barry's constituency,
but even under present conditions “ The Lan-
cashire Lass” is well received. For one thing;
the heroine suffers from a lot of unjust suspicion
and misdirected abuse. Nothing seems to please
a sixpenny gallery much better than an
innocent female, staggering under a load of sorrow.
But Augustus Glover, as a Party by the name of
Johnson, is the actual first favorite. Augustus
puts vigor and humor into his wickedness, and
carries a yard of dirty red handkerchief protruding
from a greasy trousers pocket, and turns up like a
bad penny to embitter the existence of a bloated
capitalist who is afraid to chuck him off the
premises because he has “ proofs ” of the b.c.’s
early indiscretion concealed about his person.
The idea of this gruesome object making the con-
science-stricken millionaire quake in his ponderous
frock-coat, and bri lging beads of perspiration to
the rich man’s brow, is so eminently satisfactory,
especially at popular prices, that “ The Lancashire
Lass ” will certainly flourish for a week or more.
#* * *
The coming of sundry new people from
Sydney, likewise the first production of Charles
Godfrey’s sketch, “ The Night Alarm,” caused a
great rush to Melb. Opera House on Saturday.
Taken in the bulk, the new arrivals proved to be
brighter—anyhow, they were fresher —than those
who made way for them. Godfrey’s sketch, or
“ dramatic scene,” or whatever it calls itself, was
also a success in its last moments, when the fire-
bed rang, and the lion-hearted comedian drove
away on a real engine to save his family from
being burnt alive in the next scene. A couple of
maudlin dirges leading up to the Conflagration
were very tiresome, however. The sin get had
had a bad day at the races, it appeared, and alow-
spirited punter warbling low-spirited doggerel
verses at a funereal pace is about as painful as the
income-tax.
** * *
The stage version of “ Trilby ” introduced
to Australia at Melb. Princess’s on Easter Mon-
day will satisfy people who Compare it with the
book, whilst capturing the vote and interest of the
uninitiated. It is a poetic melodrama built around
the weird figure of Svengali, the musician and
hypnotist. Pale-blue limelight gleams upon the
countenance of that fatal hybrid Hebrew, and the
creature is a strange reality. Also, he dies in a
convincing manner, failing backward across a gilt
table with an awful white face staring at the
audience to remind them that all flesh is grass.
The Svengali of the story is intensified on the
stage ; his influence over Trilby .e^n-be felt", and
the sensation of it surviveiThls decease in the third
act. The performance of Reuben Fox in this
chief character is a striking success, far beyond
general expectation.
* * * *
Miss Edith Crane makes a fine figure of a
Trilby, and does all things gracefully ; but her
brief love-scenes are marred by a tame, mechani-
cal, unprepossessing little Billee whose blighted
career seems a just reward for Ins unnatural acting.
The Laird is likewise open to improvement. Five
or six other imported artists fit their parts well,
the exponents of Geeke, Madam Vinard, and Zou
Zou being particularly right up to the mark.
Veteran H. R. Harwood upholds the credit of
local talent in the character of Rev. Thomas Bagot,
a shepherd who apologises for wantinsr to save
Trilby from the wrath to come when, he learn;
that the Prince of Wales has been enqfiiriug alter
her health.
« * * *
As a matter of course, the play is smartly
mounted and managed; incidental music and
dancing give valuable assistance, and some by-play
expressing the devotion of the three English
painters to their young girl makes a pretty studio
picture. A “ Trilby ” matinee is announced for
Saturday.
** * *
The Comic Opera Co. opened in Adelaide
on Easter Saturday with “ The Yeomen of the
Guard.”
The programme of the Reid Goverment
for next session includes a large scheme of water
conservation, the construction of light railways,
a Referendum Bill, a measure to regulate alien
labor, the amalgamation of the savings banks
(whereby G. R. Dibbs may lose his present com-
fortable billet), law-reform, a Coal Mines Bill, and
a few smaller measures. Nothing definite has
been said, so far, about reform of the Council or
Local Government, and the latter, at all events,
seems hopeless for the present. Local Govern-
ment in N.S.W. has been stranded for 30 years
n ?"j , an< l Parliament has almost lost the habit
of thinking about it.
