The Farmers’ Mutual Loan and Building
Society, whose prospectus appears in Melbourne, is an
affair which, if honestly and intelligently worked, should
fill a large want. It is to some extent an extension of the
Starr-Bowkett principle. There are to be issued 5000
shares of £6O each payable by instalments of ss. per share
per month, so that the capital should ultimately reach
£-300,000. This money is to be lent out to members on
the security of farm and orchard properties, creameries,
&c. Apparently, the funds as accumulate are
to be divided in two equal parts. A ballot will be
held periodically, and the winner gets his loan abso-
lutely free from interest and re-payable at the rate of
£6 per £lOO per annum. If a member ?an’t wait for his
chance of winning a ballot he may gef.a loan out of the
other half of the funds at 4£ per cent, per annum, also re-
payable by instalments, so that a payment of £7 per
annum for each £lOO horrowed ( will extinguish principal
and interest in 23 years. As the average lender charges
at least 7 per cent, for interest alone, this is a great con-
cession.
The unaccountable features of the arrangement
are that half the total repayments for the first four years
are to be invested in Vic. State securities, which will
somewhat diminish the lending fund, and that all the net
profits are to be similarly invested at the end of each
year. Therefore, so far as appears from the published
p ospectus, the fund available for loans will never get any
bigger, while the reserve invested in Government securi-
ties will grow steadily larger and larger, until, in course of
time, it should be a larger concern than the loan-fund.
The society is, apparently, intended to be a permanent
affair, and if it goes on for ever, and all the profits
are put away in State securities, there can’t be
any dividends, and in that case the shareholder who
doesn’t want to borrow at all, or who has repaid his
loan and doesn’t want another one, or who has sold his
property and has nothing to borrow on, will get nothing
out of it. Then, presumably, he must sell his shares to
someone who wants to borrow, for a document entitling a
man to a share in a prosperous fund which keeps accumu-
lating, and is never divided, and so can’t be got at, is
not saleable for mere purposes of investment. Should
the society prosper, a time may come when it will still
have its £300,000, or rather less, to invest in loans, and
£1,000,000 put away in State securities, and the reserve
will keep accumulating while the loan-fund remains the
same, and, though each share represents £6O of loan-fund
and £2OO of reserve, it will still only be worth £6O, for the
reserve seems to tie put away so securely that even the in-
terest on it can’t be lent out. This looks like needlessly
reducing the advantage to members —unless the society’s
advt. doesn’t fully explain things.
The Bulletin suggests the following as an
amended policy for such a society : (1) Capital, £3,000,000
in 60,000 £5O shares, payable at ss. a month, or as much
faster as the shareholder pleases ; (2) Each holder to have
as many shares as he likes, but nothing over ten shares to
carry any voting privileges; (3) Loans never to exceed 60
per cent, of the value of property ; (4) Loans to share-
holders on country lands to have preference ; loans to
shareholders on city lands to rank next; funds not in
demand by shareholders to be lent to non-shareholders on
country lands, or if such securities are not available, on
city property; (5) Interest 4i per cent. ttFshareholders;
6 per cent, to non-shareholders; all loans repayable by
annual instalments, with the option of paying in
full at any time; (6) Annual State audit to be pro-
vided for by special Act of Parliament; (7) Half the net
profits to go in dividends, and the other half to a reserve-
fund invested in Government securities ; (8) No director
to be a borrower, except on the certificate of the State
auditor that his security is up to the fixed standard.
* * * *
“Insurer”: This paper knows nothing tangible
to the discredit of your office or any leading assurance
office of this country. But what it does know is that the
private-audit system has fallen into considerable < is-
credit, and that until there is some effectual State super-
vision there will be a vague suspicion in the minds of
many people about good and bad institutions alike.
State audit ought to be agitated for by all the leading life
societies of Australasia, simply because it provides an in-
dependent guarantee and one which the public can under-
stand. The fact that the auditors are elective officers
doesn’t guarantee their efficiency—as witness many ex-
ploded banks and other institutions. Therefore, even an
office which wants the n o t rigorous inspection can’t
get an auditor’s certificate which will keep it above sus-
picion, simply because the private auditor is in bad odour
at present, and justly so. •
The Australian Mutual Investment and Build-
in"- Co. (Svdne.v) is a beautiful relic of the dead boom.
The liabilities ‘at 31st December, ’95, are set down at
£315,249, and the assets have a book-value of £116,565.
Last’ year’s losses were, on paper, about £lOO,OOO,
but they were merely the vanishment of certain paper
values which had long ceased to have any real exist-
ence. Most of these consisted of Sydney city properties with
mortgages upon them, which properties had depreciated
so much that the co. either handed them back free, or
paid the mortgagees to take them away and say no more
about them or about the £90,000 or so which the co. paid
for them in the mad old days. The entire receipts last
year from £116,000 worth of alleged property appear to
have been £958, equal to about seventeen shillings per
cent, per annum all round, which was barely suffi-
cient to cover office expenses. One of the assets,
by the way, reads, “Probable loss, £958 3s. 2d.,” which
is a quaint affair. Five vears ago this concern issued a
balance-sheet setting forth that it had a reserve-fund of
£46 000 and £555,000 worth of assets: it had £353,,000 of
deposits at that time, and it paid 25 per shent. dividends
to its shareholders. The last 25 per shent div. was de-
clared in June, ’9l, and on that date the old board made
its verv last whoop about the glorious progress of the co.,
and verv shorllv after it took in its last deposit and un-
obtrusively closed its door. Nobody seems to have
inquired about it very hard since then.
The Federal Coffee Palace, that tall, gaunt
buildin"- which looks down out of the clouds on the
hitn"TV idesolation of Melbourne, reports from its pinnacle
thatT thfhgs are reallv getting better. Last half-years
income was £BO5O, and the expenditure £8307, so that the
loss was only £‘237. For the previous ha f the loss was
£702 and the one before it was £748 , theiefore, it is
believed that the great white elephant is now looking up
and may yet be a paying concern again. +
The Bank of England five years ago adopted a
scheme for making small investments in State securities,
which device would bear transplanting to Australia. Any
one can deposit a sum of money with the bank fixed for a
certain number of years. These sums are invested in
consols: the interest as it conies in is re-mvested : and so
the money goes on accumulating without any possible
risk to tlie investor and without any trouble whatever,
phe charges are Id. in the £ commission for collecting
and reinvesting the dividends—say, lid. a year on a 1400
investment —and 3d. for a letter every half-year reporting
the state of the balance. A scheme like this should do
we “ in Australia, when there is a State Bank to work it.

Loader had voluntarily retired from the Board
of the Mutual Life Assurance Co. (Vic.) before Tiib Bul-
letin commented, in a tone of horror, upon that dis-
credited financier’s candidature for re-election. The fact
remains that newspaper “ feelers ” re Loader’s chance of
hanging on to his billet were thrown out subsequent to
the City of Melbourne Bank revelations. He didn’t retire
untfithe voioes j n rh e street had made remarks which
left him no hope of be n r able to slip back to the Board
unobserved. As a di ector of the Mutual Life Co. he
showed himself a good business man, and was esteemed
by his colleagues, it is said ; nevertheless, the mere pro-
posal to maintain the name of Loader in bold type upon
the balance-sheets was a melancholy instance of official
opinion re Smellbourne’s indifference to scandal. In any
other civilised circle the utter “ impossibility ” of Loader
in such a connection would have been recognised by him-
self and his best friends long before he finished his C. of M.
Bank story of the awful things he couldn’t remember.
The ideal song for busted bank directors:
“ ’Twere folly to remember, 'tis wiser to forget.”
“Employers free” is becoming the familiar rule
at Melb. labor offices.
• ** *
Much jumping of valuable property has of
late taken place in and around Sydney. One wealthy
citizen has just caused notices to lie served on a
host of his bewildered tenants asking them if they
have any title to the properties for which they
have, for years, been paying rent to bis agent.
This possibly as a preliminary to establishing a
“possessory title,” on a 20-years’ possession, which the
landlord has just completed. Of course, having once
paid rent to his agent, the tenants have acknowledged
the circulariser as their landlord, a fundamental principle
of law between landlord and tenant being that no tenant
can question his landlord’s title. But why does not some
democratic member bring in a Bill abrogating all future
attempts to establish possessory titles and vesting in the
State all properties for which no proper title can be pro-
duced ?

Q. Sugar Works Guarantee Act. Amount voted
by I’aiTanient, 0500,000. Total amount of approved
applications, £536,400. In ’95 two mills were crushing
under the Act; eight are to be ready for the season of
’96. Sixteen companies are at work under the Act,
14**,030 acres being mortgaged as security to the Govt.
Actual amount of money advanced up to date is £121,500
for erection of mills, tramways, etc. Contracts for 10
mills let up to date, of which five, costing £154,'000, went
to Maryborough, which has just gratefully returned two
Govt, supporters. Guaranteed mills to be ready for ’96
season will be capable of turning out 41,500 tons of sugar
per annum, the produce of 332,000 tons of cane. * As cane
has just risen to about £9 12s. 6d. a ton, the outlook for
next season is brilliant. .

There is, at least, one unwilling shareholder in
the Commercial Bank who might dispute his “calls ” lia-
bility with some hope of success. The aggrieved party in
question bought 100 shares on the market a week previous
to the Thursday when the old bank turned off its cash at
the meter. On which day the Board, having already
decided to put the business into liquidation and launch
the reconstruction scheme that lay drafted on the official
table, solemnly approved the transfer of those lno shares
20 minutes before closing-time. It was just a case of an
admittedly insolvent firm taking in a new partner with its
dying breath. » » .

N S W. Railway authorities are nothing if not

advt., otherwise another hoarding would be erected in
front of it. The owners of the advt. didn’t see it in the
same li'dit, so a hoarding was erected in front of it—shut-
tinl' them out. Determined not to be beaten, the firm is
busilv engaged in overtopping the screen, and the railway
people in preventing them. As a small hoarding close to
the carriage-windows will hide a much bigger one a short
distance away, the railway people have all the odds on
their side. ,

• • • •
Some of the reports upon which the B.H.
Bonanza was floated were written in days when silver was
about a third higher, and when boom-champagne still
flowed. Nevertheless, though “the crowd” is not the
most reliable, the prospects are just moderate. There
may be a vein branching from the main 8.11. lode —
possibly same that occurs in rich patches in the Consols,
further on. But at present the thing is absolutely a pro-
specting venture, and a pretty long shot at that. The
claim is certainly not an abandoned sulphide body to
which a new value has been given by recent mechanical
and other discoveries. The payable ore, whatever its
character, has yet to be found—just as it has to be in the
North Central, where the reports of “ hard country rock”
are already beginning to tire shareholders.

The manager of the Walter Scott mine. N.S.W.,
has resigned. He is said to have a good opinion of the

» * » *
There is a lot of changing about among mining-
managers. The sorrowful part of a manager’s life is to
wrestle with a poor mine, and to follow a man who has
proved it poor. In nine cases out of ten a manager
leaving a mine is pritna fade evidence that it should be
left by the shareholders as well. The Hong Kong G.M.
Co., Mt. M‘Donald, N.S.W., “has changed its mining-
manager, and intends now to pursue a vigorous mining
policy.” It is mournful to remember that many thou-
sands were paid for this show ; some £1(1,OOlt was spent
in machinery and in the mine, which has been worked
with poor results since the day it was acquired, some six
years ago.

* • * *
Stated that ,£15,000 and a quarter interest has
been given for the Totten Estate, in the vicinity of the
Wentworth mine, N.S W., and a heap of money is about
to be spent to prove which way the lode is trending. It
isn’t a lode—never was and never will be one. It is simply
a contact-joint between two formations—serpentine and
diorite, which is filled with rich ore occasionally. The
area of its richness is indicated by the surface, where an
enormous quantity of gold was obtained within a quarter
of a mile. This and a great deal more is embraced by the
Wentworth mine, and the whole of their gold has been
found within the alluvial area. The chance of finding
rich gold in any estate outside is remote. The only co.
that seems to have a show is the D’Arcv Wentworth, in-
asmuch as the contact-joint underlies into it. Already
it has been proved to pass out of one part of the Went-
worth mine at a depth of about 600 ft.

Mail to The Bulletin from Loudon:—“The

* * * *
The Coolgardie newspaper correspondents have
another foreign savant to play with— Oapt. Leoni, “who,
it is understood, holds an official position under the
Italian Government.” He has examined the Mt. Lovett
mine, and, of course, found it of unmistakable richness.
Says there are thousands of tons fit for a battery, and the
gold is not patchy or on the top. The cabled statement
that Herr Schmeisser favorably compares Coolgardie field
with the Rand should whoop things up a lot; but it will
put no gold into the scores of claims for which the English
have paid countless thousands and which are already
proven duffers.
Another hideous catastrophe has fallen over
Coolgardie. It had four or five inches of rain, “all the
dams are full, there is plenty of water for six months, it is
said, all the batteries are going to work full time, and a
bi" increase in the gold output may be looked for.” Un-
fortunately, several other things will happen. Within
six weeks we shall hear of a great lack of water. Many of
the batteries around Coolgardie, if they start at all, will
close down as soon as they clean up, and prove the un-
doubted barrenness of many apotheosised claims. A few
small rich parcels may be put through, but, with people
havin<>' any mining sense, they only prove the field in the
vicinity of Coolgardie to be a rank duffer, where there is
not a lease in a hundred that will pay to work.

• * * *
The Bulletin has been reading-up the back-files
of the Eastern daily press, and finds that within the last
two years or so exactly 28,937 mines have been found
in Westralia containing formations of from 3ft. to 700 ft.
wide and averaging between 6oz. and 19cwt. of gold per
ton of dirt. So far, not one of these mines has been
reported in any wire as having crushed a full 2 icwt. of
stone to the ton ; and, as at some time or other of its de-
velopment, each of the mines had at grass (as per “ our
own correspondent ”) between 200 and 7000 tons of quartz,
or "old slightly mixed with quartz, it follows that there
must be on hand in Westralia, in a more or less raw state,
scores of thousands of tons of gold. Under these circum-
stances the price of gold must necessarily fall below that
of zinc at an earl v date. The Bulletin, therefore, strongly
cautions all and sundry to beware of giving more than
tuppence for the best and newest of sovereigns.
Said that Zeb. Lane will float two of the Great

n Adelaide at a meeting of the members of the Cooljrardie
Prospecting Association is causing considerable anxiety
in certain Westralian quarters. An investigation is
demanded to find out who received the secret-service
money, and how much. But, as the game has been played
since the inception of the field, it is rather late to make a
fuss.

• • • •
Lord Kintore, late Governor of S. A., has been
secured as chairman of the Sulphide Corporation (Ash-
croft’s process) Ltd., and presided at the first statutory
meeting in London. The co. has acquired Ashcroft’s
patent rights for all the world except North and South
America. The process, the greater part of which, so far
as Australia is concerned, will be carried out at New-
castle, includes a preliminary concentration, which in all
possible cases will be done on the mine.
* * * •
Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.) Copper Co. made
a profit of £26,669 last year and has now resumed paying
dividends after a lapse of years. The miners are, how-
ever, receiving miserahly-poor wages.
• • * •
The strings which were being pulled to draw
the Northern Territory from the hold of S.A. Govt, hav-
ing snapped, the speculators are now taking up the far
Northern country in huge blocks, each covering thousands
of miles. They secure it primarily for pastoral purposes,
but the leases give them the exclusive right of prospect-
ing for minerals over specific thousand-mile blocks. The
rental is nominal, ami Parliament has yet to ratify these
exceptional compacts. There are less likely things than
another N. Territory boom, when the Westralian bubble
has finally burst.

Dr. EMILY H. SIEDEBERG, the first woman to graduate in
medicine in Maoriland.
The terrible Brunner mine disaster is
Attributed by some experts to the co’s greed in re-
moving the coal pillars from under the river as
the back regions of the mine became played out;
the unsupported parts thereupon subsided and
forced forward the gas-. Apropos, it is recorded in
connection with the Kaitangata disaster of long
ago (when only about a dozen lives were lost —
there are 66 in the Brunner) so liberally did
all the Australias help the miners’ families that
besides giving them a house and bit of land
each and help for a fetv years up to ,£lOO each
family, a surplus of £'0785 was left on the Govt.’s
hands. By Act of Parliament in ’92 this was
vested in the Public Trvistee to the credit of “ a
coal accident fund, to be available for the relief of
wives and children of coal-miners who may be
killed by mining accidents occurring in any part of
the colony. ”
* * * *
When the Mayor of Melbourne called a
meeting to open a local relief fund for the families
of the Brunner mine victims Chief Justice Madden
made an incautious remark. “ His only fear, he
said, “ as to the success of the movement was that
too many calls had lately been made on the purses
of charitably disposed people, who themselves
were in doubt whether they could financially sur-
vive the depression. ” This from a gentleman
who had just headed a movement for presenting a
purse of sovereigns to Dot Boucicault, who is not
know'n to be in any sort of distress, didn’t come
with particularly good grace, The Bulletin
fancies.
* * * »
Re the comparative position of a w r idow
whose husband has lost his life under sensational
circumstances, a typical instance of the hideous
humbug of fashionable “charity” was the fact
that, while superfluous thousands of pounds w r ere
being subscribed, years ago, in Melbourne, for the
w'idows of the pilots drowned in the “ Rip ’ acci-
dent, a woman whose husband had been more
prosaically asphyxiated in the Yarra was proved
to have died of actual starvation. If real charity
oovereth a multitude of sins, the notoriety-seeking
sort is surely one of the meanest of all sins.

sports the other day, Dot Boucicault had given his
ticket to two ladies, who did not come on time,
and that an Australian youth, who was requested
by someone who observed the comedian’s predica-
ment to hand over to the latter his spare ticket,
“ refused with a fiendish grin.” “ Not I,” said he ;
Mr. Boucicault says the Australians can’t speak

A portrait is here
printed of Dr. Emily
Siedeberg, the first of her
sex to obtain a medical
degree in Maoriland. The
charming young lady was
born several years ago in
Clyde, Vincent County,
South Maoriland, of a
German father and a
North of Ireland Quaker
mother, the daughter of
a Dublin barrister. She
has had a very bright
public school and univer-
sity career, and was, on
attaining her M.B. and
M.Ch. degree, straightway
offered by Govt, an as-
sistant medical offieership
at Seacliff asylum. Miss
Siedeberg’s brother was,
for two years, champion
chess player of M.L. be-
fore going to Berlin, where
he. has, for three years,
been studying electrical
engineering. Maoriland’s
first native-born and lo-
cally - educated doctress
now goes to Dublin to
study gynaecology.
* *
Now and then the
world hears of a returned
Coolgardian with money,
as a sort of counterpoise
to the many returned Cool-
gardians without boots,
and some of these people'
try to start a feeble imi-
tation of the red, roaring
days of old Bendigo and
Forest Creek, when the
lucky digger broke up a
whole town with a small
gum-tree at night and
cheerfully paid for it in
the morning, and when it
rained gold and profanity
and blue ruin night and day. But it is a very, very
feeble and degenerate imitation. The other day a
returned person with coin from the West tried to
get up a riot in a Tasmanian hotel. He had no
one to help him ; the show would have started in
the good old days with a free fight of 12 diggers
and 47 dogs, but this digger was “on his own.”
Also, he lacked the rich profanity, needful in such
a case, but he swept twelve glasses off the counter,
said “damn” once or twice, and hit the piano
severely with the music-stool. Then a policeman
led him away and he was fined £5, and he didn’t
burn down the hotel or destroy, the town, or do
anything as they used to do it in the fine, copious
old way of 58. A lucky digger making a row in
fifi is a very tame spectacle.
Cyclists, in costume and leading their
machines, attended the funeral of a lady at Coota-
mundra, N.S.W., tlm-other Sunday. Ihe creature
capable of leading a bicycle to a funera is just the
sort of person who would typewrite his love-letters
and have them signed by a clerk. The bicyc e
it is now the first person of the Australian trinity.
The other two are the horse and Lattersalls
sweep.

most gorgeous and glittering object, composed
almost entirely of nickel, and with ivory handles.

Polyandry on the wallaby. Bolted re-
formatory girl (17, at leas; she said so) confided in
a swagman and agreed to co-travel. When near
Mt. Cam bier they picked up two more swagmen,
who willingly joined the family party on equal
terms. Arrested in Victoria for vagrancy ; result
—imprisonment for the men, more reformatory for
the girl.
A large, elegantly-dressed,
stern, proud citizen, much revered
in mercantile circles, had an un-
pleasant experience in Queen-st.,
Melbourne, the other day, when
he was accosted by a frowsy
female with a semi-black eye, the
atmosphere of a long drunk, and
indications of having dossed in a
dust- box. The disreputable person
rolled up against the immaculate
merchant; wound her arms about
his fat, red neck, sobbed beerily
on his pure white tie, and told
him thickly that she loved him
more than ever, and besought him
to forget and forgive, and to
return home to the children, and
no questions would be asked.
The respectable citizen gasped,
turned purple, and exclaimed :
“ Begone, woman ! ” But [the
woman refused to bego, and for
five minutes the poor man wres-
tled in the street with a strange,
dusty, beery female who clung
about his collar, while a number
of virtuous workmen from an
adjacent building gathered round,
and advised him to overlook the
past, and try and live peacefully
with the poor woman. At last
the merchant gave a bricklayer
half-a-crown to hold the distressed
lady whilst he escaped, and then
the woman claimed the brick-
layer as the husband of her ten
children ; but the bricklayer was
a diplomatist—he gave her three-
pence, and showed her a near cut
to a pub.
The Poet’s Corner—mostly
the pub. The Ladies’ Column A
Births, Marriages and Deaths.

Amongst the exhibits at Sydney Agricul-
tural Show was a polished oak coffin, alongside of
which could be seen a couple of hearses and
mourning coaches. An embalmed “ exhibit ” may

A Richmond (Vic.) •clergy-
man was the victim of a rather
unpleasant practical joke one
Sunday night recently. After his
sermon he said he would be glad
if any ladies in his congregation
would embroider a few small com-
munion-cloths. ‘ “And,” added
his reverence, untying a small
parcel, “ I would like them done
in something like this style.’’
Then he shook out and displayed,
to the gaze of the horrified
“saved,” a beautifully-embroi-
dered “Oh !”

An item for school-fees, in
an insolvent’s schedule, gave
J udge Molesworth achance against
those snobs who refuse to use the
State-schools, but don’t mind let-
ting-in the poor private-school-
master. Every schoolmaster in
Melbourne can tell tales to this
effect, and also of the number of
well-dressed, fashionably-living
folk who beg education for their
children at half-fees, and very
often no fees. (hie master tells of
two boys taken at his school for
half-fees ; one came every day on
a pony, and the other had a han-
som sent for him when it rained.
Also, he lately lost a boy who
was taught for nothing because a
charge was made for his books,
the irate father a member of a
very swell-club—saying that lie
considered the school had had so
many pupils brought to it by his
patronage that he was astounded
at such meanness.

THE BRIDLE-TRACK.
N.B.W.
This worship of Horse
Is a sin and a curse,
So we hear in our parson’s talk ;
But we’re steering straight
For the Golden Gate,
And we may as well ride as walk.
Shall our friendship break
O’er the way we take
Since neither shall follow it back ?
Let him hump his load
Down the two-chain road—
Em going the Bridle-Track.

BEAUTIES OF THE BENCH.
Corruption’s penetrating stench
Is lessened somewhat on the Bench ;
With Baxter’s disappearance thence
Coes also backstairs’ influence ;
They’ve shot the Bird that fouled its nest,
And Justice finds, with smiling zest,
Although she’s lost her ( R)app// port,
Her haven in a purer court.

pangs of death. But such anguish doesn’t last
long, as a rule.

Premier Kingston lias, after all, got the
whip-hand of the angry Adelaide Hospital doctors,
who, in resentment of his recent ill-advised abuse,
have handed in their resignations in a body. He
has informed them that the resignations will not
be accepted until other arrangements have been
made for the care of the sick. Meanwhile, Govt,
is advertising for hospital-doctors at substantial
fixed salaries.

New Q. Gov. Lamington, landing at Thurs-
day Island, was presented with seven addresses,
from
1. The Japs.
2. The Manilla Men.
3. The Malays.
4. The Chinamen.
5. The Kanakas.
6. The Alleged White Men.
7. Ditto White Children.
Proportion—five colored to two white.-
* * * •
More Botany Bay mercy. Last Deniliquin
Quarter Sessions a young man named Roth,
charged with criminal libel, was found guilty, but
recommended to mercy. Being without means,
he was unable to employ counsel. He threw him-
self on the mercy of the Court and pointed out
that he had been locked up for two months await-
ing trial, and that having no money he had been
unable to collect evidence in palliation of his
statements. The Judge said he would give
prisoner the opportunity of not going to gaol (the
man who had been too poor to employ a lawyer !)
and would order him to pay a fine of one hundred
pounds only, in default six months’ gaol. Com-
ment would be an insult to the human intelli-
gence.
* * * *
Unprotected selectors’ wives are often
molested by roaming Indian and Syrian pedlars,
and another danger threatens single bus’nmen
at the hands of the Syrian women, many young
and pretty, who hawk about the bush. They are
far bolder than their dark brothers, and, as a rule,
walk straight into the lonely bushman’s camp,
squat down, and by most suggestive means
coax the inmates to buy “ a ni’ silk aankairchief
for deer leetle girl.” More than one man has
already thus been lured to ruin. These attractive
women, though mostly innocent of English beyond
the prices of their wares, seem to know exactly
how strongly British law sides with the allegedly-
offended woman. Hence their dangerously familiar
behaviour.
* * * *
The councillors of Milton (M.L.) lately
objected to the unbecoming conduct of the Mayor
in smoking a brief black pipe at council-meetings,
and sitting with his feet on the table. “When
you elected me,” said his Washup, curtly, “you
elected my pipe, too.” “Then, I suppose, we
must address you as ‘ Mr. Mayor and pipe ? ” said
a disgusted councillor. “ Quite so,” said the un-
abashed father ; “ you can smoke, too, if you like.
Then the council unanimously lit-up.
♦ * ♦ *
If any man tells you leprosy is
not attributable to the influx of
Asiatics, the following facts may
serve to convince him. In 1880
Queensland had eleven lepers all
Chinese. Not until 1892 was a
European leper known to exist in
that province. In Victoria, up to
1892, 10 lepers were recorded—all
Chinese, with one exception. In
Nov., 1894, 26 cases of leprosy existed
in Queensland. Of the patients 11
were Chinese, 6 were Kanakas, and
7 -were of colored races other than
Chinese and Kanakas. Two pnly
were Europeans. The Chows lia-
bility to leprosy may be Nature s
compensation for his comparative im-
munity from tuberculosis.

The eloping girl arrested in
Victoria for stealing the clothes (the
property of her alleged mother) she
eloped in, recalls the method for-
merly adopted by sweater-tailors,
ladies in a certain way of business,
and others, for compelling repentant
employees to stick to their bar-
gains. Their clothes belonged to
the master—or mistress— the
fear of either being arrested for
larceny, or of going away in a news-
paper, generally proved effective.
There is an express law now pro-
viding against this form of tyranny,
and, apparently, it could be very pro-
fitably extended.

Sydney doctors ask that special concessions
be granted them re telephone rates. Postmaster-
General Cook views the application with surprise.
If Mr. Cook knew how much work Sydney doctors
are doing for nothing, he would speedily recover
from his surprise.

Writes Emily Soldene from London :
T+ ; a thp correct thing now at balls, dinners, and crushes
cf ill kinds” to serve the champagne in big glass jugs-
much nicer than the bottle business. You can get a
tumbler now without any trouble, and ha\ e a good drink.
There was once, in America, a lady whose colossal
mouth was the wonder of hundreds of millions of
people That lady, if now alive, will welcome
with enthusiasm the change noted above. Liquor
flows ever so much more quickly out of a jug than
out of a bottle.
A Melb. Hospital -jiurse : “ Slaving from
12 to 14 hours a day ; carrying heavy cans of food
up steep stairs ; washing patients in the last stages
of loathsome diseases. Often sitting out a weary
night-watch of nine hours, to the music of groan-
ing, cursing, blaspheming men in various degrees
of d.t.’s, the monotony not even broken when a
couple of porters enter and remove, in the ‘shell,’
some poor wretch who has ‘crossed the border.’
Attending to the wants of the vilest outcasts,
dressing ghastly wounds, laying-out the corpses of
typhoid and diphtheria patients, washing China-
men’s feet. And all for .£l5 a year.”

last month in Silverton (N.S.W.), and the people
of the dead-horse city elected two brewers, a hotel-
keeper and a cellarman. The Great Australian
Thirst is a noble thing out West.

Ledgerdemain—Book-keeping.
Ifc is not generally known that the original
name of St. Paul’s College, affiliated to the Sydney
University, was Trinity. Why the change took
place will form a chapter in ecclesiastical history
some day.
The Q. leper has been lying low for some
weeks in order to record his vote for a friendly
Govt, at the current elections. Now he is be-
ginning to boom again, and no less than three of
him turned up last week. This paper trusts that
Lord Lamington has taken proper precautions
against infection at Govt. House.

There is likely to be a “strike” amongst
publicans and beer-drinkers at Tam worth (N.S.W.),
where beer is now 3d. a pint, which “ Bung ’ says
does not pay, but the bser-eater says lie will pay
no more. At Lithgow (N.S.W.), in the' “good”
days, such a strike occurred. The price then was
6d. per glass, and the miners thought 3d. enough.
“ Bung ” objected, and the beer-drinkers passed
solemn resolutions not to drink beer at 6d. per
pint under penalty of being ducked in the local
creek. Two duckings did take place, but after
three weeks’ total abstinence the buyers gave in.

for the family,” announces on its first page that it
is “ conducted by Dr. Mac Somebody ” (Scotch,
of course), and decorates its last page with an
extensive undertaker s advt.

Somebody was complaining, the other day,
that an unduly rough quality of boots is used* in
Vic. reformatory schools. Whereupon the secre-
tary of the Tender Board made a personal ex-
amination of the boots and stated that they were
“highly suitable for reformatory purposes.”
Chastening to the feet, no doubt.
* * » *
At a Queensland election: “Do you
believe in the Repeal of the Contagious Diseases
Act?” Candidate: “ Well—as to measles and
scarlet fever and all that sort of thing—that’s a
matter for medical men to settle. Anyway, I
believe if they’re contagious they ought to be
abolished.” One of this candidate’s supporters,
when twitted with the ignorance of his man, who
actually didn’t know what the C.D. Act was,
replied indignantly : “ Well, so much the more to
his credit! ”
MY SWEETHEARTS
SLEEVES
For The Bulletin.
My sweetheart is a girl as sweet
As one may wish to see—
Search all the world, you will not find
A fairer maid than she.
Her figure, too, is neat and trim,
Yet—ah !—my sad soul grieves,
For when she dons her smartest frock
She seems to be all sleeves !
I take the dear girl to the play
One night in every week ;
There, in the middle of the stalls,
We sit—but do not speak :
For all the young men gaze at her,
Yet—ah ! my sad soul grieves—
Never a girl can gaze at me,
I’m hidden—by her sleeves !
My sweetheart bought a bicycle
(An instrument I hate),
And soon upon the wobbling wheel
She’ll gracefully gyrate;
But one thing only comforts me,
Though still my sad soul grieves,
For, when she had her bloomers made,
She made them of those sleeves !
She does not wear those bloomers now
(We married—years ago),
And we have got a little son
(The finest child I know !);
And when the wee man went to school
(Now this one scarce, believes)
She made his first small pantaloons
Out of those same old sleeves !
J. H. WAGNER.
RESURGAM.
(The Johnny's Prayer for Rain).
He gazes on the summer sky,
So bright and clear,
And from his aching, heavy eye
There flows a tear.
“ The jimp Gee-Gees have flown this shore,”
His sobs declare,
“ No bathing-scene exposes more
Maud Hobson fair ;
And, with the pantomime must go
Those fetching tights
That Florence Young is wont to show
Amidst the lights.
Why does no shower of rain disturb
The weather warm,
To grant us glimpses o’er the kerb
Of female form ? ”
He sighed, then reck’ning that the dainty lace
May rise again,
He bowed his head and sought a holy place
And prayed for rain.
M‘G.

Sydney, April 7, 1800.
My Dear Moorabinda—
There was an unprecedented deluge of vice-
royalty at the lively Agricultural Show last
Saturday, which made rather an embarrassment
of spectacles—one wanted to be dotted all over
with eyes to get an idea of what was going on. It
was an amazingly good show. The pavilion was
packed full of exhibits, including a furriers’stall,
whereon was displayed a very taking thing in
floor-rugs—the skins of three golden dingoes on a
dark fur ground. Opossum fur seems to be coming
to the fore, though I think it is rather too
smudgy to be altogether becoming ; the Tasmanian
wallaby has the prettiest fur for capes. The newest
idea for bridal veils seems to be a soft fall of silk-
work silk, judging by a bridal doll in a case ; and,
really, the soft yellow Danae-like covering wouldn’t
be a bad idea for the omnipresent girl who marries
for money to face the music in. A femininely-
interesting exhibit was the silkworm display from
grub to reel. Silkworms were once the only hope
of the surplus Australian female, and I fancy
their cultivation must be good for the complexion
—the girl who has to chivvy the cocoons round in
boiling water gets her face well steamed, anyhow.
Hawkesbury Agricultural Society made a brave
show of tempting vegetables, including many
weird products which one hardly knew how to
deal with. For instance, a melon with a rind the
delicate color and texture of a lady’s flesh-pink
kid glove ought to be viewed more soulfully than
an ironbark pumpkin, and it seems to me that a
squash like carved and fluted ivory almost comes
under the heading of poetry.
The gee-gees were the stars of the show-ring—
the ladies’ hunting contest interesting womankind
most. The hard-faced equestriennes were not
very smartly turned out—they all used the old-
fashioned bucket-seated saddle with the unsightly
third horn, and a little more polish on bits and
buckles wouldn’t have been amiss. It seemed a
pity such plucky riders shouldn’t be stylish as
well. Almost all the human style centred in the
new reserve, a grassy terrace peppered with rustic
chairs, with a marquee in the middle. The
Governors were received with military honors—
the Anathema in two different keys by a shrill
chorus of boys and a blaring band producing a
Chinese discord that was novelty, at least. Gov.
Hampden looked elegantly but hopelessly bored
as usual—the very antithesis of the waddling Gov.
Brassey, who is vigorously alert and reminds me
more than ever of Ryley’s Duke of Muffshire seen
broadways in a soup-ladle. He wore a grey turn-
out and carried a large white brolly lined with
green. Sir Fowell Buxton was discreetly attired
like a Wesleyan elder. Doubtless he felt like it
—but on seeing H.E. I have it in my heart to ex-
cuse him for all his worldly shortcomings. When
Nature dowers a man with a long, severe upper
lip and the Nonconformist fashions make him
wear a monkey fringe of beard his face is apt to
get the better of him. lam sure many a man, if
all could be known, has had a tremendous battle
between good resolves and Family Herald legs,
with a victory for the legs, and the effect of a long,
silky moustache on an otherwise well-meaning
youth has robbed the Y.M.C.A. of many a
convert.
Lady Hampden turned up at the show in slate-
colored silk with fixings of ivory silk and black
silk gimp, a necklace of turquoises set in gold, and
a black jet bonnet with black feather and cluster
of pink roses, and black veil with black spots.
Lady Brassey wore a skittish hat of black with
waving plumes and wings of china-blue chiffon,
and a gown the color of old ivory with silk sleeves,
the bodice and skirt draped in spotted muslin ;
necklace of small pearls. Mrs. Freeman Thomas
—a nice-looking damsel with plenty of go—sjtorted
ecru with floral fixings, and a black hat with a
white veil sago-spotted in black ; and the local
viceregal damsels were all in light frocks. Autumn
is as autumn does, evidently. The Acting-
Governoress sported black, and society, fluttering
in the last robes of summer, came mostly double-
banked by naval youths, some of whom (new-
comers) looked like pocket editions of the youths
they succeed. Male fashions at the horse-show
are occasionally violent. One youth in quarter-
acre checks and a pink shirt suggested the
“Josser Jockey,” and an aide-de-camp in a pink
shirt-front and a scarlet tie (and other things, of
course) looked like the forerunner of a gaudy taste
in haberdashery on the part of the winter Johnnie.
Apropos Governors at the show. An enter-
prising thimble-rigger had fixed up a cosy little
arrangement in a convenient nook—a sort of
“ stepping-stone to gambling for the young,” and
he was only warming-up to his work on Thursday,
when the police dropped on him with the stern
mandate that “Gambling must be suspended
while the Governors are on the ground ! ” This is
a new halo for vice-royalty, but it’s a lucky thing
for the racecourses their Excellencies don’t always
wear it. If gambling was always “suspended”
when things were V.R., how much would the
loyal racing-clubs pay their many-sided Excellen-
cies to stay away ?
The agricultural banquet the same evening
in Sydney Town Hall was well-attended
—all the Governors at the spread, and all the
Governoresses in a hollow square in the Southern
balcony, where the majority of the women sat
looking on as large as life; but Gov. Hampden
was the first one to include them in his speech.
The Governors all sat at the top, but the effect
was not over-brilliant. Evidently a man can’t
get to the Apostles’ table and keep his hair on, for
the distinguished guests were mostly egg-bald.
Mind you, there is a suggestion of opulence and
well-being about a hairless cranium—a bald head
is the trade-mark of the prosperous—and I am
told that there are few bald men in lunatic
asylums. Here was a case in point (I am talking
now of prosperity, not lunacy) : the hairiest
man at the agricultural banquet was the forlorn
ex-Premier Parkes. But Premier Reid looks like
getting on—he is becoming so bald and fat. If he
continues to shed his auburn locks and put on
girth as he is doing at present, he will end in
being the humpty-dumptyist Premier on record.
The vice-regal speeches were interestin’, as Gov.
Hampden would say. Our own Excellency talks
like a mixture of actor Titheradge and a high-
church parson. He used no notes ; and his tactful,
tasteful, more or less spontaneous speech was dis-
tinctly the oration of the evening. The Governor
has a new way of presenting old facts. He
doesn’t invariably call it the “ dear Mother land,”
but alludes to the “old country ” once in a way,
and he doesn’t mention the silken bonds, though
his mention of Anglo - Australian ties being
“strong as links of iron” was rather an em-
barrassing simile to use in Botany Bay—it seemed
to me. Gov. Brassey’s boresome speech sounded
like the effort of a man with it all written out on
sheets of paper balancing in front of him on a
dessert-dish ; and Gov. Buxton talked like a Sun-
day edition of Stirling Whyte, and pronounced it
“ Austrailyah,” “bordah,” “greatab.” Gov.
Buxton, by the way, is the speaking image of
Water-jug Foster, and might be the elder brother
of Dr. Garran, of N.S.W. Leg. Council, who has
the advantage of him as far as hair goes. Admira
Bridge also orated, in a “ funny ” speech ; hut ]'
thought the best jest he made was in thanking
Australia for its hospitality to the navy. This
must have been sarcasm, for the Admiral is the
unfeeling martinet who kept his officers manoeuv
ring on the water de -pite all the manoeuvres o: !
society ashore when the fleet was at Hobart.
The vice-regal ladies observed the very opposite
tactics to feminine vice-royalty gone before, in tha ;
they only arrived when the smoke and speeches
began to ascend, whereas it used to be the caper
to leave when cigars went round. The feature of
the V.R. party was that everybody wore high
neck and long sleeves, making several half-peeler
women in the same balcony hastily put'bn their
cloaks. Lady Hampden’s coiffure was artistic,
and she wore black silk with sleeves of grey figured
silk and ruffles of black-jetted tulle. Lady Brassey
wore her favorite color in a yellow silk blouse
covered with black grenadine encrusted with jet,
and saddle flaps on the shoulders of yellow silk—
a pearl necklace with diamond pendant, grey
gloves, and a painted fan. Mrs. Freeman Thomas
wore a black skirt with a cream silk blouse pom-
padour pattern, black ostrich-feather fan. One
member of the Y.R. party in a white blouse
spotted with black suggested the very early
Briton who preferred to dress himself in
the hide of a spotted cow; and the vice-
regal damsels and the acting vice-regal damsels
were marvels of sweet simplicity. Four aides
including the depressed Capt. Freeman Thomas,
who appeared to be miles deep in meditation
and too weary to pick a flower for his button-
hole-squired the party and wore a lot of polish
on their shirt-fronts and gardenias in three of
their coats. It was no joke to squire that party—
eleven ladies to four youths. It looks as if the
present address of the superfluous woman is Govt.
House.
The first day of Randwick Autumn Meeting
was V.R. for about 10 minutes only, H.E. arriving-
after the Show, but the suite were dotted about
nearly all the time—one thin, tall private sec. in a
long grey suit looked like several feet of leaden
spouting. Sec. Wallington shed the light of his
switch-back profile on the proceedings, and
another welcome stranger was a handsome and
popular legal baritone who has returned to
Society’s bosom after a long stay in the musical
world of Paris. However, inasmuch as he brought
back his silky moustache in most excellent order,
he cannot have been badly afflicted with the stage
fever. A thirteenth-century shave is the unvary-
ing sign of the truly stage-stricken.
There was a frocky look about the well-filled
members’ stand, where the two most startling
garments were sported by two sisters (who sug-
gested comic-opera princesses) in ultramarine blue,
with blue velvet tops and white satin sleeves and
fixings fretted with silver, with felt hats trimmed
with blue and white exactly en suite. They also
looked like advts. for somebody’s washing-blue.
A usually smart society matron looked a sketch in
saffron-yellow with a stole of pansy-velvet and a
hat of many colors. The latest society bride
looked pretty as a peach in black crepon with
moonshine-green bodice draped in chiffon, and
yellow roses in her hat; and the married daughter
of a millionaire looked good form in blue and white
Alahama-coon-striped silk, with a black hat and
feathers.
A matron, just returned from a trip ’Ome,
sported an old familiar gown—oddly enough!
That is a rare occurrence. The average woman
fresh from Bond-street usually paralyses you with
the spikiness of her headdress and the eccentricity
of her frock ; though I have no fault to find in
these directions with another Society-matron, in a
very Frenchy tea-rose pink gown, with a real lace
pelerine, and her hair draped over her ears and
low in the neck, it la Trilby, and surmounted by a
warlike toque, all roses and vanity. The only
thing that wasn’t Frenchy was the lady inside, and
she looked as if she knew her prayers batter than
her Petit Journal. To make a real success of
such a Parisian turnout, a woman should see to it
that her mood matches her gown. I here weren t
many Autumnal novelties on view the first day,
the weather encouraging the people to trot out
their Summer attire.
The engagement between a dashing militaire
and one of the departed Gee-Gees has been broken
off by the Gee-Gee, who was due to arrive in
Sydney by a recent steamer. But a letter came
instead of the lady, who backed away from the
hymeneal altar on the ground that she was quite
too delicate to marry. After all the engagements
and rumored engagements between members of
the Gaiety chorus and smitten youths, only one
really came off ; you see, there are no silver kings
left nowadays. By the way, what’s the betting
now on the venturesome pressman who rushed
’Ome to marry Madge Rossell ?
An overflowing Society house welcomed B. and B.
back to Sydney Criterion on Paster Saturday,
when “The Pickpocket” went up fittingly
enough at this festive season. But it certainly
looked odd to see a great big notice —“ Beware of
Pickpockets”—on the stall-doors of “The Pick-
pocket ” itself—it was enough to discourage many
a playgoing youth with hayseed in his hair. There
wasn’t any Tanqueray frocking in the good old
farce, in which the stage-doctor made his usual
mistake, and sounded the patient with the wrong
end of the stethoscope. Emma Temple (who
certainly is one of the few women who can run
upstairs poetically) and Hilda Spong wore white
for three acts, and in the first short scene the two
ingenues wore frocks we’d all seen before. • I liked
Miss Temple’s soft white muslin better than Miss
Spong’s stiffly-starched garment, which one could
absolutely hear crackling from the dress-circle.
Now, I think Hilda has quite enough starch with-
out putting any in her dresses. Clever Dot Bouci-
cault was the waiter, “ Soup, sah ! ” and the hero
of the hour. It is really too awful to contemplate
that we must soon lose this gifted mummer, to
whom all Australia now yells out, “ Are you going
home to marry Pattie Browne ? ”
Rumored that a golden-haired and plenteous
Sydney society girl is engaged once more—this
time to a denizen of one of the Points.
Vice-royalty rallied to Rtndwick in full force
to see Wallace win Sydney c up on Easter Mon-
day. Sporting South Australians will rejoice to
hear that the austere Gov. Buxton had a seat in
the vice-regal enclosure, though His Ex. really
looked as out-of-place as a feather on a cod-fish,
and wore a coat that somehow looked unfinished
without a collection-plate. But earnest Christians
in Adelaide will rejoice to hear that though all
the other vice-regalities stood up for the exciting
Cup finish, Gov. Buxton remained seated with
the aspect of one to whom racehorses are vanity.
By the way, I wonder how Sydney’s blase Gov.
enjoys having Sir Fowell for a guest ? Consider-
ing the able manner in which Gov. Hampden
dodged coming to Australia per same canoe as
Gov. Buxton, it hardly looks as if our potentate
would consider his S.A. Ex. an indispensable
factor in the success of a race-party.
The V.R. women were not exactly over-dressed.
Lady Hampden evidently has only one best
bonnet, and not one of her daughters has the art
of tying on a veil properly to make it look like a
point in the toilette and not merely so many yards
of gauze to keep the flies off. As for their average
dressing, one might throw a stone in any boarding-
school and hit a dozen better frocks. Mrs. Free-
man-Thomas wore the “cheekiest” dress in the
V.R. box. It was a sort of pale - apricot
goffered silk, and had shoulder draperies of mellow
lace—a black sash, and Dudley Hardy hat of black
velvet and plumes and a dab of pale-blue, com-
pleting the picture. On the members’ stand there
was an olla podrida of summer and autumn dress-
ing. It was, perhaps, rather too soon for some of
the fur trimmings, which did look sudden
under the hot sun, but some of the flutter-
ing muslins were equally past the market,
it seemed to me. Perhaps the most correct-
looking garment on the lawn was worn by an
elegant Sydney woman—a snuff-colored gown, ex-
quisitely fitting, with delicate suggestions of violet
and white satin in the front of the bodice, and a
toque hat in the three colors. Purple and violet,
by the way, are the modish winter shades. The
golden-haired wife of a northern squatter wore a
regal garment of purple velvet and sleeves of
Trilby pattern, which became her to a T.
The Bland Holts had a S.R.O. opening at Syd-
ney Royal on Saturday—there wasn’t room for
even an astral body after 8 o’clock. The ladies of
the co. were warmly received, and pretty Mrs.
Bland Holt was handed up a singing canary in a
gilded cage adorned with plaid ribbons.
Australian girls may be interested to know that
smart London is going mad over very Irioht
colored straw hats, while the gorgeousness of the
new petticoats is making popular the French
custom of lifting the dress skirt very high with
loth hands.
The newest thing in Chinkie culture that I have
lately heard was a tea given to 2ti Chinamen and
presided over by a large number of young ladies.
r° ask a Chow to tea appears to me to be a rather
mettlesome invitation, considering what a connois-
seur in the beverage he generally is ; but then, so
long as it is handed to him by a plump and juicy
damsel, “John” will swallow cold jioison—for the
yellow man is very strong on the fair sex. That
is the great drawback to letting sweet seventeen
try her hand at teaching him, and is the reason
why any sensible man will chain his female belong-
ings to a log rather than let them go round con-
verting Ah Sin to the Fold—of their arms, in the
long run. For there is no gainsaying the fascina-
tions the slant-eyed son of Tea-land has for wo-
nianity. To begin with, I never can see much
difference between a nice, fresh Chow and a naval-
officer. Both are sun-burnt and clean-shaven and
both are adored and hotlv pursued by certain
members of the sex to which I find it sometimes
very amusing to belong.
Australia will soon have as many spicy Chinese
scandals as Chinatown in ’Frisco, where the efforts
of the infatuated Yankee girls to lead John from
his joss are frequently put a stop to by the iriar-
nage of teacher and pupil, with her family tearing
their hair in the background. I know of'a family
of very presentable girls living in a Sydney suburb
who make pets of a whole market-garden of
Chinkies, to whom they have given romantic
names, and whom they invariably address with deep
respect as “ Ah,” which is said to be Chinkie for
“Mister.” The conseouence is that the grateful
Chows serve up so much heart in the lettucee with
which they supply the family that every two-
pen’orth thereof almost amounts to a sentimental
declaration. Cabbagee with no worms i’ the bud
and a silk handkerchief or jar of ginger at the
Chow New Year are the sure and certain reward
of these back-door dallyings. Well does the wily
yellow man know the weight a present carries
with a woman.
New things in the soft, hygienic, cellular fabric
are the cellular stockings. Also I notice an extra
daintiness about some of the undergarments which
are frilled with fine cambric and new lace and tied
with pink ribbons, and are lovely things sugges-
tive of honeymoons, or the clothes that always
get hung nearest the neighbors’ windows on
washing-days. And the soothing part of it is that
the price of these dainty, hygienic articles is about
half what it used to be. And I would like to
personally recommend the cellular riding-corsets,
which are very comfortable and practically ever-
lasting. These corsets are much used by Society
on its bike, worn in conjunction with cellular
bloomers.
My Melb. correspondent writes: —
“An incident of a recent ceremonial was the
introduction to Gov. Brassey of the defendant in a
squalid and well-advertised slander case. The
bluff baron bolting the pill in choleric silence was
a contrast to his diplomatic predecessor in office,
who smiled arid murmured sweet nothings in the
ear of every schoolgirl to whom he handed a prize,
even unto the seventy times seventh, and would
have chatted amicably to Old Nick himself, had
the latter sought the pleasure of his acquaintance.
“The station-manager’s boundary-riding wife,
and the lady-farmers who take turns to do a
hoise’s work (vide recent Bulletin pars.), make
pale the halo of martyrdom worn by the Western
District (Vic.) woman who daily submitted to be
harnessed to her husband’s plough. To escape
detection, the tyrant drove his furrows before the
neighbors were out of bed ; but somehow they
suspected, and one morning they got up early, hid
behind a shed, and saw for themselves. Then
they rushed out, and, after handling the man
more or less roughly, laid information against him
for insulting behavior. At the police-court the
victim declined to speak, while the accused, with
tearful eyes and quivering lips, complained to the
Bench of the conduct of his assailants, ‘ They
bate me, your Anners,’he said, pitifully. ‘They
bate me on me own ground ! ’
“ Shearing before last, a clothier’s tout had a
painful experience among shed-workers. He took
orders for suits made to measurement, his terms
being a -small initial deposit and ‘ payment on
delivery.’ The customers were carefully mea-
sured, but exchanged names. Smith’s dimensions
being credited to Jones, and so on. When the
clothes came up from town, with names affixed,
the alleged owners, trying them on amid the scorn
and derision of onlookers, refused with lavish pro-
fanity to pay for the blanky misfits. The bam-
boozled agent, seeing for himself that the garments
were ‘ impossible, ’ agreed to part with the lot for a
sov. each. As soon as he was off the place, the
tricksters changed into the suits that fitted them,
and relate to this day how thev got ’em cheap.
Meantime, their gulfhad to face' his firm with the
story of the losing deal, and took the penalty of
his supposed disastrous bungling in the matter of
measurements. ,
“ No wonder the once omnipotent Wallington
feels out in the cold in the Brassey family-party
with its numerous new brooms, all more or less
akin. Then the Head of the House takes life
seriously, while his womankind are philanthropic
and recreate only in the departments of bikes and
babies. The members of the Hopetoun renime
were young-and frisky, and after the Countess
came back from with a taste for amateur
jugglery and sleight of hand, meal-times grew
more and more thrilling. The diners, one and all,
whisked and reversed overflowing glasses with
non-spilling intentions, while plates were broken
by the dozen in unsuccessful attempts to balance
them on finger-tips, elbows, and even noses.

identical chair in which Speaker Manners-Sutton

There is something in teetotaller Lormer’s
contention that every magistrate is biassed, one
way or other, on the drink question. But the
Lormer species of “ beak ” is much more biassed
than the other sort, unfortunately.
Abe Alston, the artist, who won the Vic-
torian travelling scholarship some years ago, is
making a name in London for his black-and-
white work, which adorns the pages of Pearson’s
Magazine and the Pall Mall Magazine.
At that imposing ceremony, the opening
of the Equitable Life Co’s Melbourne offices,
Gov. Brassey turned up in a suit of grey tweed
and bowler hat. He was positively the only man
there who was not frock-coated and tall-hatted.
Parkes has been elected president of
N.S.W. Zoo. The other N.S.W. ex-Premier,
Dibbs, the other night presided at the meeting
held by Poet Neild to denounce his critics. From
political to poetical meetings ! The lions are eat-
ing mfce in their old age.
Mayor Ives, of Sydney, boasts that he
was the father of the idea of a bridge connecting
the north and south sides of the harbor. The
man capable of trying to copyright a scheme of
this sort would endeavor to patent the idea of
opening an umbrella when it rained.
The “F. C. Selous,” cabled as having
recently led a force against the revolting
Matab'eles —and been repulsed—is the well-known
African hunter, and the original of Rider Hag-
gard's “ Allan Quatermain.” Haying tired of
shooting big game, he is now potting niggers.
Melb. Demise : Fred. Secretan, head of
the detectives from’6B to’73. There was a row
on his elevation, as he had been but nine years in
the force, and had not signalised himself ; but he
did fairly well —and afterwards did not do well in
a pub as uncongenial to him as to George Ireland.
For some years past, he had been satisfied with his
pension.
Bigamy appears to be somewhat lightly
regarded bv the police. One very handsome and
charming ’woman has been running round Aus-
tralia fi r the last few years picking up a husband
here and there, but no notice has so far been
taken. The Bulletin does not wonder at men
marrying her, for she is one of the gaudiest creatures
on earth.

The centenary of the death of the poet
Rnrns is to be celebrated in July. On the occasion
of the centenary of the poet’s birth, 38 years ago,
a great Scotch banquet took place m Melbourne
at which haggis, whisky, Scotch songs, and snuff
were part of the repast. Aspinall was present,
and some one asked him what he thought of
haggis. “I think,” said he, “that it is like a
dirty football with a bad smell attached.”
W. T. Forster, son of a former N.S.W.
Premier, after nearly a score of years of lawyer-
practice in N.S.W. backblocks, was admitted to
practice in Westralia the other day. At the same
time D. J. Walker Smith, English barrister and
literary man, who has successively found Adelaide
and Melbourne hard roads to hoe, was admitted,
along with about a dozen others. In all, about
150 lawyers are putting in time over yonder.
Premier Kingston and editor Finlayson,
of S.A. Register, were staunch private friends
prior to the miserable Adelaide Hospital dispute
which has brought Kingston so much trouble and
lost him his brainiest colleague, J. H. Gordon,
whose sister’s official appointment was the starting-
point of the dispute in question. Recipe—to burst
up a firm or a Ministry: Introduce the personal
interests of a, member’s women-folk. As it was
from the beginning, in the days of Paradise.
Sangster, the member for Port Melbourne,
who charges ship-owners with sending forth
coffins, is an earnest Scotchman of the heaviest
description. “What are you besides being a
member of Parliament ? ” was one of the questions
put to him at the inquiry. “ I’m a good many
things,” said Sangster, gravely. “So far as my
sea-going qualifications are concerned I am a
steamboat fireman.” It didn’t occur to him that
furnace stokers are not supposed to have any “ sea
qualifications.”
Rapiport, J.P., must have a full allowance
of Hebrew cleverness. No blind and impecunious
Gentile has yet seen his way to become a magis-
trate, a Melb. city councillor, and a leading
member of Melb. Hospital committee. The chief
wire-puller in the Hospital staff elections and
Council elections was blind Rappy, of whose
tactics some amusing tales are told. But Rappy’s
vanity—or, perhaps, it was business need of
notoriety—outran his discretion. He posed as a
secret power until his name became a by-word for
jobbery.
Commencement Day at Melb. Uni. this
year saw the admission as B.A. of its first Chinese
graduate in young James Cheong, a former dux of
the C.E. Grammar School. Young Cheong
matriculated in ’1)0 with the exhibition and first
in the first-class honors in Greek and Latin. Two
other Melo. Uni. students have been full-blooded
Chinese, viz., missionary Cheok Hong Cheong,
(father of-James above) who matriculated in ’75,
and Ling Ah Mouy, who joined in ’B7. Neither
took a degree, but Ling Ah Mouy became Melb’s
first Chinese solicitor.

Now that G. H. Reid lias acquired some
permanency, now that he has performed the or-
thodox rites, as it were, of Australian Premiership
having hanged a murderer, attended Scotch and
Irish banquets, won two general elections, and
sent a message of personal attachment to the
Throne and Person, and an offer of aid to the dear
old mother country —sculptors are turning their
attention to his potentialities as a statue. Some
difficulty stands in the way of giving plastic effect
to his eye-glass, such a thing being unknown both
in ancient and modern art. Others believe that a
bust is preferable to a full-length, for obvious
reasons. The Bulletin suggests that a “com-
posite ” of Reid's head and ex-Mayor Manning’s
legs would be a thing of beauty and a joy for
ever.
The death of M. Marius sets The Bulletin
thinking again of his one-time fiery passion for
Florence St. John, and of how the fire burned out
when Mrs. Grundy ceased to snort at it. He
wooed his Florence (then the wife of one St.
James) during the first production of “Madame
Favart,” at the Strand Theatre. There was no
make-believe about their stage love-passages.
Marius (as Favart) used to pop up through a trap-
door and hug Florence (as Madame F.) until the
audience turned pale from suppressed excitement,
and the lady melted in his embrace. It was clear
to the meanest understanding that both parties
were quite in earnest. A divorce-case, St. James
v. St. James and Marius, happened a while after
this, and the illicit lovers became Aian and wife.
Then Mr. and Mrs. Marius went into management
for themselves at the Avenue Theatre. Their first
venture was a revival of the old success, “ Madame
Favart.” Again did the French comedian pop up
through the floor to fondle Florence—but the icy
coldness of it! No ravenous kissing, no responsive
squeezes, no fierce clutching of the hands, no
thrilling effects whatever. The mated pair had
exhausted their stock of passion, and lost even the
power to play at being in love. A sight for gods
to weep over and the apostles of unbridled
“ healthy animalism ” to make a note of.
The other day the personally popular
Editor Gillon, of Wellington (M.L.) Evening
Post, who is head and front of the opposition
to the beneficent course of democratic legisla-
tion in M.L., came to Sydney to undergo a
surgical operation, but the heat-wave forced him
speedily home. Editor Gillon typifies in his own
person almost every era of M.L. journalistic his-
tory. Born in the Isle of Man in Jan., ’42, he
arrived in Otago in ’5l, and contributed to Otago
Witness in his teens. He soon became its re-
porter, was “special ” to the West Coast gold-rush
in ’(5l, and chummed on the Witness with Julius
Vogel, novelist Farjeon, Vincent Pyke, and the
Fenwicks. Parliamentary reporting haseverleen
his speciality: he is a first authority on M.L.
Parliamentary usage and history. He reported
the Provincial Council for the Witness in the
early days ; became Vogel’s chief reporter when
the Otago Daily Times, the second M.L. daily,
was started ; was made Hansard reporter in ’157,
then Clerk of Private Bills; resigned, and man-
aged the Press Association when started in ’72;
edited the Post; managed a rival Press Associa-
tion, and amalgamated it with the old one ; and
came back to edit the Post in ’B4—for good. Edi-
tor Gillon is the doyen of M.L. journalism, and is
a brother of Dr. Gore Gillon, of Sydney.
Jawbone Neild, the N.S.W. M.P. who
lately published a deplorable book of alleged verses
with gilt edges (the book, not the verse, is gilt-
edged), and is now exciting public derision by his
humorlessly-petulant platform attacks on the
reviewers, has hitherto not posed publicly as a
poet, so far as The Bulletin is aware. The
ferociously - moustachioed Mr. Neild is a tall
gentleman, with straight legs, who looks a little
like the late Marshal Lelxeuf in his best
days, and also strongly recalls a certain hand-
some Creole long an officer of the Victorian
J iistice Department. Ileis so well-groomed as to be
the ideal Duke of the London Journal, the dream
of tailors, and the joy of barberdom ; all of which
(in a country where every professional bard wears
seven-shilling boots) is strong prima-facic evi-
dence against his being a poet. Some excellent
legislative work stands at Mr. Neild’s cr< dit
in the N.S.W. Statute-book, and up to a late
date the only offences chargeable against him
were his memorable nine-hour Parliamentary
speech and a long string of hideous puns.
Just now Mr. Neild is in such a state of fury
(or self-advertisement) that on a calm night the
lashing of his tail in Woollahra may be distinctly
heard at Port Macquarie. He actually threatens
the Evening News with a libel-action for in-
stating that the idea of the Aged Sailor in a
poem of his wherein sea-monsters rear and wave
their tails, and wherein occurs the immortal
phrase, “eftsoons a nocent waterspout,” &c., was
inspired by Coleridge’s “Ancient Mariner.’'
Writes Mr. Neild :
But I am more particularly interested in your repetition
of an accusation made by other papers : “ He has evi-
dently dipped into Browning, also into the would-be
comical irreverencies of the modern American humorist.”
And it is also plainly charged that I have stolen from
Mr. E. T. GILLON , the Maoriland
Opposition.
Photo, by Connolly & Co., Wellington.
Coleridge’s “ Ancient Mariner.” In these libels yon, aad
others, have, in one way, unintentionally paid me a com-
pliment. But misrepresentation of this kind is injurious,
and I now say I did not read Coleridge's poem until after
my “Out West,” was in the printer’s hands, aid I have
never read Browning at all, except one short piece, and
that also since my book was in type. Of American rhym-
ing humorists, beyond Bret Harte’s “Heathen Chinee,"
“ The Row on the Stanilaus ” (sic) “ Dod s Flat ” (?
Dow’s), and a jingle about “ Miss Montague and a Big
Emeu,” I have never, to my knowledge, read a line.
Certainly I have never heard the names of some writers I
am accused of copying, and 1 positively never saw their
books. I do not object to criticism, but I do object to
libellous misrepresentation, and there is a method by
which this can be put a stop to—a method I shall not
fail to evoke if necessary, and shall actively resent any
rervetition of the injurious accusations now replied to.—
Yours, &c., J. C. Neild.
The, mental condition of the man who cheerfully
wants to let the world know that he never read
Coleridge’s masterpiece, one of the greatest glories
of English literature, until his old age, is indeed
a magnificent illustration of the bliss of ignoranoe.
The curse of Dean followed Judge Win-
deyer over the Pacific Ocean. On the morning of
Feb. 18th His Honor, who was staying at the
Palace Hotel, awoke and saw (unless he still fol-
lows the Australian judicial custom of never read-
ing any newspaper except The Times, Argus, and
S.M. Herald), in that highly respectable and
wealthy paper, the Examiner, a three-column
interview-dissertation, done in the best American
manner, and illustrated with large portraits of
the inevitable Mrs. Dean and infant, Messrs.
Crick and Meagher, and G. Dean, Esq.,
master mariner. The interview is decorated
with 11 headlines and labelled “The Hang-
ing Judge,” and it is stated in black type
that His Honor’s severity ill capital cases has
made him “the worst hated man in Australia.”
However, the yarn proceeds that he “is not
a savaige-looking man, neither has he a tender ex-
pression. He is a strong man. The most con-
spicuous feature of his strongly-marked face
is his thick and pendulous nether lip be-
tokening strong appetite for material things,
just as the long compressed upper lip betokens
great stubbornness.” The Examiner goes on to
say that “ like most public men His Honor is not
half ho bad-looking as his cartoons would have you
believe,” and that “of course he would be better-
looking if he wore a moustache ; but then it is not
many men who can shave their upper lips with
impunity, and in British communities it is highly
unprofessional for a member of bar or bench to
wear a moustache. Even Judge Windey r’s highly-
unbecoming chin-and-cheek whiskers are a com-
promise ; perhaps he wears them because he does
not like the appearance of his own chin. Yes, it
is a strong face, and a shrewd face, too. It is the
face of one who would be a good fellow in his own
set, and that is what Judge Windeyer is. For the
rest, Australia’s hanging Judge is of medium
height, rather short in the legs, and heavily built,
with astrong tendency to fleshinessinthealiinentary
regions.” When it was suggested bythe interviewer
that the Judge was going to England in quest of a
Privy - Councillorship, lie “preserved a discreet
silence and dismissed the subject with a wave of
his rather fleshy hand as he stood in the hotel
lobby ; in fact, there was no subject on which he
cared to talk at any great length. He was in a
hurry to sally forth and see Chinatown, but
liis heavy face sparkled for a moment when
the Dean-ease was mentioned.” After briefly
outlining the incidents of the case, the Judge said,
“ ‘ Well, it’s all over now, and I am satisfied ; I am
going to take a rest.’ He smiled largely as he
spoke, shrugged his shoulders, and walked into
the street.” The Examiner then goes into the case
and concludes with “ No wonder .Judge Windeyer
wants a rest.” The S.F. Chronicle also had an
interview, which this paper has not yet seen.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Property may not be robbery, but the pro-
perty-vote is robbery.
The S.A. Anglican clergy have been hold-
ing meetings instructing the women electors how
to vote.
J.D. : Tasmania has free postage for news-
papers. Both dailies have representatives in Par-
liament.
The Imperial Govt, has at last assented to
the S. A. Act reducing the Governor’s salary from
<£sooo to £ 1000.
Candidates for the approaching S.A. elec-
tions head their addresses, “ To the electresses and
electors of—Coannaville.”
Mrs. Nicholls, the brainy head of the S.A.
new women, probably has more influence in S.A.
just now than Premier Kingston himself. She is
the wife of an Adelaide clerk.
Fegan, M.L. A., speaking on the threatened
coal-strike at Newcastle, N.S.W., trots out the
old gag—“ British fair play.” What s the matter
with Australian fair-play ? There must be some-
thing.
During the first three months of ’96 the
imports at Sydney were =£<892,000 more than during
the first quarter of ’95. How much less local em-
ployment does this represent ? Also, where is the
money to pay the bill ?
Despite Tory howls at the strong R.C.
element in his Ministry, S.A. Premier Kingston
announces that he would rather resign than en-
danger the system of free, compulso** mid secular
teaching in State schools.
For a pure, un-
adulterated fool the
temperanoe faddist
is easily first. Up
in Bananaland, if a
candidate for Par-
liament won’t sup-
port Prohibition the
teetotal organisa-
tions oppose him
tooth and nail. Now,
Labor candidates
are nearly all be-
lievers in the Goth-
enburg system, and
consequently not
prohibitionists.
This they say open-

water man rushes to
the Boodlewraithian
candidate, who takes
him to his bosom,
promises to sign the
pledge and shut up
all the pubs., and so
wins a vote. Hence
the curious specta-
cle of high officials
in the ranks of Tem-
perance supiiorting
the party which is
backed up by every
publican ana beer-

try.
“It is the in-
tention of the pre-
sent N.S.W. Gov-
ernment to prorogue
at the end of Nov.
in each year, and
not meet again until
the middle of April.
This will give the
cool months to the
deliberations of pub-

leave the festive sea-
sons and hot wea
ther to hon. mem-

homes or attending

attends to his own

weather may, in his
old age, manage to
lceepout of the jioor-
house; but the busi-
ness or professional
man who serves the
public when it’s cool, and only looks after himself
in fly-time, will have his books and his household
pots and pans sold over his head long before he
has become bald and shed his teeth. Work for
yourself in the cool time, young man.
Queensland Govt, recently ukased that all
walk-about kanakas who had been more than a
month unemployed should be compelled either to
return to the islands or re-engage. Tommy Tanna
may be a savage, but he is speedily waxing a very
keen one. Interviewing Caulfield, Polynesian in-
spector, Tommy wanted to know why white men
of the “ walk-about ” type were not compelled to
re-engage. A poser, truly ! Also —“ I go —ask
for job. Boss he say, 1 Suppose you work—l give
ss. a week.’ What must boy do? ” The inspector
then, in a feeble voice, assured the inquisitive
islander he would “ see justice done in the matter
of wages,” and the meeting broke up. Talk about
sumptuary legislation !
No reform of the J.P.-system is complete
which does not provide for appeals from decisions
of justices on questions of fact as well as of law.
It is here that the unscrupulous or spiteful justice
at present reigns unchecked.
There are now two Phillins in the N.S.W.
Legislative Assembly. One a Hebrew, the other
a Roman Catholic. By the return of Mr. Phillips
for Cowra, the Legislative Assembly has increased
its number of lawyers by one.
Bents of Western (N.S.W.) squattages
have, on the average, been brought down 50 per
cent, on reappraisement under the ’95 Act. Few
holdings west of the Darling will now average over
per acre per annum ; a few in the extreme
north-west are under a farthing.
The number of persons gaoled for drunk-
enness in Maoriland in 1885 was 1200, and since
then it has steadily declined until it has get down
to less than 600. The total number of arrests for
drunkenness in 1890 was 5677. In 1894 it was
only 4465.
Though the usury laws are repealed, the
courts often upset transactions between money-
lenders and “reversioners ” in cases where grossly
exorbitant interest is charged. Why not extend
this rich-man’s-son’s privilege to the general body
of the community ?
The Sydney Ministerial organ was quite
sure that Freetrade-candidate Lainond would
easily win at Cowra, but the Protectionist attorney
Phillips boat him by about 300 in 1200 vote 3
polled. The Freetrade dailies make no comment,
but content themselves with baldly recording the
result.
Drink statistics show that, in the last 60
years, the number of prosecutions for drunkenness
in Australia has fallen from one case per annum
for every 40 of population to one case for every
216. Also, whereas the ratio of publicans to popu-
lation was, in 1831, 4T per 1000, it is now only 2'3
per 1000.
In Maoriland lately two defaulting post-
masters were convicted and sentenced within a few
days of each other. The one who stole <£loo was
let out under the First Offenders Act, and the one
who stole £5lO got two years’ hard. Also, about
the same time, a solicitor who went insolvent with
a shortage of som. wh.ere between <£50,000 and a
quarter of a million in the trust funds, was ac-
quitted altogether, as having no fraudulent intent.
A CONFIRMED VICE.
CONSTANT READER : “ Hello! I see you've got Parkes in again this week.
Why don't you give him a rest; he's done, isn't he?”
OOR ARTIST : “ Oh, yes, he may be ‘ done ’; but, you see, Parkes has become a
sort of vicious habit with me. I acquired it when a child, and I'm too
old to leave it off now. It's far worse than drink.”
Under the N.S.W. Landaninkum - tax,
Broken Hill Proprietary mine, with its £500,000 or
more of annual profit, came within an ace of
getting off practically scot free. The way
the Act was drafted, it is alleged that if
the B.H. Proprietary had been freehold pro-
perty it would have had to pay only a land-tax on
the unimproved surface value of its patch of
hungry desert—probably about Bs. 4d. per annum.
The ground being leasehold it comes under the
income-tax provisions. But it was a narrow
escape, and the people who drew up the slipshod
Act have a lot to be thankful for.
Twenty-five linotype machines are under
order for Melb. Age. Some months ago Ihe
Bulletin pointed out a passage from an A . <
leader in which Li Hung Chang was commended
for not introducing railways in China, because to
do so would mean the throwing of many men out
of work ; and it was pointed out that, by the same
argument, I). Syrne should never introduce ie
linotype.
