This (Saturday, April 11) is nomination
day for S.A. general elections. It is considered
likely that all 10 Labor members who sat in last
Assembly will get back again, though Wood, at
North Adelaide, will be much squeezed. Women
relatives of all the candidates are canvassing hard
among their sister voters.
Brisbane Courier before the election :
“ Every elector of Brisbane knows perfectly by
this time that the defeat of Mr. Byrnes and Mr.
Kingsbury at the polling-booths will be a triumph
to the Labor party.” And now the return of
Fraser and Macdonald Paterson is trumpeted
forth as a Ministerial victory.
There is friction in Adelaide Hibernian
circles because the R.C. Archbishop insisted upon
taking half the proceeds of the late St. Patrick’s
demonstration to pay church debts, instead of, as
usual, allowing all the money to be sent to Ireland
to help the National League. Next year the
bishop hopes to annex the lot.
The S.A. churches are hopelessly divided
re the forthcoming plebiscite on the education
question. Anglicans, Catholics, and Lutherans
want a capitation grant for their own schools, but
all the other denominations oppose it. Some dis-
senters want Scripture-reading in school-hours,
but many prefer the Act as it is.
Asked by Ballarat Benevolent Asylum for
an increased grant, Premier Turner said the
revenue was falling, but steps were necessary to
provide for the poor. Parliament would have to
increase the income-tax, or to cause a municipal-
rate to be struck. Personally he favored the latter
course. The poor-rate is coming.
Australian Federation has been 15 years a
question of practical politics. It existed years
before in weekly newspaper and magazine articles,
and the forecasts of political and debating-school
leaders, but its first serious discussion in N.S.W.
Parliament was in 1880. In ’B3 the Federal
Council Bill was first submitted.
Much friction has been caused in inner
N.S.W. Protectionist circles owing to the reten-
tion of John Norton as secretary to the Central
Committee. After his intervention as a witness
for the Crown in the recent trial, the bulk of the
party regard Norton’s official connection as a
standing “red rag” to the infuriated though in-
dispensable Crick. Considering the poor debating
talent generally on that side of the House, the
party can ill afford to drive its brightest debater
and ablest Parliamentarian into a political cave.
A difference of practice with respect to the
necessity of renewing bail in ordinary day-to-day
adjournments in criminal oases causes confusion in
Melb. Criminal Court. The C.J. thinks special
applications unnecessary, but the gaol officials
won’t let accused go until the application is made
and granted, on account of the strict view other
judges take. Probably it will not be settled until
some speculative bailed individual neglects to
apply, is detained, gets a habeas corpus, and sues
a governor or two for false imprisonment, assault
and battery.
Saturday, April 25, is polling-day for S.A.
Assembly elections, which should be the stormiest
on record. The greatest interest centres m the
city and suburban polling, where the Ass and
Labor parties are running candidates in every
instance. The issue depends upon the
woman vote. Ever since the W.C.T.U. decided
to vote for “ moral ” men, candidates have been
damning each other’s characters, and the New
Woman has consequently got so mixed that it
is not unlikely she will at the last moment plump
for the candidate with two wives and a harem.
One probable result of Bananaland’s anti-
“ walk-about” legislation will be that larg*
numbers of kanakas will return to the islands.
They detest plantation-work, but love casual jobB-
“ one pound one week, allee same white fellow.” A
leading Polynesian threatened to go the other day
and take 400 boys along with him if forced to r«
engage.
The Govt, platform for S.A. elections, a«
outlined by Premier Kingston, includes the refer
endum and elective Ministries ; reduction of Leg.
Council qualification from =£2s to =£ls; increase of
Income-tax exemption from £l2O to <£lso; the
Employers’ Liability Bill—again ; possibly State
insurance ; and a number of more shadowy propo-
sals —all in the direction of reform.
In the Q. election, much priestly influence
was used on behalf of Byrnes and Boodlewraith.
At one of the chief southern Q. towns the parish
priest, a real force in local politics, slanged his
flock to vote against the democratic candidates in
the fashion of “ th’ ould sod.” His reverence, by
the way, was rank Labor last election. This tim«
a gentle hint went round from headquarters.
/ The N.S.W. Government has approved o
an arrangement whereby produce coming from the
country districts to Sydney is to be carried on the.
railways at a reduction of between 20 and 50 per
cent, on former rates. Produce carried from
Sydney inland pays the old rates, so, in an under-
hand fashion, the local producer is to be protected
against the foreigner at the expense of the commu-
nity—which has to make up the railway deficiency
by taxation —and the doctrine of absolute free-
trade and free competition is burst up once more.
There is no difference between handicapping the
importer by extra railway freights and handicap-
ping him by Customs duties; and to artificially
foster the agricultural industry is quite as im-
moral, on Freetrade principles, as to artificially
foster manufactures.
Pariss Nesbit, on Drink and Smoke and
Women :
Repressive legislation was not the way to cure drunken-
ness. A hundred and twenty vears ago no gentleman
went to lied without being drunk. (“Gentleman?”)
Yes, they generally went to bed drunk three nights a
week. (Laughter.) Was that so now? (“No.") Why?
Because of the greater complexity and variety of color
and interest in modern life. That was why the middle
classes did not get so drunk. That was why it was a dis-
grace now, when it used to be a glory. They drank then
because they had nothing better to do.' (Laughter.)
They did not know how to pass the time. What was
wanted to cure drunkenness was not repressive legislation,
imt brighter lives and happier conditions for our poorer
classes. (Applause.) Would they leave it to a lot o<
women to say whether they should have a glass of beer on
Sunday or not? Had they seen the published platform of
the W.C.T.U. ? No tobacco, no beer, no wine. And they
wanted to raise the age of consent to 9!) years. (Loud
laughter.) That was too old. (Laughter.) Ninety-nine
years and no beer. (Renewed laughter.) He said to Dr.
Magarey recently, “ It seems to me you want to prevent
me having a glass of wine in my own house.” He re-
plied, “ Yes, that is just what we want to do.” (“ Rot.”)
Yes; rot. That policy would uproot all the vines in
Bar ossa and make it a howling wilderness. (“ No.”)
They said so. (“ No ” and hear, hear.) And they wanted
them to do without their pipes. (.Laughter.) He was
reminded of Rudvard Kipling’s verse
“ A woman is oii'y a woman,
But a good cigar is a smoke.”
(Daughter.) This (holding up his pipe) was the only rival
a woman really had, because she could not scratch its
eves out. (Loud laughter.) Kingston did not smoke nor
drink, because it made him sick-(laughter)—and there-
fore was a patron saint, holy and chaste, of the W.C.T.U
and Social Impurity Society.

At Poverty Point .
The show run by Percy St. John struck Hasting(M.T,)
at race-time, and announced a leg-show. When the gaff
opened the hall was fairly filled with trainers, jockeys
touts, spielers, and Maoris, one of which latter yelled out
in his lingo that the legs were no good. They were just
like those of the poukako (swamp-hen, noted for havin"
long, thin legs and a slender body). He wound up by
yelling out “ Kapai Maori wahine ” (very good the Maori
girl!) paying a just tribute to his countrywomen’s well-
developed limbs. The house noisily concurred.
The relative sizes of things, as they appear in Ararat.
B. and B.’s manager struck that sad Victorian town some
years ago when he was taking Frank Thornton around,
and went to the best furniture man to hire his very best
suite of furniture for the drawing-room scene in “Charley’s
Aunt.” The furniture man was cold and unresponsive.
“ No,” he said, “ I wouldn’t hire that furniture to any
theatrical co.” Then the manager smiled a superior smile.
“This isn’t a common travelling c 0.,” he explained. “I
represent Brough and Boucicault, and they should be
good enough to satisfy you.” “ No,” said the furniture
man decisively, “ I won’t lend that furniture to Brough
and Boucicault. It’s no use talking. I wouldn’t even
lend it to Dan Barry 1 ” Then there was a deafening
pause.
Maud Hobson is interviewed in the current number of
To-day. About the only thing she says is that she “ does
not care much about America, and Australia is dull.”
That’s not quite it. Maud herself is —er not dull
enough.
Miss Nellie Stewart’s failure to enfever the Americans
is discounted by the fact that she got much nearer that
goal than her predecessor, the Gaiety favorite, Ellaline
Terriss.
Of an actor well-known in Australia this wheeze is
told. He is a combination of Adonis and Lothario, and is
nightly in receipt of letters written on highly-scented
pink paper. A friend was in his dressing-room recently
when the call-boy brought round a letter. The actor
opened and read. “ Poor little woman,” he muttered,
tearing the paper and throwing the pieces on the floor.
The friend, however, had caught sight of the opening
lines : “ Dear Sir, —Unless your washing account is settled
by”
Writes Percy Abbott from Pietermaritzburg, Natal,
under date 28th Feb. : —Dear Bulletin, —We have almost
finished with Africa. For my opinion of it, see Snazelle.
If you have an enemy in the show biz. advise him to come
here. Fillis’s returns published in the papers say he took
1:11,561 —and exes. 1:15,679—in Capetown in about five
montns. The Gaiety Co. in Johannesburg made money,
so did Arnold and Sir Chas. Halle. Holloway is freezing.
Miss Fortescue froze. So did Camilla Urso and countless
others. Hellers are now do’ng well, but at first it was up-
hill work. In Cape Colony the Dutch go to bed at 7 p.m.,
and even when they stay up they don't want shows.
Natal and the O.F. State are better, but the distances are
long and exes, very heavy. One agent got into Pretoria
without cash, and while waiting for a remittance was
hiked before the land-rost, who took all his jewellery from
him, and told him if he did not pay his hotel bill (£1) in
two days he would be sent to gaol. He walked 47 miles
to Johannesburg, got his remittance, and when he re-
turned to pay his bill and get his watch, &c., he was fined
10s. How would some Australian pros, fare in such a
country?— P. Abbott.
Mrs. Gown Trotter, who has just accepted an engage-
ment of four months from George Musgrove, has ordered
a most wonderful collection of gowns from Worth.
Although Melba got the worse of her encounter with
Yvette Guilbert, she has scored heavily in New York, and,
at the conclusion of the last operatic season there, she
was ovated even to her heart’s content. She was recalled
time after time to receive the acclamations of the audi-
ence. They demanded still another song, and, to please
them, she sang “Home, Sweet Home” (they a’ do it),
Jean de Reszke playing the accompaniment. Then she
was presented with a diamond tiara. Did the Diva in
her wildest dreams ever look forward to her present
triumph ?
The programme of Plain Bill Holloway’s show at
Johannesburg is characteristic of that modest performer.
A list of names of artistes appears on the back page.
Holloway’s name heads the procession, then come Annie
Mayor and other ladies of the co., followed by the rest of
the men. This arrangement marks a wide distinction
between Plain Bill and the common or garden variety of
male actor.
Miss Maud Williamson wishes it to be known that she
is not suffering from any throat-affection, as rumored, and
would much like to know who started the report.
Some dramatic piracy-cases have arisen in Australia
through the importation of contraband “ scrip.” In Eng-
land and America there are persons whose profession it is
to secure shorthand reports of plays, and have them
transcribed for sale abroad. In New York you can buy
the “ scrip ” of any new i>lav for 30 or 40dol. within a few
weeks of its first production’on either side of the Atlantic.

headed his little bill to the capital B’s.

got his part in the play. Twenty years ago he would have
made a pretty good fist at Svengali, for it is hard to say
what he could not act.

Boucicault attributes Australia’s failure to produce
good actors up to the present to three reasons :—(1)
Accent; (2) limited theatrical scope : (3) lack of oppor-
tunities for a thorough grounding in the art of acting.
He is a great supporter of a State-aided school of drama
attached to the Universities, just as music and painting
are now aided by means of conservatoriums. “I think
Australia is going to be the great voice-producing country
of the future;” and “Dot” adds that when he was in
London he was struck with the great inferiority of the
comic opera choruses there as compared with “the
firm s ” warblers. The latter’s voices are far and away
more natural, purer, and fresher in tone, he says.
Nat. Goodwin, ’Murkan comedian and pout prandial
raconteur, comes to this country in July next with his
co. of 16 under engagement with “the firm” for 12 weeks.
Courtneidge made his first appearance on the stage at
the Prince’s Theatre, Manchester, about 19 years ago. He
was then a super., in receipt of 9s. a week. Last month,
he appeared again at the s line theatre—one of the finest
in England—in the capacity of manager for the syndi-
cated lessees, so now this amiable potentate can hold him-
self up to the bibulous mummer with the red nose as an
example of what “ ability, perseverance, and strict
sobriety ” can accomplish. But friend Courtneidge is
not the man to make speeches concerning his start in
life with the traditional Is. fid. in pocket.
Irving’s knighthood is expressed in the advts. by the
omission of the prefix “Mr.” Thus: “Lyceum. Under
the management of Mr. Oscar Barrett. Lessee, Henry
Irving.”
Gus. Elen’s new song, “ It’s a great big shame,” pro-
mises (writes a Bulletin correspondent) to rival in popu-
larity his famous “’E dunno where ’e are,” which made
him what he is— i.e., the highest-paid coster-delineator in
England. Chevalier, now ruined by the “ Trocadero
Hall” venture, is completely outshone by the new man.
Elen’s voice is very reminiscent of Robert Br ough, as the
Lord High Chancellor in “lolanthe.”
Amurkan stage training—on the authority of manager
and trainer Belasco :—I took Mrs. Carter in hand and
started her with the body exercise, to limber the ankles,
knees, wrists, and all the joints. Then I instructed her
in the rough exercise, showing her how to fall down,
without hurting face or body, on a mattress, on rugs, on
the bare floor forward, sideways, backward. Finally,
after courses of voice-cultivation, chinning, emotions and
passions, and Shakespeare, I would struggle with her
around the room, slap her in the face, dash her against
the wall, throw her over apd stamp on her. That ended
her training. . " .
A sword-swallower at Westminster Aquarium varies his
performance by swallowing a gold watch, which can be
distinctly heard ticking inside.
The young Duke of Manchester (grandson of the lady
who eventually married Lord Hartington) has the stage-
fever almost as badly as our own adopted johnnie, the
Earl of Yarmouth. The Duke, however, is more modest
than Yarmouth, thusfar. He plays small parts only, and
engages a clever actress to stage-manage his amateur
operatic shows.

The serio-comic is never seriously comic, and seldom

cart, who, as far back as 1850, played under Charles Kean,
and was for 16 years stage-manager to John Hare.

Chirgwin, the white-eyed musical Kaffir, was in his
younger days a common or garden kind of all nigg'er,
who did a song and dance with his brother. One day his
brother fell sick, or quarrelled and parted company with
him, and then he reckoned his occupation gone. But the
manager of the hall where the brothers had appeared, not
carin<>- to lose a “ turn,” bounced Chirgwin into trying to
be funny single-handed. The lone, lorn creature painted
a white‘diamond round his eye, went upon the stage, and
was emboldened by the laughter of the audience to make
a great, original success, as nigger business goes. The
white eye was an inspiration.

A remarkably clever act is now being performed at the
London Aquarium. A lady artist fixes a loaded rifle on
one side of the stage, goes to the other, places an egg-shell
on her head, and fires with a second rifle at the trigger of
the first. In nearly every case the bullet from the fixed
weapon smashes the eggshell.

A Paderewski performance is described in a recent S.F.
Argonaut. The great pianist’s face “is one of the memor-
able faces of this epoch-poetic, worn, of a spiritual
beautv. The eves are full of an inexpressible, suffused
pain, like the eyes of a brooding eagle.” His performance
is abo-.e all, electric. “The piano, under his assault,
<rave forth the tones of an orchestra, and tremendous
harmonies, bizarre as the dance of the witches on the
Brocken, shook the audience from their languid appreci-
ation like a hand on the backs of their necks. . . The
instrument gave forth strange, unearthly sounds. The
thunderous opening chords clanged like the iron grating
of huge, reluctant gates grinding on their hin-es. . .
The thunders crashed away, shot through with gleams of
lifht little tingling shivers of notes that skimmed
across the piano ltke gleams of small, silvery fish,
as they twinkle by in the murk of streams.
Then hurrvinu- up in accumulating undulations, crowding
closer and' came floods of crystalline notes, runs
and ripples of them trickling through, like water over
stones, or sunshine through leaves, or bird-notes in the
silence of a breathless noon —anything that is clear and
quick and melodiously rapid. Storms of sound swept up
a.'ain and obliterated them ; tempests crashed over them.
Now and then they flashed out for a moment, sparkling
tricksilv and tremulously, like water-reflections in the
s in or small flames ; but the martial march of the bass
chords came up and up, clashing and clanging their vic-
tory sweeping the piano with their barbaric clamor, till
the audience for a moment forgot their manners, and ap-
plauded like live men and women who have heard a great
and wonderful thing.”

ACTING.
“ What did she wear ?”—Modern conversation.
She was mad to go play-acting,
And she went upon the stage;
But in art that’s so exacting
She’d no notion to engage.
So she bought a hundred dresses—
For it is unwritten law
That Worth’s the best of actors
And the only one to draw.
So she made these lines her motto,
To be followed not by halves—
“ While dress and undress fill the house,
The one who studies starves.”
W.A.S.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The old maxim “ Always back a good horse,”
was once again exemplified in the A.J.C. Doncaster mile.
The top weight, Courallie, though previously unlucky in
l>ig events, made a one-horse race of it, nothing being
able to match his pace in the home stretch. Albicore,
Patrona and Response were the favorite starting-price
picks, but the last-named only just secured a place. The
run was but (see. slower than the 1.40 record held by
Paris and Delaware.
* * • *
A peculiarity of the Doncaster was that the
ring unconsciously gave the public the straight tip. For
over a week before the race Courallie was a hot favorite,
and reigned so up to Friday night. The papers reported
thousands as having been laid against him, and the
public, thinking the stable had a good thing, stepped in
and took the odds straight out and in doubles. As a
matter of fact, the owner had taken none of the thou-
san Is, the horse having been forced into the market to
stimulate betting. On the day Courallie went back to 14
to 1, and the stable got its little bit. When the son of
Gemma di Vergy romped home Ikey Mo was consternated.
A little bit of inner turf history.
Courallie’s Doncaster win makes out Maluma,
who donkey-licked him in the Newmarket, to be a really
fine filly. She is at present suffering dental troubles, and
tw’o more of Redfearn’s are in Queer-street. Luck comes
in lumps.
» * * *
Though Courallie’s Doncaster win with 9.3 was
good, it doesn’t approach Marvel’s. The black demon’s
defeat of St. Blaize and 34 others with 10.4 up will never
be forgotten by those who saw it.
» * * «
Instead of an exercise-canter, A.J.C. St. Leger
proved a downright hard race for Wallace. Cabin Boy’3
ground-thieving tactics troubled the chestnut as at
Fleinington, and he only managed to score an impossible
win in the last couple of strides through sheer gameness ;
walloped from the turn he never shirked his gruel, and
finished in his characteristic plugging fashion.
» * '» *
There’s no such thing as a moral in racing.
Backers laid odds on Fucile in the Maiden, and nearly
fell down dead when a Nordenfeldt youngster, Snapshot,
almost upset the good thing on the post. Odds layers on
Wallace, too, looked glum until Cabin Boy was beaten on
the very threshold of victory.
» * * *
Response’s third in the Doncaster must have
been tantalising to her connections. She is one of the
sort that would just miss winning in either Melb. Cup or
a pony race with equal facility. If Richmond’s daughter
ever does win a race it will probabl v be on a protest.
* * • *
There wasn’t the toss of a coin between Dela-
ware, Valiant,and Aemena in the Autumn Stakes. Strange
to sa3% all the jocks appeared to have the same instruc-
tions. Even Delaware, when leading, was ridden a waiting
race. The last four furlongs was consequently a clinker ;
Delaware held his own, and that was all. Valiant and
Aemena were bang up alongside, and Toreador and The
Harvester handy.
* * * *
When Wallace just pipped Osculator in Vic.
Derby it was said he would have won in a canter had the
pace been sound. Yet, in every race since the pace has
been uncomfortably hot, and he has just held his own.
♦ » » »
Wallace carried the public through in Sydney
Cup, and the closing scene was reminiscent of Carbine's
win in the same event. The hilarious crowd was averse
to the colt’s defeat, not only because of its sincere liking
for the straightgoing and liberal sportsman for whom he
ran, but because he was the son of his sleepy-eyed, lazy-
going, sterling old dad, who had so often pulled them out
of trouble. Trentham’s pace-making was a prominent
feature, and it was only when near home that. Callinan
hinted that time was up by looking round. Wallace,
valiantly ridden by Gough, was able to come, but blocked-
in Toreador wasn’t until it was too late. Then he finished
perhaps faster than the winner. Wallace’s win was cer-
tainly most meritorious, but he ought to divide honors
with his stable mate, who ran third.
Newhaven, the odds-on soft snap for the
Champagne, met his Waterloo in Coil, who landed a 7 tol
chance easily, and avenged his Vic. Ascot Vale Stakes
defeat with interest. No one dreamt, either, of the un-
penalised Gozoozar beating Newhaven for places. There
may not be much difference between Coil and Newhaven
on past performances, but the writer believes Cobbity’s
brother will prove far and away the best three-year-old.
W.f.a. running isn’t a reliable guide for handi-
cap events. Delaware, Valiant, and Aemena, the Autumn
Stakes trio, were completely out of Sydney Cup. Torea-
dor, unplaced, ran second.
Wallace’s 3min. 31sec. in the Cup was only put
up once previously—bv Carbine as a three-year-old. But
Monday’s hero had only a stable companion, and not a
Melos and Abercorn, to beat.
Bob Ray unexpectedly beat Wallace in the
Champagne last year, and the Randwick-trained Coil fol-
lowed his footsteps on Monday in respect to the red-hot
Newhaven.
Trentham, third in Sydney Cup, was one of the
best-bred in the field. His half-brother, Lochiel, didn’t
develop into a stayer until a year or so after he came
across from M.L. and won the Newmarket. He won N.Z.
Cup after a number of tries, and then beat Australia's best.
As the combination books had Courallie and
Wallace scribbled all over them, besides being heavily laid
individually, Iky Mo’s Easter harvest will be robbed of
much of its richness.
During the last 37 years, Australian Peer and
Wallace have been the only two Vic. Derby winners who
annexed Sydney Cup same season.
The easy win of Cydnus in the First Nursery
indicates at least one strong Derby colt doing battle for
St. Alban’s in the Spring.
Chesterman was one of the last home in Sydney
Cup. He’d do better in another sphere, pulling some-
thing behind him —say a hansom.
Courallie carried 101 b. more in the Flying than
he did in the Newmarket, and won in 1.15. !
During the Cup finish Cook, on Toreador, yelled
to the beaten crowd to give him a chance, and one rider
replied in a most aggravating fashion: “ Garn, you can’t
win 1”
• • • * (
Now that Paris has got into form, he is very
likelv to win one or both of his important engagements
for the Great Metropolitan Stakes (at Epsom) and the
Chester Cup. The circular course at Chester ought to
suit the handy little horse to a nicety, and, although the
distance (21 miles) seems a little bit too long for him on
paper, it is less trying, than the same length of course at
Newmarket. However, Paris’s victory in the Northamp-
tonshire Stakes, a race of only 1£ mile, or thereabouts,
doesn’t prove that he could have come with a rattle at
the end of an extra f-mile. In Australia, he used to be
“ all out” before the finish of the Melbourne Cup. True,
Paris humped 9st. 81b. into the front rank when Glenloth
won, but on that day the luck of getting a good position
at the start made him sure of beat ing about 20 horses who
were flounderin''' in the mud behind him all the time.
Fortunately, he Is quite at home on soft-going, of which
there is pienty to be had through an average English
season.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Agricultural Show-people seem
sadlv in want of a trotting-expert on their committee.
For'instanoe, the alleged trotting-exhibition prize, judged
or speed, style, and action, was given to Tuxedo, whom
the writer holds to be a pacer. The event was advertised
as a trotting-exhibition, no mention being made of pacing,
and the writer feels sure that the owner of Era could
legally demand first prize. To test trotters for speed on a
quarter-mile track is a farce. Tuxedo, a galloway, is a
splendid animal, but his size (14.2) and gait, pacing,
enable him to fly round a track upon which such upstand-
ing square-trotters as Tommy and Era would cut them-
selves to bits. Alec. White, behind the majestic Tommy,
would not risk injury to his charge, but simply jogged
him round the track, whilst Mr. Bartlett was foolish
enough to let Era out for a round, with the result that he
cut himself badly about the elbow. If the competition is
for trotters and pacers , and for speed alone, it should be
so stated.
The trotting in saddle was a sorry business, the
competition resting between Tommy S. and First Tem-
pest. Tommy S. trotted squarely, and for pace beat
Tempest, who trotted with his head drawn pretty well
round to his rider’s knee. The pair was then called back,
and First Tempest was given a big start for a mile go,
when Tommy S. again beat him. Why Tommy S. should
be asked to concede another horse a start in a best-and-
best competition caused much comment.
* * * *
A feature of the jumping-contests at Sydney
Show was the fine form displayed by the veteran cham-
pion, Fairfield. Though a big crowd was saddled for
Tattersall’s Hunter’s Plate it was a foregone conclusion
from the start, the chestnut jumping in faultless style.
* » * »
Judging thoroughbreds at shows is an uneviable
task. Experts differed widely at the Show in the stallion
class. St. Blaize, Correze, Matador, Sir William, Manton
and Co. all had their admirers It was a toss-up, but
Correze got the judges’ verdict. The Musket horse,
Manton, awarded second, seemed to have equal claims
with any of the others for first.

* • • •
A layer and a backer were run in at a Perth
(W.A.) rural meeting, though everybody else was betting
all day. Bail was refused from prominent men until late
at night. Next day they were fined a couple of pounds
each. Under W.A.’s precious law if they again offend
they are liable to 12 months “ vag.”

race-books and issued them gratis, as a species of revenge
for exclusion from the course. The sleepy-headed club
hasn't even the gumption to copyright its weight-lists.

• • * •
The mystery of the Victorian turf to some
people is Sam Cook. Nobody ever called Samuel a bloated
capitalist—within Tiib Bulletin’s hearing-yet he trains
and races, as his own, a perennial stud of horses with
uncommonly poor results. He never seems to feel hurt
by a run of very bad luck. Whether he wins or loses it’s
much about the same to the imperturable Sam.

“ Baulky horses are best cured,” writes an
authority, “by diverting their attention. Go up to the
baulker’s head, stroke him a litt.e, and suddenly stick a
pin through one ear and leave it there. Gee-gee thinks
a tty is biting him on that tender point, tries to shake the
offender off, and can't. At this gee-gee wonders much,
forgets all about his baulking, devotes all his intellect to
trying to shift that ‘ fly’ pin, and goes like a lamb.”
Mr. Matt. O’Shannassy’s idea of fixing an inner
inside rail at the “ home ” turn was put into execution
at Caulfield on Saturday, and see ned to fill a long-felt
want. But it wasn’t tested with a big field of horses. No

one.

At Roinsey (Vic.) the other day, a man named
Keenan lost his life under what may have been heroic cir
cumstanees without getting any glory by the transaction
Keenan rushed upon the racecourse to assist a disabled
jockey before all the horses had passed him. The goon
Samaritan was unluckily knocked down and killed on the
spot. Another sort of racecourse Samaritans are the men
who make it a regular business to stay about the juinjic
at the back of the big steeplechase course and pick up
fallen jockeys—whom they rob of their rings and all other
portables with much expedition.

Melbourne’s own Fitz-Gibbon appears to
have brought the members of the old Yorick Club
to a sense of what was due from them in “ calls.”
A pathetic circular issued to the members by Fitz
aforesaid should give pause to all other old gentle-
men of his class who aspire to glitter in the centre
of a “literary, artistic, and scientific” circle—as
the old Y orick loved to call itself. In its recon-
structed form the club can’t pretend to be much
else than a rendezvous for ex-boomsters and similar
wearers of tall hats. Fitz-Gibbon, some years ago,
“ suffered the vacant position of one of its
three trustees to be thrust upon him.” A
couple of Bank officials shared this literary,
artistic, and scientific distinction with the then
town-clerk, and . they—during Fitz.’s absence in
England— renewed the club’s lease at a high
figure. Fitz., “ relying on the honor of the mem-
bers to meet the liability,” added his signature,
“and so became, conjointly with the other two
trustees, personally liable for fulfilment of the
The Australasian declares there is a
chestnut tree at Mt. Etna over 2000 years old. It
was from this tree, no doubt, that the jokes were
gathered which the Australasian publishes under
the heading “ Miscellany.”

Recent evidence of Smellbourne’s hard-
uppishness appears in the insolvency of Southwell
Coultas’s estate. Coultas was the fashionable
tailor whose profits, during boom-time, must have
run to =£sooo a year, or higher. “Depreciation in
the value of real and leasehold estate ” is the
nominal cause of insolvency, but a steady decline
Unanimous for WARNER’S "SAFE” CURE as being the best, safest, and surest
remedy to use when the Liver is so inactive as to cause Dyspepsia, Jaundice, Sick
Headaches, Nausea, Dizziness, Insomnia, and General Lassitude. For sale by all

covenants.” The club became unable to pay
its debts, the other trustees more or less
faded out of sight, seemingly, and Fitz. stood
alone, or almost alone, on the bridge of the
sinking ship, exposed to the volleys of unpaid
bills from truculent tradesmen. Hence his heart-
rending circular. The call made upon town and
country members in 1894 was calculated to rescue
Fitz. from his melancholy situation, but the
number of members who cashed up compared
unfavorably" with the number that didn’t, so he
had no choice between summoning those latter or
getting summoned himself. Legal proceedings
have thus far been avoided by the tardy awakening
of these defaulters, and Fitz., The Bulletin
hop es, will not suffer the “unjust and undeserved
indignity and disgrace of having his name called
in question for debts which he did not contract,
and for a liability which the club induced him to
assume.” The circular recalls to mind G. A.
Sala’s allegation re the Yorick Club. He said it
was composed mainly of a “gang of cads,” mas-
querading in literary and artistic characters. A
club which called itself the Yorick, rented pre-
mises at £6OO a year, and elected two bankers and
a town-clerk as trustees, was, perhaps, a good
deal less libelled by Sala’s angry remark than it
imagined itself to be.

Henri Rochefort : “ Every human being
sees what concerns him through a telescopic glass,
ror oo years the baker, at whose shop my parents
bought bread, went to her counter at 'noon and
never budged from it till eight. Yet she ex-
plamed the presence of gray locks in her hair by
the fact that her life had been tumultuous.”
From Charleville : “The thermometer
averaged, for the five weeks I stayed at Tinten-
burra, 114 deg. in open-air shade. Now the Paroo
is up; black soil ahead (you know what that
means); horses are starving ;no feed between here
and Humeburra—6s miles. This is a cheerful
country ! ”

Poisoned by Phosphorised Pollard.
Messrs. English and Merrigum, of Runny-
mede, have had a number of sheep and cows
poisoned through using phosphorised pollard,
and Mr. English lost forty (40) sheep, including
a valuable ram, whilst Mr. Merrigum lost 11
milch cows. Pigs and poultry were also
poisoned. Station-holders are recommended
to use TOXA, as this preparation-will not
ignite, and stock will not eat it.*

Mr. S. Zollner, of 443 Kent-street, Syd-
ney, exhibited at the Sydney Agri. Show gal-
vanised steel piping which he claims will prove
invaluable by reason of remarkable lightness and
cheapness, for ventilating mine shafts, for irriga-
tion purposes, for flaming, for water-supply—in
short, for everything requiring piping. The Water
and Sewerage Board of Sydney are using Zollner’s
speciality for all the vent shafts of the sewers. So
light and cheap are they that the other day a big
order of pipes despatched to Hillgrove (N.S. W.)
froin Sydney arrived there at a cost for pipes,
carriage, &c., equal to the cost of cast-iron pipes
for carriage alone. The pipes’ diameters vary from
six to fifteen inches ; they stand a pressure of 3001b.
per sq. inch upwards, are not corrosive, will last
from 30 to 40 years; and have stood every test
required of them.*
One of the best things exhibited at Sydney
Agricultural Show was Jeffery’s filter, the Sydney
agent for which is Mr. J. S. Peardon, of Bond-
street. Dr. Gresswell, chairman of the Board of
Public Health, Melbourne, says : “ Out and out
the best filters are Jeffery’s ; I would fit these
filters to every service tap in the community.” J.
W v Springthorpe, M.A., M.D. (Melb.) says :
“ Filter all drinking-water through a Jeffery filter ;
the ordinary filters are useless against germs so
small as the typhoid germ.” The filter is sim-
plicity itself, and is very cheap. Tens and tens of
thousands of people had a drink from Jeffery’s
filters at the show.*
Messrs. John Brush, Son, and Co., who
run the well-known harness emporium at 403
George-street, Sydney, advertise the first sale they
have ever held in their career. This is on account
of the expiry of their lease and the dissolution of
their partnership ; and for one month only a dis-
count of 4s. in the £ will be given on the whole of
their varied stock. People who want harness,
saddlery, travelling trunks, perambulators, go-
carts, sulkies, wagsrons, rocking-horses, boys’
bicycles, bags, and other leather goods, are
advised to grasp this opportunity by the hair and
haul tight.*
The Western Boom.
The West Australian gold-mines are being roughly
criticised in many journals in Australia at°the
present time, and comparisons are drawn between
their output and that of fields established for over
half a century, of course to the disadvantage of
the western colony. No doubt, if the writers of
some of these articles were known, the public would
find that the critical scribe was a disappointed
jobber whose little game had been exposed, and lie
now works off his superfluous spite in a dog-in-the-
manger attempt to stop others from doing what
he could not do. We are happy to say that all
things in West Australia cannot be influenced by
the animosity of a few mediocre pressmen, and
that a good article will obtain public support even-
tually wherever it is introduced. Frossard's
Cavour Cigars have stood their public trial all
over the colonies for two years, and the people are
so satisfied that in many of the colonies it is antici-
pated that the Governments will consider it to
their advantage to considerably reduce the duty
on these popular weeds. Frossard’s Cavour Cigars
are obtainable everywhere in Australia in packets
of 8 for Is.*
Exo’s “ Fruit Salt.”— “ Rosina Cottage, Vent-
nor, Isle of Wight.—Mr. Exo.—Dear Sir,—The
effects in my case are astonishing, as I am 'consti-
tutionally bilious, and am now 52 years of age.
My mother and youngest sister were never cured
of sickness (biliousness seems hereditary), and
quite expected to suffer like them for the rest of
my life. I am now taking my fourth bottle, and 1
was joined in the others by some of my family, so
that I have taken scarcely three bottles. I feel I
ought to make some acknowledgement, so trust
you will excuse this.— Eliza Belling.”’ Sold liv
all Chemists and Stores.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Ehzabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*

The Costliest Memorial Window
in the World
cannot be appreciated except under favorable cir-
cumstances. Looking at it from the outside, em-
bedded in its huge frame, one gets no idea of its
beauties, but merely sees the faint outline obscured
by the deeper darkness within, and overshadowed
by the building in which it rests. But go into the
quaint old church : stand where the sun’s descend-
ing ray is softly sifted through the colored, artisti-
cally enamelled and arranged prisms of glass, and
you then see blended in one grand whole the
beauties of art in a harmony pleasing to the
dullest eye. < .
One of the finest memorial tablets in the world
is that recently erected by M. P. Millar, of “ Glen-
cairn,” Upper William Street, Darlinghurst,
Sydney. A tablet which memorises his thankful-
ness, one to which others may look with confidence
and admiration, and one which will stand the test
of years. The words on this tablet are deeply cut
in moments of direst agony and pain, and every
letter, every character, stands out boldly, carrying
invaluable lessons to us all. The text of this
tablet is “ I can speak in the highest terms of
Warner’s Safe Cure and Pills, which I always
recommend to my friends. When travelling
through the South' Sea Islands I suffered very
severely from my liver and kidneys, and conse-
quently consulted the local doctors, who prescribed
for me but without any good effect. Iliad often
been advised by my friends to try Warner’s Safe
Cure, and as a last resource did so. Upon giving
it a fair trial by taking several bottles I became
perfectly well, and have not had any return of the
complaint since
Poverty pays 40s. in the £.
For the last 30 years there has been a
“private” post office in Harris-street, Pyrmont,
the last of its kind in or near the N.S. W. metro-
polis. It was conducted in a stationer’s shop, and
lias now been closed in consequence of the death
of the “ postmaster.”

Metropolitan clubs should enact that every
Jockey weighed-out should weigh-in also, whether placed
or not. Cronk owners and trainers have a knack of ab-
stracting pieces from the lead-bag when compelled to put
up a straight rider. Disqualification ensues if he wins,
and they get their betted money if the backed pea runs
second. Also bag-tampering fits the bill nicely in case a
jock, is tempted to have a go “on his own.” Owners
should be compelled to pay a stiff fine for light weight, as
backers are.
* * * *
How a backing crowd can be victimised was
shown at Horsham (Vic.) the other day when the narked
owner of a hot cert, put up a healthy-looking fat man at
the last moment, and instead of 619. the neddy carried
12st. 131 b.
* • • »
The watchful “ talent ” waiting for a stable lead
is a feature of small meetings. Accepting short odds is
safer than supporting probable dead meat. A plunging
owner risked £l5O at Randwick the other day, and the
crowd fell over each other to get on to the good thing. It
went down. “ Following the money ” is only safe when
other dangerous stables aren’t trying, and then some
cranky, turned-loose outsider is liable to spoil things.
A recent arrival from India offers to wager that
he there saw a champion Sydney pony long since re-
ported as dead.
» • * *
Joe Thompson was very much to the fore at
Liverpool with his double, treble, and quadruple book,
and did an enormous business, writes The Bulletin's
London correspondent. “ But I’m much afraid that the
‘ Leviathan ’ will not come out very much on the right
3ide when all is over, as some of the picks were very hot
indeed for the quadruple—and Joe laid 10,000 to 1, some-
times thrice over.”
* • » *
Chows are good horse-dealers. Jimmy bought
a moke for £(! down, t(i more due, horse to be returned if
unsuitable. The animal got bogged and died. When the
seller came for the remaining L 6 he was met with “ Hossy
no suitee; you takee; find ’em cleek. No savee six
pong.” And this to a publican.
* * * •
Sydney regatta committee accepted .£35 in
prizes from Lord Brassey. In all likelihood nobody can-
vassed H.E., whose gift seems to have been purely volun-
tary ; but all the same, it doesn’t look over-well to allow a
guest, however rich and distinguished, to play the host
to so considerable an extent.
* » * *
The Martin-Parsons races conclusively proved
(writes a Bulletin correspondent) that Parsons’ superiority
of legs does not make up for his inferiority of head. The
plugging Yank, sees only the dollars ahead from the re-
port of the pistol. The crowd’s cheers have just the same
effect upon the man as they have upon his machine. The
Victorian, on the contrary, is a “gallery” rider of the most
pronounced type. Pedalling slowly around the track,
prior to starting in a race, he gazes patrpnisingly into the
faces of those surrounding the arena in order to observe
if each countenance wears a due expression of admiration.
At the M.B.C. meeting, after Parsons had thus saunter-
ingly completed several circuits, one of those “ sabre cuts
of Saxon speech ” came in stentorian tones from a spec-
tator : “ It’s all right; you get on your mark ; we’ve had a
good look at you.”
“And so it is at every meeting. The way Par-
sons allowed Martin to outwit him in the last lap of the
Mile would have done discredit to a novice. That he will
profit much by experience gained in continental contests
is extremely problematical. The case of the man who,
after two years’ continuous racing, shows no glimmer of
generalship, may fairly be considered hopeless. That
Parsons would have won the 10 miles with competent
pacing, even his opponent admits. He twice had nearly
a half-lap advantage, and could have gone on had the
pace been forthcoming. To quote Porta : ‘ After 10
mile, Marrtin very much exhaust—Parrsong, only a lit.
Put Marrtin head on Parrsong ; Parrsong win.’ And the
chances are that had Parsons only guessed how ‘ ver’
much exhaust ’ his ultra - game opponent really was, he
could have won the 10 miles despite the indifferent pace.
However, any ill-luck which the Australian may have hail
in the longer race was amply compensated for by his
good fortune in gaining a ‘ dead-heat ’ verdict in the five-
miles.”
The medley of pace-making in the Martin-
Parsons match appeared rather ridiculous to old sports
accustomed to seeing “ champions ” in every other kind
of racing travel on their own merits. And the wailing
after the 5 and 10-mile events ! If there had been a
better pace for one, or other, or both men, and if some-
body’s triplet had picked him up earlier or dropped him
sooner, or done anything different to what was done
where would the other fellow have been when the bel
rang? The prattle of the cycling world seems more
puerile than the awful blasphemy of the punter who
“ knew it all along and never backed it after all.”
The quadruplet was a dead failure as a pacer at
the Martin-Parsons matches. The four men up hadn’t the
hang of the thing for lack of practice, and so the quad,
wasn’t in it with Martin’s lightning triplet. The machine
was geared to 93, but runs to 120. Pacemakers are
squeamish about straddling a quad, at its best, knowing
that if am thing “ went ” it would probably mean coffins
for four.

Harris is, at length, getting into the semblance
of form, and there is much talk of a match between the
Englishman and But it is extremely provable
that the latter’s shrewdness will prevent him being drawn
into a genuine go with the “ Leicester Flyer ’’—un-
doubtedly the best man, when in trim, at present in Aus-
tralia. Both Martin and Harris, by the way, ride Humber
machines.
* » * *
As showing how much the universal craze cuts
into the profits of Melb. Tram Co. Between the hours of
6.30 and 10.30 on a recent morning, 1280 bikes crossed
Prince's Bridge from the southern suburbs.
* * * *
The height of tantalisation—to be shaft-horse in
a tandem bike with a perfectly-formed Venus always just
ahead of you.
*i * » *
“You must be making a fortune out of your
bicycles,” recently remarked a Melb. journalist to a big
hardware man in the same city. “ Not so much as you
think,” replied the man of iron. “ Certainly the jiggers
are moving all right, but the perambulator sales have
gone down to nothing. We re just making up on one
line what we lose on the other.”
» * » *
Cyclist Porta had strong ideas of joining Dr.
Fiaschi, of Sydney, in that nigger-hunting expedition to
Abyssinia. A few terse remarks from business-man
Martin, however, convinced him of the fatuity of this
course while life and love and big purses remain on this
side for the man who can turn off a mile under 2min.
* * * *
Don Walker attempted to lower the half-mile
record of 58 sec., but took 59 l-sth. Judging by the w-ay
he looked for the numbers to go up he didn’t seem to
think he was going “too slow'.” Apparently, however,
some enthusiastic admirers attributed his failure to “ bad
pacing.” So he made a second attempt and registered
61 l-sth sec.

hetw'een Martin, Harris, Parsons, Megson, Payne, Pither,
and Don Walker w'ould be worth an enterprising
manager s consideration.
* * * «
Don Walker lowered Zim.’s record on the same
track for Jmile from 30 4-ssec. to 23 l-ssec. But too much
reliance can’t be placed on ticks of a sec. or secs, in cases
of a flying start where the antiquated pistol is used and
the ear and eye of the judge are trusted. The “ personal
equation ” enters too largely into the question.
Surely by a little electrical ingenuity it could be
managed that the passage of the front wheel over a certain
portion of the starting-line should sound a gon"- and set
the clock going, and that the passage of the wheel over
the winning-line should likewise sound a gong and stop
the ticker.
• * * •
Men of color, prominent in most other sports,
do not seem to be happy on the bike. Those weak shins?
Cyclist Martin, judging from his portrait auto-
graphs, pushes a pen with more difficulty than he plugs a
bike along.
• • * *
Football has developed a new and hideous skin-
disease known as “scrum-pox,” due to the scratches and
abrasions received in rough play. It is infectious if the
secretion touches <* surface during a “ scrummage”—
and is apparently a sort of eczema or pruritis.
For good fishing, go to the old penal settlement
of Port Macquarie. The ordinary fish-liar is there non-
existent—everyone catches fish so plentifully that he
hasn’t time to lie. Along the banks of the Hastings
whiting and flathead are generally plentiful, and on the
outside beaches fine bream and tarwhine up to 6 and 71b.
rage furiously. Last week a party of four caught over
260 schnapper in six hours. And fishing is not the only
attraction. One can sleep in the street at night without
being “ moved on ; ” likewise there is a municipal council
whose deliberations would rouse Washington Irving from
his grave to laughq also, a cheerful graveyard, where,
when the sun is hot, you can kill snakes on the dilapi-
pated brick-work of the tomb “ Sacred to the memory of
Joanna Pumpkin, relict bf Sergeant Elias Pumpkin of
H.M. 4th Buffs, who died full bf grace, June, 1823. And
the Serpent tempted Eve,” &c. Moreover, you can fall
over cows in the main street at night.
* » '* *
Every season interest in Adelaide football grows
beautifully less ; all the senior clubs begin the present
winter with heavy debts. On the other hand, lacrosse is
making rapid strides, and a junior association has now
been formed. There are now 12 lacrosse clubs in Ade-
laide, against only half that number in ’93.
» « * *
Extracts from balance-sheet of a Southern foot-
ball-club : “Petty expenses, £6345. Bd. ; training expenses
and fees, £123 12s. (!); travelling expenses, £149 Bs. 6d.”
And the players all play purely for honor and glory !
Tom Williams, beaten by Tracey at ’Ome, beat
his opponent twice in Melb. some time ago. Tracey has
apparently come on wonderfully under ’Murkan tuition,
and adopted Williams’s rushing style of fighting with
success.
* * * •
One feature of the finish of the Doncaster was
that three brothers, the Delaneys, rode the placed horses.
» * * »
The Bulletin last week said Mick Dooley
should beat Peter Felix in two rounds. Michael won, on
Saturday night, in a round and a-half.
♦ * * *
The very newest bluff of Pompadour Jim reads
as follows : “ James J. Corbett has decided to re-enter the
prize-ring and defend the title of Champion of the World
against all comers.” It would almost seem as if the hair-
oiled one hadn’t yet realised that he has no title left to
defend against any one.
» * * *
Let the cycle mad public beware of matches.
The first few will most probably be bona-fide ; but, after
that—hold your nose !
» » » *
It has arrived. The township of Johnson, Ohio,
prohibits the riding or driving of a horse, mule or other
animal through the streets on account of the liability of
such action to interfere with the freedom of—hats off !
cyclists. On entering Johnson, every equestrian or driver
is compelled to dismount and lead his prad within a pres-
cribed space on one side of the roadway until he reaches
the boundary of the town. Offenders incur lOOdol. fine.
» * » *
Kortwright, the celebrated amateur bowler,
while trundling at “ Lords,” was badly pasted by a parson
at the wickets. At last Kortwright impatiently said, loud
enough to be heard over the field, “I’ll knock his pulpit
over this time,” and delivered the ball. Since which he
has never been allowed to play at “ Lords.”
» » » *
In Melbourne the bicycle-race boom has shrunk
a lot since New Year’s Day.
* • * *
The Waitangi sunk at Lyttleton turns out not
to have been the M.L. champion yacht, but a much smaller
Waitangi. It seems that Waitangis are almost as plenti-
ful in the M.L. yachting world as Hinemoas in the world
of M.L. fiction.
* * * *
The American shearing record (lOhrs. daily) is
held by F. H. Moreland, with 356, shorn at Baggs, Wyom-
ing, recently. At same place on same day Phil. Walsh
also, with 322, beat the previous record, which was 312.
» * * *
Memmott, the Victorian billiard-player, was
beaten in London, the other week, by H. W. Stevenson,
the pair being bracketed as “ colonial ” players. Who is
H. W. Stevenson ?
* * * *
Percy Cavill, the quarter and half-mile champion
swimmer of N.S. W., is a good professional allowed to swim
as an amateur. He was born at Kensington Baths,
London, and is now 20 years old. He won a few races as
a schoolboy, and at the age of 11 saved another boy from
drowning and received the Royal Humane Society’s medal.
Last Jan. he won the N.S. W. half-mile and mile champion-
ships quite easily, and the other day at St. Kilda, Melb.,
he won the quarter-mile as he liked, and frightened every-
one from contesting the half-mile with him. On the
whole, this is an advantage, for a half-mile swimming
contest is as tedious an entertainment as a half-mile-long
sermon.
* * * *
Prior to the championship battle, El Paso
parsons Circularised churches and Christian crumpet and
other associations throughout the State, asking that
“ prayers be offered on a certain Sunday for the stoppage
of the fight.” Also, “ that El Paso be saved from the
attendant disgrace.” The whole of the disgrace fell on
Maher. Beyond which Providence didn’t specially inter-
fere.
* • * *
Jack, the Australian boxing kangaroo, has died
at London Aquarium after a five years’ pugilistic career.
* * * *
Tough and level-headed Billy Murphy has
decided to return to his trade, tailoring, and give up box-
ing except as an exercise. About the first pug. on record
to leave the ring for hard work.
* * * »
Harvard ’Varsity, U.S.A., has invented the
new game of “push-ball.” A 6ft. diameter ball, weigh-
ing 1201 b., is shoved for goals by two teams of eight men
each.

lad, A. Jack, who weighed under 6st.

The linotype is a-movin’ on. Launceston
Examiner leads the way in Tas. with a complete

The re-election at Townsville of Philp
emphasises the fact that Separation is dead
in .Townsville. Philp publicly abjured Separation
when he accepted office ; slapped his constituents
in the face by declining to resign his seat and
put up again in accordance with constitutional
usage ; and his constituents have dutifully eaten
the leek and re-elected him by a spanking majo-
rity. If, however, the Boodlewraith continues to
spoon-feed Townsville as hitherto, that town no
more requires Separation than does the horse-leech
from the body of its victim.
Mr. Ziman, the representative in Maori-
land of a powerful English mining co., has offered
to contribute £ 1000 to start a fund to present
England with a battle-ship costing half-a-million
pounds, the whole of the money to be raised by
subscriptions in Australasia. Mr. Ziman (whose
idea is taken from a recent Pall Mall suggestion
that a war-ship should be provided by the mer-
chants of London) is going to do some big
mining flotations in London presently. His £ 1000
would go a very short way in buying a iJoOO.OOO
ship, but it would go far as a mining-float advt.
By the way, Ziman s London secretary (or chair-
man) is ex-Agent-General Perceval.
Any one who wants to see how three
governors look when they get together, is hereby
referred to a fine photo, lately issued by Newman,
of Sydney. Gov. Buxton, the S.A. potentate
with the reduced salary, sits on the right with a
solid expression and a shaven upper-lip. Gov.
Brassey, also shaven, sits down heavily on the left
and gazes straight at the camera with a hard,
rocky aspect. Hampden stands up in the centre
with a feeble smile on his lips, and looks strikingly
like ex-Premier Shiels of Victoria, before that
long slim gentleman ceased to smile. Gov. Hamp-
den is the only human-looking man of the trio ;
the other two look like hunks of displeased road-
metal.

ECZEMA
CURED BY
Ayer’s Sarsaparilla
Mrs. E. Wyatt, of Port Road, West
Hindinarsh, So. Australia, writes of
the sad condition of her iit.tle daugh-
ter, whose portrait she also sends:
“My daughter was afflicted with
Eczema of the most aggravated
type. The disease first appeared in
eruptions on her head, then her hair
began to fall ont, and in spite of
the best medical advice and treat-
ment she grew steadily worse. The
sores were full of matter and were
extremely offensive. Her eyes be-
came affected, arid she was, in truth/
in a terrible state. My neighbors
were very sympathetic and took
great interest in the case. They
persuaded me to try Ayer’s Sarsa-
parilla, and I am most thankful to
be able to say that this wonderful
medicine completely restored my
daughter’s health. She has now as
good a head of hair as anyone could
wish, her eyes are perfectly well,
and she is a fine girl of eight years
with every prospect of growing up
to be a strong and healthy woman/’

The Pedaleuse: A Guitar Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Pedaleuse” is coming into vogue in England as a
name for the female cyclist.
Collins treated (his first name was Wilkie)
Long ago of “ The Woman in White,”
But now, in a garment less milky,
I he Woman on Whe Is is in sight;
Coll ins-street’d be almost romantic,
The “ Block ” no incentive to blues,
If She had not driven us frantic,
The hard-faced, stuck-up Pedaleuse !
So loose !
A he don’t-care-a Pedaleuse !
Some wear the high collar and shirting,
The necktie and clip d la male,
Over heavy inelegant skirting
That catches the wind like a sail;
Or, the sex s sweet coyness is bartered
r l' or the giddiest gaiters and shoes,
Not to mention—well, things that are gartered
’Neath your knickers, O brave Pedaleuse !
Sing low !
Is it brazen, or brave, Pedaleuse ?
They skirmish ahead of the dummy,
They join the parade to the church,
In a costume that’s meant to be “crummy,”
The hansom they leave in the lurch ;
The larrikin, knowing no better,
Hurls after ’em ribald abuse,
Then the newspapers publish a letter
That is penned by the pained Pedaleuse !
She’s on deck !
The pneumatic and “ New ” Pedaleuse !
But there’s something to us more ende,.
In the girl who has never aspired
To a saddle and knowledge of gearing
And who loves with a love never tyred ;
Give us Her with the Heart that won’t doubt us,
Give us lips that no kisses refuse,
Give us arms winding warmly about us—
The other, the best Pedaleuse!
Piano !
The sweet, and not fleet Pedaleuse !
Ballads !
The dear, side-by-side Pedaleuse !
Sweetheart !
The guarding and good Pedaleuse !
Here’s love !
The Angel-on-Earth Pedaleuse!
Melb., March, ’96.
H.C.C.
No educational body in Australia is legally
empowered to confer the title of D.D. Hence, as
Cardinal Moran’s church is a law unto itself,
certain University dignitaries fear that unless
they obtain the “faculty” at once, St. Patrick’s
College at Manly will lead the Australian field in
this respect.
lie Rbntgen’s photography. The ordi-
nary philosopher is apt to forget that solids
are solids only in a manner of speaking. The
atoms of metal, stone, &c., are merely juxtaposed,
not united. In experiments with the hydraulic
press it was found that iron cylinders 3in. thick
“sweated” when the inside pressure reached
tons per square inch. This sweating was due to
the molecules of water forcing their way between
the atoms of iron and oozing to the outer surface.
Where water can go, certain rays forming part of
the ordinary light-ray will also find a passage. As
the expansion of solids means that their particles
are forced farther from each other, it will probably
be found that the x rays pass through heated metal
or wood better and more clearly than through
the same material at a normal temperature.
There is very fair ground for the assertion that
the Rontgen discovery is the “ biggest ” regarding
light since the days of Newton. Who will bet
that within a year studios won’t be opened in
Melbourne and Sydney for the purpose of “ taking
your skeleton ” ?

Mr. Button writes later: —“The Pills I had
from you were of great benefit to me and others.
In one case, when three hunters and trappers

gave him a few of your Pills. lam pleased to
inform you that having to pass their camp next

~ ® ov - Brassey, like Robert Burns, has
quotations for every occasion.” H.E. never
makes a speech without throwing in a handful of
quotations. The Governor keeps up in Melbourne
tne custom he has followed for many years, of
laving notable extracts from his reading copied
y a type-writer on to separate sheets of fcap.,
and placed in order in index-boxes. So he
can turn up information, quotations and figures on
any subject from the wings of angels in the A box
to the breeding of zebras in box Z.

“He professed religion very ardently,
being a lay reader up to a few months ago, and
was president of the Christian Endeavor Society,”
says the Age, summing up a Vic. solicitor now
detained m a commodious stone building awaiting
trial. He frequently offered prayers in his office
when clients waited upon him for money, and
generally handed round tracts at the close of the
interview. One creditor was handed a tract,
headed Patience and Resignation,’ after dunning
him in vain for money.” Tne attorney is in trouble
on charges of getting at a workman with a large
family by a fraudulent mortgage on his wooden cot-
tage, and annexing a few hundreds from a police-
man. Also, there are certain suspicions about J 23700
belonging to a widow, .£450 belonging to another
widow, large sums belonging to four more widows;
the entire pile of an old man, who is beginning to
show signs of insanity over his vanished money,
and two or three dozen other items—in fact, if all
the suspicions are correct, the town has been
practically eaten up. And it took 20 years for
the local inhabitants to begin to suspect a solicitor
who “ frequently offered prayers in his office when
clients waited'upon him for money.”
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, 3, Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *
Magnificent peroration of Q. Attorney-
General Byrnes, on the eve of his defeat for North
Brisbane :
Your chief has given you the signal—answer to the call.
Let to-morrow’s work gladden your chiefs heart, and that
of your old chief, Thomas M‘llwraith, who is faraway, but
not forgotten : and praise be to God he is improving day
by day ! Gladden Ms heart! This election is being
watched throughout the country. Men, women, and
children in distant parts will say, if you do your duty,
“ Thank God, Brisbane North is saved; peace and pro-
gress will reign throughout the land ” ; and many Aus-
tralian natives will throw up their hats and give three
cheers when they see you have returned ME, and. the
gentleman who is running with ME. . . . The signal has
been given by your chief as it was by another Nelson of
immortal fame : “ Queensland to-morrow expects that
every man will do his duty ! ” I ask you to rally together
side by side, shoulder to shoulder. The slopes are steep,
but with one great effort you will those slopes and
you will plant at the top the banner of your pride, and
the air will resound with the watchword of every patriotic
Britisher—“ Nelson and Victory ! ” (Loud and prolonged
cheering.)
Then the electors threw Byrnes out on his swelled
head with a thud heard as far as Thursday Island;
and the patriotic Britishers and the bogus Aus-
tralian natives steeped themselves in beer and
gloom ; and the little children in distant parts
cried because North Brisbane wasn’t saved ; and
dear old chief M‘llwraith’s heart didn’t gladden
one bit; and the disgusted Thomas Joseph went
out and bought a hat four sizes smaller and found
it miles too big for him.

The N.S.W. Seamen’s Union has passed a
resolution expressing its entire confidence in the
integrity of Sam. Smith, its late secretary, “not-
withstanding the verdict of the jury in the Daily
Post case. ”
Little Dr. Maloney, M.P. for N. Melb.,
seems to have sold the Age and his electors badly.
At the beginning of the year he applied for Par-
liamentary leave of absence on the plea of ill-
health, and stated that he intended taking a trip
to Ceylon and running over the island for a couple
of months. The Argus thereupon averred that
he really was going to India to join electric-healer
Geismardo alias Richards, in whose powers he
sincerely believed. The Age hurled back the base
insult with scorn. Now it would seem that the
Argus was more or loss right; Maloney’s name
appears bracketed with Richards’s in the latter’s
advts. The Age, in indignation, publishes ex-
tracts from the Indian papers in order to get
ahead of the Argus, and the North Melb. Labor
League has decided to cast out Maloney for ever-
more—whereupon a Bulletin correspondent :
“ Maloney’s troubles ! He never made a cent out
of politics. Most of his electors loafed on him for
medical attendance and comforts, and now that
the little doctor has cut himself adrift he can com-
bine money-making and amusement, and snap his
fingers at those he served if not too wisely, at all
events too well.”
“The City of the Dismal Drizzle ” is the
new name for Dunedin (M.L.). Someone else
christened it, years ago, the City of Slop, Slush,
I and Snuffle, but the name failed to stick.
Apropos the late flogging sentences : a
' Sydney lawyer, who once drove a coach-and-four
through the flogging clauses of the N.S. Wales
Act, and damaged it to such an extent that an
Amending -Act was passed to repair the gap, is
lying in wait to repeat the feat.
Sydney Mayor Isaac Ellis Ives boasts that,
although an old Sydney resident, he has never
attended Ratulwick races, owing to his deep and
consistent hatred of all gambling practices. Seeing
that the highest functionaries of the State-
Governors and Premiers —attend these celebra-
tions, this boast of the glorified alderman looks
like a haughty disapprobation of the morality of
his official superiors.
As surmised, the British expedition to
Dongola, undertaken despite the pronounced ill-
will of Prance, Russia, and Germany, is chiefly a
bid for an alliance with Italy which will
relieve Britain’s “splendid isolation.” Italy
is still alleged to be a Great Power, and J.
Bull has cash and credit enough to finance
her if the pinch comes. And, if she can be de-
tached from the Triple Alliance—which seems not
improbable, now—Germany will get another rub
in return for her bluster. Exactly how the Eng-
lish expedition will help Italy in Abyssinia isn’t
very clear, but there’s no doubt it is a clever diplo-
matic move in that direction, and it is gratefully
regarded by the Italian press.

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official not ice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ TIEHR-lELE] CASTLES 77 and lc C-A.IEPS EC-A-ZEnT. 35
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Ci(/areltes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. (Agents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

Some Sporting Matters.
Another Keirl story : Just before last Melb.
Cup that accomplished swindler limped into Dr. O’Hara’s
Collins-st. surgery and spun a yarn about an electric-light,
wire falling across his ankle, which he feared was badly-
injured. The surgeon examined the stranger’s limb and
pronounced it perfectly in order ; upon which the patient
airily remarked : “ Well, doctor, I haven’t a guinea with
me; I suppose it will do all right if I send it up ? ” O’Hara
at once put the 21s. down among the bad debts but
acquiesced, upon which Keirl thanked him and said good-
day. On turning the door-handle, however, a sudden
thought seemed to strike him. “ By the way,” he cried,
producing a book of slips from his pocket, “ I’m getting-
up a sweep on the Cup, 100 at £1 Is. What do you say to
taking one, doctor, and calling the deal square?” Always
ready for anything in the horse line, the doctor detached
a slip and, at the other’s request, signed the block. Forti-
fied with the signature, Keirl immediately made a house-
to-house canvass of Collins-st. East, calling on every
medical man in the locality and hat he was
the authorised agent of O Hara to conduct a guinea sweep.
The result of that sweep will be advertised when the sea
gives up its dead.
* * * *
An exacting, garrulous trainer borrowed an ap-
prentice from a friend’s stable to ride a dead cert After
he had carefully explained at great length how he was to
start, where he was to stay, when to come, &c., the young-
ster said he understood. The neddy didn’t get a place,
and the trainer, as usual, complained that the jock hadn’t
done as he was told. “ Why didn’t you ride according to
my friend’s orders?” said the boy’s boss, later, as he
belted him with a stick. “ So I did, sir,” he moaned
pitifully ; “ but it was a six-furlong race, and he gave me
orders for at least 10 miles.”
* * « *
In the recent match for the Wimmera premier-
ship Stawell batted first and made 135 ; Dimboola had lost
five wickets for 90 when two men got together and took
the score to 335, the Stawell men being meanwhile sub-
jected to merciless “ chyak.” The bves had totalled 25,
when a small boy gravely emerged from the pavilion and
walked into the circle struggling under the weight of a
huge practice-net. Amid the roars of the crowd he
deposited it behind the wickets, and, after quietly indi-
cating its whereabouts to the local Johns, with a gesture
worthy of Fred. Kaye he retired. Even the Stawellites
had to smile.
* * * *
W. P. Crick’s jockey, in a burst of professional
anger, held forth thus to an admiring circle of equestrian
mites : “ Oh, Crick, he’s all right; but he’s got no brains.
He won’t never do as I tells him. He’s got no more nouse
than a, baby, he ain’t ! I can’t keep my temper with him,
sometimes. Why, if he’d taken my advice, he’d been a
rich man long ago. He’s got no more brains than a goat,
you take it from me ; and he knows no more about ’orses
’n a baby.” Which shows the value of “standpoint” in
criticism.
» * » *
If W. R. Wilson had been asked which horse he
would take if the pick of his lotteried stud had been
offered him, he would probably have picked the horse he
won in the sweep. London Sportsman reckons him the
finest St. Simon has yet sired.
* * * i
Mat. O’Shanassy’s plan for obviating the chance
of a horse getting “ shut in ” at the last turn of a race-
course is practical on the face of it. He proposes to force
the field towards the outer fence by means of an inner
railing, which will start from the curve of the inner fence
and continue until it gets into a parallel line with it some
30ft. or so further out, according to the shape of the
course. Then the railing will break off, leaving the full
width of the straight for the accommodation of the
crowd. The field will, naturally, open out, and the horses
in the second and third divisions will get a chance to come
through, if equal to the effort. Accidents among the
front division will be rather less likely to happen than at
present, and the crowding behind them ought to be con.
siderably lessened, one imagines. If the excuse for
crowding is made smaller, cases of foul riding will bo
tne more clearly apparent.

* * * *
A sporting organ speaks of the con-fraternity of
sporting men. The “conk” (or “cronk”) fraternity is
probably meant.
* * * *
In a M.L. trot once a Maori rode his own
neddy, a flea-bitten grey, and won so handsomely from
the limit time mark that he was re-handicapped for the
last event on the card. His penalty left him with no
apparent show, but he won comfortably, and paid a big
div. Half-an-hour later one of the stewards noticed the
joyful Day and Martin attending to two flea-bitten greys
in a pub.-yard of the township hard by, but it wasn’t till
after a week’s hard thinking that he tumbled to the fact,
which he afterwards verified, that the artful Maori had
won the last event with a clinking trotter passed out for
two years in an adjoining province a short time previously.
♦ • * *
“ Footy ” Pearce’s son, who promises to make a
champion on the water, should take to the bike. There
is more money in it just now.
“Footy,” in the good old days, could nip with
the big and first toe with two-pound-crab power. On
one occasion the late Mr. Dailey and a party were out net
fishing with Pearce, and Plain Bill had his trousers tucked
up to his knees. His massive calf proved too big a tempta-
tion to “ Footy,” who, standing next to the shirt-sleeved
Caesar, gave him a lightning nip, and went on smoking as
if nothing had happened. A blood-curdling yell went up
from William Bede, who never discovered where that nip
came from.
* * * *
The average johnny who tosses his chum for
cigars can't tell you what are the odds against winning
any two successive tosses. Very few readers of this para-
graph can state, on the instant, the odds against crying
Heads or Tails correctly any particular three times in
succession. They all think they can, but they can’t.
N.Y. Amateur Athletic Board has decided that
a man who has “no visible means” is not an amateur.
What is an amateur, anyhow ? Sydney Rowing Ass.
the other day disqualified a member who helped his
brother to do a little plumbing.
The best way of impartially judging amateurism
in Australia is to walk round the three-ball shops.
* * * *
A pitiable sight at Dunedin (M.L.) meeting was
the horde of bookmakers lined up outside the gate, be-
seeching admission. Though prohibited they will lay
tote odds on the quiet, and wandering private detectives
are useless in the paddock. On this occasion the tote-
receipts went up considerably. If other clubs adopt the
exelusion policy, bookie will have to view the races from
a neighboring gum-tree.
* * * *
“ Twopence for a bicycle and its rider,” say the
new horse-ferry regulations between Sydney and North
Shore. A case going on in England when last mail left
related to a passenger who carried his bicycle in his arms
to escape the extra toll But the Australian likes a law-
court less than an Englishman does.

Sweeney, the marvellous Irish-American high-
jumper, is soon to have a worthy foeman, as Ryan, holder
of Irish and English championship with a record of 6ft.
4>£in., has crossed the Atlantic to meet him. On paper,
the Australian, Conway, should be nearly as good as
either.
Rabbits are gradually making their way
into Gippsland. Settlers would infinitely rather
see the devil himself. The devil, anyhow, doesn’t
eat grass.
