N.S.W. Agricultural Department has im-
ported from Persia some cuttings of the Persian
vines which produce large, seedless grapes, with a
view to establishing in N.S.W. a seedless-raisin
industry.
Dogs’ tailors are the latest in fad-stricken
Europe, where pet Tobies now have their seaside
costumes, reception gowns, Cup dresses, &c., pro-
vided even with pockets for Toby’s special railway-
ticket. On wet days, Toby wears goloshes. At
weddings, dog Tray is garlanded with orange-
blossoms.
An eccentric French surgeon, Dr. Delar-
mel, has been experimenting on the larynxes of
various animals, with a view to changing their
voices. He now has a cat which barks like a
terrier, and a sheep which makes remarks in the
tones of a Newfoundland dog. He has been
offered big sums to lend them to various travelling
shows for a term, but he refuses.
The J.P. once more, and Port Melbourne
again. One John Simpson came up to a total
stranger, a respectable woman, and addressed her
offensively. Told to mind his own business, he
promptly knocked her down and kicked her
severely. Fine, 40s. If you can knock down the
first woman you meet in the street and jump on
her for 405., Lady Brassey, as an asset, would be
worth —how much ?
Dr. Arthur Macdonald, social pathologist
to U.S. Bureau of Education, avers, with Pro-
fessor Krafft - Ebing of Vienna, that general
paralysis is largely on the increase among women
who follow the occupations of men ; hence an in-
creased tendency toward crime in women. If
there were as many women in active work and
business as there are men, then, instead of one
woman to every five men in the prisons of the
world, there would be five to five.
Dear Bulletin, —How is it that, with
apparently few exceptions, medical practitioners
are “stern, unbending Tories ”? Recently, when
travelling to Maoriland, a large number of my
fellow-passengers were medicoes —members of the
Interprovincial Medical Congress which sat at
Dunedin last month. Of the 50 or so whom I met
going and returning, I honestly believe not half-
a-dozen could be found—l could not find even
one—with the slightest leaning towards Liberal-
ism. They all regarded manhood suffrage as a
curse ; would deprive every unpropertied man of
his political right; favored land monopoly; and
considered that working men were overpaid, and
that the real saviours of the country were to be
found in the various Legislative Councils of Aus-
tralia ! Yet amongst these men, supposed to be
educated gentlemen, were many who had been
hit hard in the land-boom and by rotten financial
institutions—including two Melbourne medicoes
said to have dropped respectively =£50,000 and
£30,000 by the collapse of the boom and the failure
of the banks. These men blamed the Trades Hall
for their losses ; though if you asked them to
explain why they could not tell you. Now, how
is it that, while good democrats are so frequently
met at the bar and in journalism, they so rarely
gain foothold in physic? In my opinion, the
average medico is ten times a more rabid Tory
than even the average parson.
J.R.
An Object Lesson.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Archbishop Carr, of Melbourne, has set up a really solid
defence for his beloved church-bazaar gamble. People
come to bazaars, says the prelate, with a view to spending
a certain amount of money pleasantly, and as regards the
games of chance in vogue at these Christian gatherings
they are “ rather calculated to moderate persons’ desires
to embark much money in such enterprises.” The race-
game, in short, is a sanctified means of warning its
votaries to avoid the Turf, and its motto ought to be —
“ Keep off the grass !”
Young Gussie, gay and debonnair,
Went down to a bazaar,
And, having half-a-sov. to spare,
Fell captive to a creature fair
Who stood behind a bar.
Not like to those where wicked drinks
Are vended by a forward minx,
With crafty smiles or brazen winks—
But quite another sort of bar,
Innocuous and circular.
“ One shilling in—the winner, ten !
Now, buy a ticket, do.”
Poor Gussie anted-up and, when
She’d wheedled nineteen other men,
A mob of gee-gees flew
Around a board in manner queer.
The scheme of working wasn’t clear,
But presently the little dear
Announced the winner—Number Two,
And ten bright shillings Gussie drew.
The maiden took the tickets back
To sell again, she did.
The winning number from the pack
Was dealt to Dick, or Tom, or Jack,
Whose luck the losers chid.
Dame Fortune smiled on every one
(For that’s the way ’tis often done)
Till twenty races had been run.
The truth could be no longer hid—
Each man had lost just “ arf a quid.”
Young Gussie scratched his hairless chin
And smiled a smile of woe ;
He thought it cwuel thus to skin
A simple lamb who wants to win
Some money, doncherknow.
The maiden spake, with gentle force—•
“ Suppose yourselves upon the course ;
You are the flies who back a horse
And I’m the spider, Ikey Mo. —
I beg you to regard me so.”
Those twenty punters thanked the maid
For teaching them to quit
The Turf, where Ikey’s odds are laid
Upon the system here displayed.
They blessed her ready wit,
And, gazing at her earnest face,
Swore by their hopes of Heaven’s grace
They’d never wager on a race,
Except when Holy Church thinks fit
To bid them drop their little bit.
EDMUND FISHER.

J Hasty Inspection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Minnie M‘Bride, conditional owner of 100 acres,
was taking her ease on her father’s farm when
word came that the new Crown Land Ranger was
close at hand.
For three weeks' Minnie had neglected her
selection, but in less than an hour she was in her
hut, removing the superfluous matter with
astonishing vigor. Before she had finished, how-
ever, and when only her front room had assumed
a home-like look, the ranger rode on to her land.
She peeped through the window-curtain, and
caught him looking at her new fence. A little re-
assured, she straightened her hair, donned a
smart apron, and went out to meet him. He m-
traduced himself, and complimented her on the
fine land she had secured. She put his horse in
the shed, and showed him round the place, draw-
r ’ ,
ing his attention particularly to her new fence and
shed. He admired both, and, with a light heart,
she invited him to the hut, and sat beside him on
the verandah. The Ranger was a young man
with a pleasant manner. Minnie felt quite at
home with him. '
“ Ever lonely here ? ” he asked. “You hear the
dingoes often, I suppose ? ”
“ Oh, yes,” she answered. “ Last night I dozed
beside the fire with the doors all open, and my
salt-beef boiling away beside me, and I went to
sleep, and when I woke the salt-beef was gone.”
“ They come right in, then ? ”
“ No, I forgot to put it in.”
He inquired about the people and selections
around, and Minnie answered in detail, laboring
slightly to give the-impression that she was. on a
higher plane than her neighbors. The evening
wore on, and the gathering darkness had a charm
all its own for them, until the mosquitoes gathered
also, and forced them inside. Minnie lit the lamp,
and the Ranger looked round with a comprehen-
sive glance that embraced the clean boards and
curtains, and the flowers and books on the table.
He sat down and gazed abstractedly at Minnie as
he talked, and treated her somewhat incoherent
replies and remarks with the utmost deference.
She felt elated. She knew girls who would con-
aider him deeply in love for less than this, and she
wished they were looking through the window
just then. Her books—novelettes for the most
[.art—were engaging his attention, when a horse
cantered round to the back of the hut.
Minnie rose with a heightened color. The light
fell on her fair hair and red cheeks as she passed
through the doorway to the dark kitchen. A
young man met her outside, and took her in his
arms. She whispered that the Ranger was in the
hut. He whispered, only his thick voice prevented
it being a success, that he had come directly Jack
Casey had told him she was there. He asked
anxiously if she thought she would pull through,
and she replied confidently that she would, adding
that the new man was none of your prying sort.
“ I don’t know,” the young fellow said, “Mrs.
Casey says he asks questions, but Jack says he’s a
queer sort—collects beetles, and lies on his back
under the trees. He said he would hang round
and watch the Ranger off, and he reminded her
that he had had a long ride. She dissuaded him,
however, and coaxed him away in a good humor,
with the promise of seeing her the next night,
“ I must lend him some novelettes,” she thought
as he rode away—“he is a little rough and his
mouth is alwaysWet.’’
“ They killed at home to-day,” she explained on
her return to the room. The Ranger was still
looking over her books, and she began to dilate on
her favorite stories, but in less than half-an-hour
he rose to go. He shook hands heartily, and ex-
pressed a hope to see Minnie soon again.
She sat for a few minutes after he had gone
arranging her little triumph for recital, until a
slip of paper among her books caught her eye. She
lifted it carelessly and read—“ M. M'Bride’s selec-
tion.—Owner in occupation. New fence and shed
completed. One room of hut comfortable. Dirt
and cobwebs in bedroom. Hornet’s nest hanging
from bed-curtain.”
Bananaland.
BAY ASH.
A Study in Competition.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The same current that flung him across jagged
ironstone reefs swept the woman high amongst
weeds and' sea-shells under the lee of the island.
Eastward, a brown belt of dawn lifted from the
grey mists and storm-smoke that hung upon a
sullen sea. From the outer reefs came the grind-
ing crunch of ’a keel against ironstone, and when
the tide heaved across the red sand-spit the parrots
in the palm-scrub heard a rending of bolts and a
splitting of planks : it was the.death-rattle of the
good ship Diana.
Soon a radiating heat sent the bruised man
limping to the cliff shelter, and the same tropic
warmth shook the woman from a death-like torpor
and quickened her pulse. They met unexpectedly
in the cool rock shadows. A laughing gladness
crept into the man’s heart when he saw that the
awful solitude would be shared. But the woman,
.with thought-modes opposed to his, acted strangely
and became more elusive than thistle-down. He
watched her sadly, making mad, restraining ges-
tures from afar.
Then, being a sailor and somewhat of a car-
penter, the man built a house without hammer
or nails and heaped therein a fine, luxurious couch
of sun-dried weed and banyan leaf.
“For you to live in ! ” he shouted, and stalked
across the island to build himself another.
She crept into the shelter without a word or
sign of gratitude. Day after day he came over
with offerings of fish and wild bananas which were
accepted in silence and with much caution. His
hair grew about his ears, and gradually his rags
fell away and an era of fig-leaf clothing began.
The Diana-vestal quaked upon her altitude of
ascetic morality—the sailor-man’s legs were knotty
and short—in direct opposition to Art (which is
long)—and threw parabolic shadows on the sward.
Spring came, and one morning she peered from
her lair of ferns at the strange object that
approached her on all fours.
“Let me live here !” The object made signs
and a noise that filled the air with a myriad jargon-
ing birds. “ Let me live here, woman ! There
are devils in my house ; I see ’em at night. ” But
her hand made such a swift movement to the knife-
haft in her girdle that he fled. The offerings of
food continued, dished up on leaves—the last
touch of his civilisation.
“ Let me live here ! ” Thereafter he repeated it
daily ; and the same thing happened always —he
fled from the knife-haft. The equinoctial gales
brought another ship in the night to the grinding
maw of the ironstone reef. At dawn a wild-eyed
man without clothes glanced under his long-nailed
fist and waited for things to stir. A boat pitched
across the seething line of near surf ; no sound
from the black, forbidding wreck —hull spiked on a
fang of reef and stiller than a plague-ship.
“See !” he cried, in a language almost forgotten.
His finger went out to the incoming boat.
“ A woman in it; a woman only ; the others are
dead and drowned. Good. She’ll sing to me, and
talk. God, I hope she can sing!” He capered
frivolously and “without shame.” A white hand
sought his naked shoulder ; he turned to face, not
the terrible knife, but the beauteous, half-fawmng
Diana-woinan. „
“ This poor creature must live somewhere, she
whispered, “ and your house is very large.”
The wild-eyed man swung round awhile ; a big
blush stole down to his white legs.
“I have built, and built: now where do 1
live ? ”
“ In my house ! ” _
Diana’s arm swept seaward ; a knife vanished
in the blue like a gleaming wedge.
ALBA DORION.
The gall of a gas co. to even threaten to
cut off the supply when the <£l deposit, which the
company demands and holds from all its cus-

course, the co. wouldn’t dare to do it; but most

H. : “To be really powerful, the parson
requires to sink again into absolute poverty,
the state depicted by Macaulay. The clergy all
hang on to the skirts of the rich, because of the
fiction that they must keep up a position in society,
and not allow their families to mix with the vulgar
herd.
Otago Witness (M.L.) publishes a screed
from a man who climbed Mount Tarapounamu
and looked down on the country round :
Far away to the east rises the great mass of Maungupo-
hatu, with its cliffs of limestone and its nigged contour
a mountain beloved of the sons of Tuhoe, the home of the
moa, the resting-place of the ancient dead, the abode of
the Tamakaimoana, descendants of the lost people, and
erstwhile the most savage of mountaineers. For is it not
told how Taneatua and Tawhaki and other old-time
ancestors came from the coast lands, from the Land of
Toi of old, bringing with them the mana of the new race,
the vigor of the younger branch, the strength to dare and
to strike ; and of how the ancient Ngapotiki melted away
like the sea-foam before the stalwart Polynesian vikings?
Hence it is that we do hear half-concealed allusions to
Nga Whatu-a-Maru and the sacred talismans concealed in
the great forest of Tuhoe Land ; and to Te Iho-o-Kataka,
the strange phallic emblem which stands at Ohaua-te-
rangi, near unto Ruatahuna, and to which sacred hinau
do resort those women of Tuhoe who have not been
blessed with offspring. To the south we see Maungatani-
wha and the sharp peak of Te Turi-o-Hana, and eastward
from those there looms the colossal Huiarau, the Sierra
Nevada of the term incognita of Tuhoe Land -Huiarau,
the great bulwark of the Uriwera, which protected them
from the tribes of the Rising Sun, the children of
Kalungunu. Westward rises Tuwatawata and the
great vertebrae of Pohokura, the last home of
the aborigines ; the valley of the Upper Whirinaki,
where the ancient tribes abode, and Wharepakua of im-
mortal fame slew the multitude of Te Marangaranga ; Te
Whaiti-nui-a-Toi, the home of the demon kawau, Hine-
ruarangi; and Te Ana-tuoro, which was formed in ancient
times by the tuoro. The valley of the Okahu, where
Tarewa-a-Rua and Te Manako and a host of other old-
time heroes lived and fought and died ; where the sons of
Pukeko held the Fort Oromaitaake, near unto Ngaputahi,
how came the vengeful Ngati Mahangi on the war trail,
and the descendants of Pukeko retreated to Niho-whati,
fell back on Umu-rakau, and fled to Awa-tarariki.
The Bulletin drops a tear over these brave old
days when Wharepakau slew Te Marangaranga in
Hine-ruarangi, and the Ngati Mahangi drove the
Pukekos from Oromaitaake to Awa-tarariki. They
were good times, but just a little trying to the
lower jaw.
Court Day at Billy billy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The court-house interior was almost bare of furni-
ture, the walls were unlined, and the weather-
boards gaped here and there, so that the grass-
hoppers jumped in and out at their pleasure. Their
worships (two of them) sat on a small form behind
a pine table, the prisoner hung over a deal railing
about eighteen inches from the wall, and Constable
O’Toole stood near, reciting the villainies of the
accused in a thick, monotonous, unintelligible
brogue. , ,
It was a day in Gehenna: the shrill, ringing
whirr of the locusts filled the air for miles around,
a bird chipped its beak sharply on the iron roof,
and a tall, attenuated goat stood in the doorway,
supinely observing the proceedings of the court,
and emitting an occasional contemptuous baah.
But the court seemed oblivious to everything
but the dreadful heat and its own sorrows. Half
of the court, open-mouthed, followed the meander-
ings of a lame bull-ant on the table, between in-
tervals of sleep, and occasionally stirred the
insect up with a straw to add to the excitement.
The other half, with his head thrown back, and
his occiput resting on the chair-rail, gazed medita-
tively at the roof, day-dreaming of strong drinks,
and occasionally relapsing into a gurgling snore
suggestive of a frog croaking in a ship’s hold, and
then pulling himself together with an effort and
trying to look wise.
And all the time constable O’Toole droned
along about “this yere mahn ” who had been dis-
covered the night previous, howling on the road
“ forninst widdy Johnson’s beyant,” with no-
thing on, “barrin’ th’ dust, yer worships, which
he’d buried hisself in.”
Prisoner was charged with drunkenness and
disorderly conduct, and was a woebegone object—
the Sahara incarnate. His tongue could be heard
grating against his palate, and he looked piteously
thirsty. When the policeman had done, he put in
a word or two in extenuation of his weakness, re-
minding their worships of the weather they had
been having, and concluding with a touching and
wholly ineffectual effort to expectorate.
Then the court felt itself called upon, and one-
half arose, and, steadying itself by leaning over the
table, assumed a look of inhuman gravity, and
said :
“ Prisoner, sush conducksh wholly indefenshible
—disgrash tyer manhood. Your are fined sheven
daysh or tweny-four hoursh. ”
Then that half sat down, with the air of a man
who has done his duty by his country, and the
other half arose, and, after blinking for a few
moments at the prisoner, with an assumption of
owl-like wisdom, added
“ Sheven daysh ’r tweny-foursh hoursh— both of
l/ou.'”
“ Yesh,” corroborated the first half, rising again,
“ both of you ! ”
Then the court adjourned.
